How to Use This Book

Linux Bytes is designed as both a structured learning path and a practical reference for engineers working with Linux in production environments.

Structure

  • Part I — Foundations: Start here if you are new to Linux or need a refresher on the essentials.
  • Part II — Systems & Operations: Day-to-day administration and production operations.
  • Part III — Advanced Topics: Deep dives for senior engineers working on performance, kernel internals, and observability.
  • Part IV — Containers & SRE: Container internals, Kubernetes, troubleshooting, security, and interview preparation.

Chapter Format

Each chapter follows a consistent structure:

  1. Learning Objectives — What you will gain from this chapter
  2. Why This Matters — Real-world relevance

  6. Common Mistakes — Pitfalls to avoid
  7. Practice Labs — Hands-on exercises
  8. Quick Reference — Cheat sheet for daily use
  9. Troubleshooting — Diagnostic guidance
  10. Quick Quiz — Test your understanding
  11. FAANG Insight — Interview and career tips

Difficulty levels: Chapters are marked Beginner, Intermediate, or Advanced. Follow the recommended prerequisites for best results.

Foundations

The essential Linux foundations every engineer must know: the filesystem hierarchy, permissions, CLI basics, shell scripting, text processing, and version control with Git.

Chapter 1

History of Linux & Unix

beginnerFoundations · 5 min read · 10 min practice

Linux is the dominant operating system powering 96% of the top 1M web servers, all Android devices, 100% of the TOP500 supercomputers, and the majority of cloud infrastructure. Understanding its origins is essential context for every Interview.

faang-insightLinuxhistoryinterview

Learning Objectives

  • Understand the fundamentals of history of linux & unix

Real-Life Analogy: Linux as a City

The kernel is the city government - it manages resources, enforces laws (permissions), and provides services. Distributions are different neighborhoods built on the same infrastructure. Open source means anyone can propose improvements to the city plan.

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes
Mistake: Checking /proc/meminfo inside containers for memory
Solution: Use cgroup v2 files: /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current, memory.events

Quick Reference

| Command | Description | |---|---|

Troubleshooting

Symptom: command returns unexpected output
Cause: Incorrect arguments, wrong working directory, or stale cached data
Solution: Verify arguments with `man command`; check current path with `pwd`; clear cache if applicable
Symptom: Configuration changes not taking effect
Cause: Service not reloaded or syntax error in config file
Solution: Validate config syntax with built-in test flag; reload service with `systemctl reload` or `restart`
Symptom: history operation slow or timing out
Cause: Resource exhaustion (disk I/O, memory pressure, or lock contention)
Solution: Check `iostat`, `free -m`, and `top`; look for stuck processes with `ps aux --sort=-%cpu`
Symptom: Permission issues accessing history resources
Cause: Incorrect file ownership or overly restrictive permissions
Solution: Inspect with `ls -la`; fix with `chown`/ `chmod`; check ACLs with `getfacl`

Quick Quiz

Q1: What is the primary purpose of linux-history?

A: To history on modern Linux systems

Q2: How do you list the contents of a history related directory?

A: Lists files and directories in the current or specified path

Q3: Which command would you use to view disk usage of the history directory?

A: du -sh /path/to/directory

Q4: A user reports "Permission denied" when trying to access a history directory. What is the first command you should run?

A: ls -ld /path/to/directory

Q5: In a history scenario, your monitoring alerts show ls failing intermittently. What is the correct debugging workflow?

A: Check logs → verify configuration → test in staging → apply fix → monitor

FAANG Insight

  • FAANG Architect's Note: When I interview candidates, I ask: 'What makes Linux different from other kernels?' The answer reveals depth
  • FAANG system design, this lesson applies: community-driven standards (Kubernetes, Docker, Prometheus) often beat proprietary solutions

Production Tips

  • Monitor key metrics continuously; alert on anomalies before users notice.
  • Automate routine tasks; manual processes do not scale.
  • Document runbooks for common incidents; practice them regularly.

Best Practices

  • Use version control for all configuration (/etc, scripts, Dockerfiles)
  • Implement infrastructure as code; avoid manual server changes
  • Test changes in staging before production; have rollback plans

Enterprise Perspective

In enterprise environments, history of linux & unix is managed through automation (Ansible, Terraform), monitored centrally (Prometheus, Datadog), and governed by compliance policies (CIS benchmarks, SOC2). Changes go through change advisory boards with rollback procedures documented.

Chapter 2

Filesystem Hierarchy Standard

beginnerFoundations · 15 min read · 11 min practice

The Filesystem Hierarchy Standard (FHS) defines the directory structure on Linux systems. Knowing where things live — and why — is table-stakes for every Production infrastructure interview. When an interviewer says 'your service is logging to /var/log and filling up the disk,' you need to know where everything is.

faang-insightLinuxfilesysteminterview

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux history

Learning Objectives

  • Understand the Root Filesystem (/)
  • Understand /etc — The Configuration Nerve Center
  • Understand /proc & /sys — Virtual Filesystems for Kernel Introspection
  • Understand /var — Variable Data (Logs, Databases, Spool)

Real-Life Analogy: Filesystem as a Library

The root directory (/) is the main entrance. /bin and /sbin are the reference section (essential tools). /etc is the catalog system (configuration). /var is the archives where new records (logs) arrive daily. /home is personal study carrels. /proc and /sys are live feeds showing what the library is doing right now.

The Root Filesystem (/)

Everything on Linux starts at /. The root filesystem is the top of the single unified directory tree — there are no drive letters (C:, D:). Every storage device is mounted at a directory under /. This unified namespace is one of Unix's most important innovations.
bash
# Essential root directories at a glance
/bin      # Essential user command binaries (available in single-user mode)
/sbin     # System administration binaries (e.g., fdisk, mkfs, iptables)
/etc      # Host-specific system configuration (the nerve center)
/var      # Variable data — logs, databases, spool files
/tmp      # Temporary files (cleared on boot, world-writable)
/usr      # Secondary hierarchy — user utilities and applications
/opt      # Add-on application software packages
/proc     # Virtual filesystem for process and kernel information
/sys      # Virtual filesystem for kernel objects and device info
/dev      # Device files (every hardware device is a file)
/run      # Runtime variable data (PID files, sockets — tmpfs since boot)
/home     # User home directories
/root     # Root user's home directory

# A Quick map:
# /etc  → Configuration
# /var  → Data (logs, DBs)
# /proc → Kernel/process introspection
# /dev  → Hardware as files
# /tmp  → Scratch space (watch for security!)

/etc — The Configuration Nerve Center

/etc holds all host-specific configuration. If something isn't working, you start here. In Production environments, /etc is typically managed by configuration management (Ansible, Puppet, Chef) and changes are reviewed through code review.
bash
ls /etc
# Key files every engineer should know:
/etc/passwd       # User account info (login shells, UIDs)
/etc/shadow       # Encrypted password hashes (root-readable only)
/etc/group        # Group definitions
/etc/sudoers      # Sudo privileges (edit with visudo!)
/etc/hosts        # Static hostname-to-IP mapping
/etc/resolv.conf  # DNS resolver configuration
/etc/hostname     # System hostname
/etc/fstab        # Filesystem mount table (auto-mount on boot)
/etc/crontab      # System-wide cron schedule
/etc/ssh/sshd_config   # SSH server configuration
/etc/nginx/nginx.conf  # Nginx configuration (if installed)
/etc/systemd/    # Systemd unit files and system configuration

# Tip: /etc is typically on the root filesystem
# Don't let logs fill up / — it can make the system unbootable

/proc & /sys — Virtual Filesystems for Kernel Introspection

/proc and /sys are virtual filesystems — they don't exist on disk. Reading files in /proc triggers kernel code that returns real-time data. Writing to some files changes kernel parameters. These are your window into the running kernel. Every Production SRE knows /proc intimately.
bash
# === /proc — Process and System Information ===
cat /proc/cpuinfo      # CPU details (model, cores, flags, cache)
cat /proc/meminfo      # Memory: total, free, buffers, cache, swap
cat /proc/uptime       # System uptime in seconds
cat /proc/version      # Linux kernel version string
cat /proc/loadavg      # System load averages (1, 5, 15 min)
cat /proc/1/cmdline    # Command line of PID 1 (init/systemd)
cat /proc/self/status  # Current process's status (Name, Pid, VmRSS, ...)
ls /proc/<PID>/fd/     # Open file descriptors for a process
cat /proc/<PID>/maps   # Memory-mapped regions of a process

# === /proc/sys — Kernel Tunables (sysctl interface) ===
cat /proc/sys/net/ipv4/ip_forward        # Is IP forwarding enabled?
cat /proc/sys/net/ipv4/tcp_tw_reuse      # TCP TIME_WAIT reuse
cat /proc/sys/vm/swappiness              # Swap tendency (0-100)
cat /proc/sys/vm/dirty_ratio             # Max dirty pages %% before writeback

# === /sys — Device and Driver Model ===
ls /sys/class/block/    # All block devices (sda, nvme0n1, etc.)
ls /sys/class/net/      # All network interfaces (eth0, lo, wlan0)
ls /sys/devices/system/cpu/  # Per-CPU information

# Enterprise insight: When debugging production issues,
# /proc/<PID>/ is your first stop for process-level diagnostics

/var — Variable Data (Logs, Databases, Spool)

/var holds data that changes in size and content during normal operation. This is where logs go, and disk space monitoring of /var is a standard part of any SRE runbook. Common Interview scenario: 'Disk is full, find and clean up /var/log without restarting services.'
bash
ls /var
/var/log          # System and application logs (journalctl, syslog, nginx)
/var/lib          # Persistent state data (databases: mysql, postgres, docker)
/var/cache        # Application cache data (apt, yum, pip)
/var/spool        # Print and mail spools
/var/tmp          # Temporary files preserved across reboots
/var/opt          # Variable data for /opt packages

# === Production scenario: Log Rotation ===
# Logrotate manages log rotation. Config at /etc/logrotate.conf
# Watch for: services that log too verbosely, filling /var

# Check disk usage by directory:
du -sh /var/log/* 2>/dev/null | sort -rh | head -10

# Safely truncate a log without restarting:
# (The file descriptor stays valid even after truncation)
> /var/log/myapp/access.log          # Truncate in place
# OR:
truncate -s 0 /var/log/myapp/access.log

Interview Q&A

Q: 'Your production server is running out of disk space on /. Walk through your debugging process.'

A: 'First, df -h to see which filesystem is full. Then du -sh /* | sort -rh to find the largest top-level directory. Common culprits: /var/log (unrotated logs), /tmp (temp files from batch jobs), Docker overlay storage in /var/lib/docker. For /var/log, check logrotate config. For Docker, prune unused images and containers. In a Enterprise environment, we'd have monitoring alerting on disk usage at 80%, 90%, 95% — and automated cleanup via cron or systemd timers.'

Q: 'What's the difference between /proc and /sys?'

A: '/proc was originally process information — the original design was one directory per process (/proc/PID). It evolved to include system info (/proc/cpuinfo, /proc/meminfo, /proc/sys). /sys (sysfs) is newer — it exports the kernel's device and driver model in a structured way. /sys/class organizes by type (block, net, input), /sys/devices by topology. Both are virtual filesystems backed by kernel callbacks. Use /proc for process data and kernel tunables; use /sys for hardware and driver information.'

Q: 'Why does Linux use forward slashes (/) instead of backslashes (\\)?'

A: 'Historical compatibility with the MULTICS/Unix design choice. Forward slashes were chosen because they didn't conflict with the shell's escape character (backslash). Every major OS except Windows uses forward slashes.'

References & Further Reading

  • Filesystem Hierarchy Standard 3.0 — The official FHS specification
  • man hier — Description of the filesystem hierarchy on your system
  • Linux Filesystem Hierarchy — TLDP comprehensive guide

Chapter 3

File Permissions & ACLs

advancedFoundations · 17 min read · 13 min practice

Linux file permissions are the foundation of system security. Every Production SRE and infrastructure engineer must understand the permission model cold — including SUID/SGID, sticky bits, ACLs, and how they interact. Permission misconfiguration is a leading cause of security vulnerabilities in production.

faang-insightLinuxpermissionssecurityinterview

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux filesystem, linux cli basics

Learning Objectives

  • Understand the Permission Triplet: Owner-Group-Other
  • Understand directories: Execute Bit = Traversal
  • Understand special Permissions: SUID, SGID, Sticky Bit
  • Understand access Control Lists (ACLs)
  • Understand umask & Default Permissions

Real-Life Analogy: Permissions as House Keys

Each file is a room. The owner has the master key (read/write/execute). Group members have a shared key (read/execute). Others might only peek through the window (read). The sticky bit on /tmp is like a coat check - you can hang your coat, but only you can take it back.

The Permission Triplet: Owner-Group-Other

Every file and directory has three permission sets: owner (u), group (g), and others (o). Each set has three bits: read (r=4), write (w=2), execute (x=1). This 9-bit mode (plus 3 special bits) is the standard Unix permission model.
bash
# Permission modes: rwxr-xr--
#                  uuu ggg ooo
#                  user group others

ls -l /etc/passwd
# -rw-r--r-- 1 root root 2.8K Mar 15 12:34 /etc/passwd
# ^  |  |  |     ^    ^     ^
# |  u  g  o     |    |     size
# type           owner group

# Numeric (octal) representation:
# rwx = 4+2+1 = 7
# rw- = 4+2+0 = 6
# r-x = 4+0+1 = 5
# r-- = 4+0+0 = 4
# --- = 0+0+0 = 0

chmod 755 script.sh    # rwxr-xr-x — owner: all, group/other: read+execute
chmod 644 config.conf  # rw-r--r-- — owner: read+write, group/other: read
chmod 600 id_rsa       # rw------- — SSH private key: owner only!
chmod 700 ~/.ssh       # rwx------ — directory: owner only (lock down SSH)

chmod u+x script.sh    # Add execute for user
chmod g-w file.txt     # Remove write for group
chmod o=r file.txt     # Set others to read-only
chmod a+rx /app/bin    # Add read+execute for all (user,group,other)

Directories: Execute Bit = Traversal

On directories, the permission bits have different meanings: r = list files, w = create/delete files, x = traverse/search (enter) the directory. Without the execute bit, you can't access any files in the directory even if you know their names — a common source of confusion.
bash
# Directory permissions explained:
# r (read):    can list directory contents (ls)
# w (write):   can create, rename, delete files in the directory
# x (execute): can traverse the directory (cd into it, access files by name)

# Common directory permissions:
chmod 755 /home/user      # rwxr-xr-x — standard: owner writes, others traverse
chmod 700 /home/user/.ssh # rwx------ — SSH directory: only owner!
chmod 1777 /tmp           # rwxrwxrwt — world-writable with sticky bit

# Gotcha: read WITHOUT execute on a directory
chmod 644 /restricted-dir # rw-r--r--
ls /restricted-dir        # Can LIST files (has r)
cat /restricted-dir/file  # Permission denied! Can't traverse (no x)

# Minimum permissions for web server to serve files:
# Directory: r-x (5) — traverse and list
# File:      r-- (4) — read only
# No need for w (2) on files or directories

Special Permissions: SUID, SGID, Sticky Bit

Three special permission bits add additional behavior. SUID (4000) — run a file with the owner's privileges (e.g., passwd runs as root). SGID (2000) — on files, run with group's privileges; on directories, new files inherit the directory's group. Sticky Bit (1000) — only file owners can delete their files (used on /tmp). These are frequent interview topics.
bash
# SUID (Set User ID) — runs as file owner, not the user executing it
chmod u+s /usr/bin/passwd  # Set SUID (s in user execute position)
ls -l /usr/bin/passwd
# -rwsr-xr-x ... passwd — the 's' means SUID + executable

# Check all SUID binaries on your system:
find / -perm -4000 -type f 2>/dev/null

# SGID (Set Group ID) — on directories: new files inherit group
chmod g+s /shared/project   # Set SGID (s in group execute position)
# Now any file created in /shared/project belongs to the directory's group

# Sticky Bit — only owners can delete their own files
chmod +t /tmp               # Set sticky bit (t in others execute position)
ls -ld /tmp
# drwxrwxrwt ... /tmp — the 't' means sticky bit is set

# === Interview: SUID Security ===
# Why is SUID dangerous? If a SUID root binary has a vulnerability,
# any user can escalate to root.
# Mitigation: minimize SUID binaries (use sudo instead),
# mount filesystems with 'nosuid' option where possible.

# Numeric shortcuts:
chmod 4755 script  # SUID + rwxr-xr-x
chmod 2755 dir     # SGID + rwxr-xr-x
chmod 1777 /tmp    # Sticky bit + rwxrwxrwx

Access Control Lists (ACLs)

POSIX ACLs extend the traditional permission model to support multiple users and groups. When the basic u/g/o model is insufficient, ACLs provide fine-grained control. Production use case: shared team directories where multiple service accounts and humans need different access levels.
bash
# Check if ACLs are enabled on a filesystem
mount | grep acl
# (Most modern filesystems have ACLs enabled by default)

# View ACLs on a file/directory
getfacl /shared/project
# file: shared/project
# owner: alice
# group: dev-team
# user::rwx
# user:bob:r-x       # Bob has read+execute only
# group::r-x
# mask::r-x
# other::---

# Set ACLs
setfacl -m u:bob:rx /shared/project    # Add Bob with read+execute
setfacl -m g:devops:rwx /shared/tools  # Add devops group with full access
setfacl -m o::--- /shared/project      # Remove all access for others

# Recursive ACL (be careful — performance impact)
setfacl -R -m u:ci-user:rx /app/deploy

# Remove specific ACL entry
setfacl -x u:bob /shared/project

# Default ACL — new files/dirs inherit these ACLs
setfacl -d -m g:dev-team:rwx /shared/project

# === Enterprise Insight ===
# ACLs add complexity. Before extending ACLs, ask:
# Can I solve this with Unix groups in /etc/group?
# ACLs are harder to audit and debug than traditional permissions.
# In Enterprise environments, we minimize ACL usage and rely on:
# - Clear group membership
# - Service accounts with minimal permissions
# - Config management (Ansible/Terraform) to enforce permissions

umask & Default Permissions

The umask determines default permissions for newly created files and directories. It's a mask (inverted) — the permissions are base - umask. Files default to 666 (rw-rw-rw-), directories to 777 (rwxrwxrwx). The umask subtracts from these.
bash
# Current umask value
umask                     # 0022 (most common)
umask -S                 # Symbolic: u=rwx,g=rx,o=rx

# How umask works:
# File defaults:   666 (rw-rw-rw-)
# Minus umask:     022 (----w--w-)
# Result:          644 (rw-r--r--)

# Directory defaults: 777 (rwxrwxrwx)
# Minus umask:        022 (----w--w-)
# Result:             755 (rwxr-xr-x)

# Common umask values:
# 0022 — rw-r--r-- files, rwxr-xr-x dirs (standard)
# 0027 — rw-r-----, rwxr-x--- (shared team with restricted group)
# 0077 — rw-------, rwx------ (private — SSH keys!)

umask 0077               # Set for this shell session
# Add to ~/.bashrc for persistence

# === Interview ===
# Q: 'A user reports that files they create in /shared/project
#     are not accessible by their teammates. What's wrong?'
# A: Check umask. If it's 0077, files are created with no group access.
#    Fix: set umask 0027 in the team's .bashrc or the shared script.

Interview Q&A

Q: 'What does chmod 4755 do?'

A: '4 indicates the SUID bit is set. 755 means rwxr-xr-x. So the file runs with the owner's privileges regardless of who executes it. Common for utilities like passwd and ping that need elevated access.'

Q: 'How do you audit permissions across a fleet of 1000 Linux servers?'

A: 'At Enterprise scale, we never SSH into individual servers to check permissions. Instead, configuration management (Ansible, Chef, Puppet) enforces desired permissions via declarative config. We also use auditing tools like auditd with watches on sensitive paths, and drift detection that compares current permissions against a golden baseline. For security-critical checks (SUID, world-writable files), we run automated scans and alert on changes.'

Q: 'Scenario: You need to give a CI/CD service account write access to /app/deploy but read-only access to /app/config. The owner is deploy-user, group is dev-team. How?'

A: 'Traditional permissions: if ci-user needs different access than deploy-user, the basic u/g/o model won't work. I'd use ACLs: setfacl -m u:ci-user:rwx /app/deploy and setfacl -m u:ci-user:rx /app/config. Or create a dedicated group (ci-agents), add ci-user to it, and set group permissions accordingly. The group approach is simpler to audit.'

References & Further Reading

  • man chmod — Change file modes bits
  • man getfacl / setfacl — POSIX ACL management
  • visudo — Edit sudoers safely

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes
Mistake: Checking /proc/meminfo inside containers for memory
Solution: Use cgroup v2 files: /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current, memory.events

Practice Labs

Run `ls` and examine the output. Note the structure, key fields, and patterns you observe. Try different flags to see how the output changes.

Explore: Directories: Execute Bit = Traversal

Run `chmod` and examine the output. Note the structure, key fields, and patterns you observe. Try different flags to see how the output changes.

Explore: Special Permissions: SUID, SGID, Sticky Bit

Run `cat` and examine the output. Note the structure, key fields, and patterns you observe. Try different flags to see how the output changes.

Quick Reference

| Command | Description | |---|---| | `ls` | The Permission Triplet: Owner-Group-Other | | `chmod` | The Permission Triplet: Owner-Group-Other | | `chmod` | Directories: Execute Bit = Traversal | | `ls` | Directories: Execute Bit = Traversal | | `cat` | Directories: Execute Bit = Traversal | | `chmod` | Special Permissions: SUID, SGID, Sticky Bit | | `ls` | Special Permissions: SUID, SGID, Sticky Bit | | `find` | Special Permissions: SUID, SGID, Sticky Bit | | `mount` | Access Control Lists (ACLs) | | `getfacl` | Access Control Lists (ACLs) | | `setfacl` | Access Control Lists (ACLs) | | `umask` | umask & Default Permissions |

Troubleshooting

Symptom: ls returns unexpected output
Cause: Incorrect arguments, wrong working directory, or stale cached data
Solution: Verify arguments with `man ls`; check current path with `pwd`; clear cache if applicable
Symptom: Configuration changes not taking effect
Cause: Service not reloaded or syntax error in config file
Solution: Validate config syntax with built-in test flag; reload service with `systemctl reload` or `restart`
Symptom: permissions operation slow or timing out
Cause: Resource exhaustion (disk I/O, memory pressure, or lock contention)
Solution: Check `iostat`, `free -m`, and `top`; look for stuck processes with `ps aux --sort=-%cpu`
Symptom: Permission issues accessing permissions resources
Cause: Incorrect file ownership or overly restrictive permissions
Solution: Inspect with `ls -la`; fix with `chown`/ `chmod`; check ACLs with `getfacl`

Quick Quiz

Q1: What is The Permission Triplet: Owner-Group-Other?

A: A standardized directory structure that defines where files and directories should be placed on Linux systems

Q2: What does `ls` do?

A: Lists files and directories in the current or specified path

Q3: Which command would you use to view disk usage of the permissions directory?

A: du -sh /path/to/directory

Q4: A user reports "Permission denied" when trying to access a permissions directory. What is the first command you should run?

A: ls -ld /path/to/directory

Q5: In a permissions scenario, your monitoring alerts show ls failing intermittently. What is the correct debugging workflow?

A: Check logs → verify configuration → test in staging → apply fix → monitor

FAANG Insight

  • FAANG Architect's Note: Permission management at Internet scale is fundamentally different from single-server Linux

Production Tips

  • production

Best Practices

  • Use version control for all configuration (/etc, scripts, Dockerfiles)
  • Implement infrastructure as code; avoid manual server changes
  • Test changes in staging before production; have rollback plans

Enterprise Perspective

In enterprise environments, file permissions & acls is managed through automation (Ansible, Terraform), monitored centrally (Prometheus, Datadog), and governed by compliance policies (CIS benchmarks, SOC2). Changes go through change advisory boards with rollback procedures documented.

Chapter 4

CLI Fundamentals

beginnerFoundations · 17 min read · 13 min practice

The Linux command line is the primary interface for every engineer at Enterprise. GUI tools are rarely used in production. This guide covers the essential commands, the pipe model, I/O redirection, environment variables, and the shell startup sequence — all from a Architect's perspective.

faang-insightLinuxCLIbasicsinterview

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux history

Learning Objectives

  • Understand the Unix Philosophy: Small Composable Tools
  • Understand essential Commands Every Production engineers Knows Cold
  • Understand i/O Redirection & Here Documents
  • Understand environment Variables & Shell Configuration
  • Understand job Control & Process Management

Real-Life Analogy: CLI as a Conversation

The shell is a bilingual interpreter. You speak in commands (verbs) with arguments (nouns). Pipes (|) pass the output of one conversation directly into the next - like whispering a message down a line. Redirection (> and <) saves or loads the conversation from a transcript file.

The Unix Philosophy: Small Composable Tools

Every Linux command does one thing well. Commands are connected via pipes (|) to build powerful pipelines. This composability is the key insight that makes the CLI so effective. In Interviews, candidates who think in pipelines demonstrate system-level thinking.
bash
# The pipe model: command1 | command2 | command3
# stdout of first → stdin of second → stdin of third

# Classic examples:
# Find top 5 largest files in /var/log:
du -sh /var/log/* 2>/dev/null | sort -rh | head -5

# Count unique IPs in an access log:
awk '{print $1}' /var/log/nginx/access.log | sort -u | wc -l

# Find processes using most memory:
ps aux --sort=-%mem | head -10

# The building blocks:
# stdin  (0) — input to command
# stdout (1) — normal output
# stderr (2) — error output

ls /nonexistent > output.txt 2>&1    # Redirect both stdout and stderr
ls /nonexistent &>> output.txt       # Bash 4+: append both

# /dev/null — the bit bucket
grep -r 'secret' . 2>/dev/null       # Suppress errors

Essential Commands Every Production engineers Knows Cold

These commands should be second nature. Your interviewer won't wait while you figure out basic syntax. Knowing these without thinking signals senior-level muscle memory.
bash
# === NAVIGATION ===
pwd              # Print working directory
cd -             # Go to previous directory
ls -la           # List all files with details
ls -lhSr         # List sorted by size (smallest first)
tree -L 2        # Show directory tree 2 levels deep

# === FILE OPERATIONS ===
cp -r src/ dst/               # Recursive copy
mv file.txt /new/location/    # Move (also rename)
rm -rf dir/                   # Remove directory and contents (DANGER!)
mkdir -p a/b/c/d              # Create nested directories
touch file.txt                # Create empty file or update timestamp
ln -s /actual/path link_name  # Create symbolic link

# === VIEWING FILES ===
cat file.txt    # Full file to stdout (small files only!)
less file.txt   # Scrollable viewer (q to quit, / to search)
head -20        # First 20 lines
tail -f log     # Follow log in real time (journalctl -f for modern)
tail -n100      # Last 100 lines

# === SEARCHING ===
grep -r 'error' /var/log/    # Recursive search
find / -name '*.log' -type f # Find files by name
locate nginx.conf            # Search indexed database (updatedb)

# === PROCESS ===
ps aux             # All processes
ps auxf            # Forest view (show parent-child)
top                # Interactive process viewer
htop               # Better top (install if needed)
kill -9 <PID>      # Force kill (SIGKILL) — use as last resort!
kill -15 <PID>     # Graceful termination (SIGTERM) — preferred

# === SYSTEM ===
df -h              # Disk free (human-readable)
du -sh /dir/       # Disk usage (summary)
free -h            # Memory usage
uptime             # How long since boot + load averages
uname -a           # Kernel version and system info
whoami             # Current user

I/O Redirection & Here Documents

Redirection is how you control where command output goes. Beyond basic > and >>, understanding file descriptors and here-docs is essential for scripting and debugging.
bash
# File descriptors
# 0 = stdin, 1 = stdout, 2 = stderr

command > file     # Redirect stdout to file (overwrite)
command >> file    # Redirect stdout to file (append)
command 2> file    # Redirect stderr to file
command &> file    # Redirect both stdout and stderr (Bash 4+)
command > file 2>&1   # Classic: stdout to file, then stderr to same place

# Suppress all output
silent_cmd > /dev/null 2>&1

# Here Document — feed multi-line input into a command
cat << 'EOF' > config.txt
server {
    listen 80;
    server_name example.com;
}
EOF

# Here String — feed a string into stdin
grep 'error' <<< "this line has an error here"

# Process substitution — treat command output as a file
diff <(ls dir1/) <(ls dir2/)

# tee — output to file AND stdout
echo "deploying..." | tee deploy.log

Environment Variables & Shell Configuration

Environment variables control almost everything on Linux: the PATH for finding commands, the editor for git, locale settings, and proxy configuration. Understanding the shell startup sequence (~/.bashrc, ~/.bash_profile, /etc/profile) is a common interview topic.
bash
# Essential environment variables
echo $PATH          # Colon-separated list of command directories
echo $HOME          # Current user's home directory
echo $USER          # Current username
echo $SHELL         # Current shell (/bin/bash, /bin/zsh)
echo $LANG          # Locale settings
echo $EDITOR        # Default editor (vim, nano, code)

# Shell startup order (Bash):
# 1. /etc/profile        — System-wide (login shell)
# 2. ~/.bash_profile     — User-specific (login shell, overrides #1)
# 3. ~/.bashrc           — User-specific (interactive non-login)
# 4. /etc/bashrc         — System-wide (interactive)

# Zsh startup order:
# .zshenv → .zprofile → .zshrc → .zlogin

# Common config to add to ~/.bashrc:
export PATH="$HOME/bin:$PATH"           # Add personal bin to PATH
export EDITOR=vim                        # Set default editor
unalias grep 2>/dev/null; alias grep='grep --color=auto'
export HISTSIZE=10000                    # Bigger history
export HISTCONTROL=ignoredups            # No duplicate history lines

# Source a file after editing:
source ~/.bashrc   # or just: . ~/.bashrc

# Set environment variable for a single command:
EDITOR=nano git commit   # Uses nano just for this commit

Job Control & Process Management

The shell provides built-in job control: running commands in the background, suspending them, and bringing them to the foreground. Essential for managing multiple tasks in a terminal session.
bash
# Background a command
long_running_task &
sleep 10 &

# List background jobs
jobs
# [1]+  Running                 sleep 10 &

# Bring job to foreground
fg %1              # Bring job 1 to foreground

# Suspend current foreground job
# Press: Ctrl+Z    # Suspends the running command
# Now it appears in jobs list as 'Stopped'

# Run suspended job in background
bg %1

# No-hup — keep running after logout
nohup long_task &

# disown — remove job from shell's job table
long_task &
disown              # Now closing terminal won't kill it

# screen/tmux — terminal multiplexers (essential for remote work)
screen -S mysession    # Create named session
screen -ls             # List sessions
screen -r mysession    # Reattach to session

# Ctrl+A, D — detach from screen (stays running)

Interview Q&A

Q: 'You SSH into a production box. There's no htop, no standard monitoring tools. List the commands you'd run in the first 30 seconds to understand what's wrong.'

A: 'uptime for load averages, free -h for memory, df -h for disk, top -bn1 | head -5 for top CPU consumers, dmesg | tail -20 for kernel messages (OOM, disk errors), journalctl -xe -n 50 --no-pager for recent system logs.'

Q: 'What's the difference between .bashrc and .bash_profile?'

A: 'When you SSH into a machine, you get a login shell — it reads .bash_profile. When you open a new terminal window in a GUI, it's typically an interactive non-login shell — it reads .bashrc. Best practice: put everything in .bashrc, and source .bashrc from .bash_profile.'

Q: 'You accidentally ran rm -rf on important files. What do you do?'

A: 'Immediately stop all writes to the filesystem. Unmount the partition if possible. Use extundelete or testdisk to recover. If this is production, fail over to a replica. In Enterprise, we have automated backups — no critical data lives on a single server without replication. Also, train your team to alias rm to 'rm -i' or use trash-cli.'

References & Further Reading

  • Bash Reference Manual — Official GNU Bash documentation
  • The Linux Command Line — William Shotts, free online book

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Running destructive commands without verification
Solution: Always use `rm -i` or `ls` first to confirm what will be deleted
Mistake: Using kill -9 as first resort
Solution: Send SIGTERM first (kill PID); wait for graceful shutdown; only use SIGKILL if process ignores SIGTERM
Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime

Chapter 5

Shell Scripting Basics

beginnerFoundations · 18 min read · 14 min practice

Shell scripting is how Linux systems are automated. From CI/CD pipelines to deployment scripts to cron jobs — if you work at Enterprise, you will read and write shell scripts. This guide covers the essentials with a focus on patterns that matter in production environments.

faang-insightLinuxshell-scriptingbashinterview

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux cli basics

Learning Objectives

  • Understand script Anatomy & Shebang
  • Understand variables, Parameters & Quoting
  • Understand conditionals & Control Flow
  • Understand loops & Arrays
  • Understand functions & Error Handling

Real-Life Analogy: Shell Scripts as Recipes

A shell script is a recipe card. Variables are ingredients. Loops are "repeat until done." Conditionals are "if the oven is hot, bake; else preheat." Functions are prep steps you reuse across recipes. The shebang (#!/bin/bash) tells the kitchen which chef to use.

Script Anatomy & Shebang

Every shell script starts with a shebang (#!/bin/bash or #!/bin/sh) and should set strict error handling. The shebang tells the kernel which interpreter to use. In Production scripts, you'll almost always see #!/bin/bash with set -euo pipefail.
bash
#!/bin/bash
# The standard Production header:
set -euo pipefail
# -e:  Exit on error (any command fails)
# -u:  Treat unset variables as error
# -o pipefail: Pipeline fails if any command fails (not just the last)
# -x:  Print each command before executing (for debugging)

# Making your script executable:
chmod +x script.sh
./script.sh          # Run it
bash script.sh       # Also works

# Enterprise note: Never use #!/bin/sh in production
# On Ubuntu/Debian, /bin/sh is dash (minimal POSIX)
# On RHEL/CentOS, /bin/sh is bash
# Use bash explicitly to avoid compatibility surprises

Variables, Parameters & Quoting

Variables, command-line arguments, and proper quoting are the foundation. Always quote your variables — unquoted variables undergo word splitting and glob expansion, causing bugs that are hard to debug. This is a Interview red flag when missed.
bash
#!/bin/bash
set -euo pipefail

# Variables — no spaces around =
NAME="World"
echo "Hello, $NAME"          # Variable expansion
echo "Hello, ${NAME}!"       # Braces for clarity/concatenation

# Command-line arguments
echo "Script: $0"            # Script name
echo "First arg: $1"         # $1-$9 are positional args
echo "All args: $@"          # All args as separate words
echo "Arg count: $#"         # Number of args
shift                        # Shift $2→$1, $3→$2, etc.

# Special variables
echo "Exit code: $?"          # Exit code of last command
echo "PID: $$"               # Current script PID
echo "Last BG PID: $!"       # PID of last background process

# QUOTING — this will bite you in production:
FILE="My Document.txt"       # Filename with space
cat $FILE                     # ERROR: tries to cat 'My' and 'Document.txt'
cat "$FILE"                  # CORRECT: passes the whole filename

# Using defaults
echo "${NAME:-default}"       # Use default if unset
echo "${NAME:=default}"       # Set default AND use it

# Command substitution (two styles)
CURRENT_DIR=$(pwd)            # Modern style (preferred)
CURRENT_DIR=`pwd`             # Old style (avoid)

# Arithmetic
COUNT=$((COUNT + 1))          # $(( ... )) for integer math

Conditionals & Control Flow

The if/test/[ ]/[[ ]] system is unlike any other language. The test command (or [) checks conditions. The double-bracket [[ ]] is a Bash extension with more features (pattern matching, regex).
bash
#!/bin/bash
set -euo pipefail

# File tests (most common in scripts)
if [ -f "$FILE" ]; then echo "$FILE exists"; fi
if [ -d "$DIR" ]; then echo "$DIR is a directory"; fi
if [ -x "$BINARY" ]; then echo "$BINARY is executable"; fi
if [ -z "$STRING" ]; then echo "String is empty"; fi
if [ -n "$STRING" ]; then echo "String is not empty"; fi

# String comparison
if [ "$NAME" = "root" ]; then echo "Is root"; fi     # POSIX: single =
if [ "$NAME" != "admin" ]; then echo "Not admin"; fi
if [[ "$NAME" == r* ]]; then echo "Starts with r"; fi  # Pattern match (Bash)
if [[ "$HOST" =~ ^web[0-9]+ ]]; then echo "Web server"; fi  # Regex match

# Numeric comparison
if [ "$COUNT" -gt 10 ]; then echo ">10"; fi          # -gt, -lt, -ge, -le, -eq
if (( COUNT > 10 )); then echo ">10"; fi             # Modern math syntax

# File descriptors in conditionals
if [ -t 0 ]; then echo "stdin is a terminal"; fi     # Interactive session?

# elif and else
if [ "$1" = "start" ]; then
    /usr/bin/myservice start
elif [ "$1" = "stop" ]; then
    /usr/bin/myservice stop
else
    echo "Usage: $0 {start|stop}"
    exit 1
fi

# case statement — cleaner than long if-elif chains
case "$1" in
    start|restart)   action="start" ;;
    stop)            action="stop" ;;
    *)               echo "Unknown: $1"; exit 1 ;;
esac

Loops & Arrays

Loops iterate over files, commands, or sequences. Arrays are underused in shell scripts — they make scripts cleaner and more maintainable.
bash
#!/bin/bash
set -euo pipefail

# for loop over explicit list
for fruit in apple banana cherry; do
    echo "$fruit"
done

# for loop over command output
for file in /etc/*.conf; do
    echo "Found: $file"
done

# for loop with seq (C-style)
for ((i=0; i<10; i++)); do
    echo "Number $i"
done

# while loop — read file line by line
while IFS= read -r line; do
    echo "Line: $line"
done < input.txt
# IFS= preserves leading whitespace
# -r prevents backslash interpretation

# Arrays
FRUITS=("apple" "banana" "cherry")
echo "${FRUITS[0]}"          # First element
echo "${FRUITS[@]}"          # All elements
echo "${#FRUITS[@]}"         # Array length
FRUITS+=("date")             # Append

# Iterate array
for fruit in "${FRUITS[@]}"; do
    echo "$fruit"
done

# Associative arrays (Bash 4+)
declare -A COLORS
COLORS[apple]="red"
COLORS[banana]="yellow"
echo "${COLORS[apple]}"       # red

Functions & Error Handling

Functions organize reusable logic. Error handling patterns with trap ensure cleanup on failure — this is non-negotiable in Production scripts.
bash
#!/bin/bash
set -euo pipefail

# Define a function
log() {
    local level="$1"        # local makes variable function-scoped
    local message="$2"
    echo "[$(date '+%Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S')] [$level] $message"
}

log "INFO" "Starting deployment"
log "ERROR" "Connection failed"

# Return values
is_root() {
    if [ "$EUID" -eq 0 ]; then
        return 0             # 0 = success (true)
    else
        return 1             # non-zero = failure (false)
    fi
}

if is_root; then
    echo "Running as root"
fi

# === The Production pattern: trap and cleanup ===
cleanup() {
    log "INFO" "Cleaning up..."
    rm -f /tmp/deploy.$$
    log "INFO" "Cleanup complete"
}
trap cleanup EXIT          # cleanup runs on any exit (success, error, signal)
trap 'exit 1' INT TERM     # Handle Ctrl+C gracefully

# Enterprise standard deployment wrapper pattern:
deploy() {
    local version="$1"
    log "INFO" "Deploying version $version"
    
    if ! ./preflight.sh; then
        log "ERROR" "Preflight failed"
        return 1
    fi
    
    ./deploy.sh "$version"
    log "INFO" "Deploy complete"
}

# Run it
deploy "v2.3.1" || exit 1

Interview Q&A

Q: 'Write a script that monitors a log file for ERROR patterns and sends an alert if more than 10 errors appear in 60 seconds.'

A: 'tail -f /var/log/app.log | grep --line-buffered ERROR | head -10 — but for a real solution, use a while loop with timestamp checking and a counter. In production, you'd use a proper monitoring system (Prometheus, Datadog) — this script is for bootstrap scenarios.'

Q: 'What does set -euo pipefail do, and why do you always use it in production scripts?'

A: '-e: exit on first error. -u: error on unset variables (catches typos). -o pipefail: propogate failures through pipes. Without it, 'false | true' would succeed. These three flags turn shell scripts from fragile collections of commands into reliable programs.'

Q: 'Write a script that backs up /var/lib/mysql to /backup, rotating the last 7 days.'

A: See the code block for a complete backup rotation script.
bash
#!/bin/bash
set -euo pipefail

BACKUP_DIR="/backup/mysql"
SOURCE="/var/lib/mysql"
RETENTION=7

mkdir -p "$BACKUP_DIR"

# Create timestamped backup
TIMESTAMP=$(date '+%Y%m%d_%H%M%S')
BACKUP_FILE="${BACKUP_DIR}/mysql_backup_${TIMESTAMP}.tar.gz"

tar -czf "$BACKUP_FILE" "$SOURCE"
echo "Created: $BACKUP_FILE"

# Remove backups older than RETENTION days
find "$BACKUP_DIR" -name 'mysql_backup_*.tar.gz' -mtime +$RETENTION -delete
echo "Cleaned up backups older than $RETENTION days"

# Verify backup integrity
tar -tzf "$BACKUP_FILE" > /dev/null && echo "Backup verified successfully"

# Report to monitoring
curl -s "https://monitor.internal/report?metric=mysql.backup&value=1" > /dev/null ||
true

References & Further Reading

  • Bash Reference Manual — Official documentation
  • Google Shell Style Guide — Enterprise standard for shell script style
  • ShellCheck — Shell script static analysis (use this!)

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes
Mistake: Checking /proc/meminfo inside containers for memory
Solution: Use cgroup v2 files: /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current, memory.events

| Command | Description | |---|---| | `set` | Script Anatomy & Shebang | | `chmod` | Script Anatomy & Shebang | | `bash` | Script Anatomy & Shebang | | `set` | Variables, Parameters & Quoting | | `NAME` | Variables, Parameters & Quoting | | `echo` | Variables, Parameters & Quoting | | `set` | Conditionals & Control Flow | | `if` | Conditionals & Control Flow | | `elif` | Conditionals & Control Flow | | `set` | Loops & Arrays | | `for` | Loops & Arrays | | `echo` | Loops & Arrays | | `set` | Functions & Error Handling | | `log` | Functions & Error Handling | | `local` | Functions & Error Handling | | `set` | Interview Q&A | | `BACKUP_DIR` | Interview Q&A | | `SOURCE` | Interview Q&A |

Troubleshooting

Symptom: set returns unexpected output
Cause: Incorrect arguments, wrong working directory, or stale cached data
Solution: Verify arguments with `man set`; check current path with `pwd`; clear cache if applicable
Symptom: Configuration changes not taking effect
Cause: Service not reloaded or syntax error in config file
Solution: Validate config syntax with built-in test flag; reload service with `systemctl reload` or `restart`
Symptom: shell basics operation slow or timing out
Cause: Resource exhaustion (disk I/O, memory pressure, or lock contention)
Solution: Check `iostat`, `free -m`, and `top`; look for stuck processes with `ps aux --sort=-%cpu`
Symptom: Permission issues accessing shell basics resources
Cause: Incorrect file ownership or overly restrictive permissions
Solution: Inspect with `ls -la`; fix with `chown`/ `chmod`; check ACLs with `getfacl`

Quick Quiz

Q1: What is Script Anatomy & Shebang?

A: A standardized directory structure that defines where files and directories should be placed on Linux systems

Q2: What does `set` do?

A: Lists files and directories in the current or specified path

Q3: Which command would you use to view disk usage of the shell basics directory?

A: du -sh /path/to/directory

Q4: A user reports "Permission denied" when trying to access a shell basics directory. What is the first command you should run?

A: ls -ld /path/to/directory

Q5: In a shell basics scenario, your monitoring alerts show set failing intermittently. What is the correct debugging workflow?

A: Check logs → verify configuration → test in staging → apply fix → monitor

FAANG Insight

  • FAANG Architect's Note: Shell scripts are the most common type of code I see deployed in production that has no tests
  • FAANG, we enforce: (1) ShellCheck runs as part of CI, (2) scripts must have error handling (set -euo pipefail), (3) no script longer than 200 lines without justification

Production Tips

  • production that has no tests
  • production experience

Best Practices

  • Use version control for all configuration (/etc, scripts, Dockerfiles)
  • Implement infrastructure as code; avoid manual server changes
  • Test changes in staging before production; have rollback plans

Enterprise Perspective

In enterprise environments, shell scripting basics is managed through automation (Ansible, Terraform), monitored centrally (Prometheus, Datadog), and governed by compliance policies (CIS benchmarks, SOC2). Changes go through change advisory boards with rollback procedures documented.

Chapter 6

Text Processing: grep, sed, awk

beginnerFoundations · 17 min read · 13 min practice

grep, sed, and awk are the three pillars of Linux text processing. At Enterprise, every engineer — from SRE to ML researcher — uses these tools daily. Mastering them signals that you think in Unix pipelines: finding patterns, transforming output, and extracting insights without needing to write a script.

faang-insightLinuxtext-processinggrepsedawkinterview

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux cli basics, linux shell basics

Learning Objectives

  • Understand grep — Global Regular Expression Print
  • Understand sed — Stream Editor
  • Understand awk — Pattern Scanning and Processing Language
  • Understand xargs — Build and Execute Commands
  • Understand pipeline-Fu: Combining Everything

Real-Life Analogy: Text Processing as a Factory Line

grep is the quality inspector (filters lines). sed is the find-and-replace machine. awk is the programmable assembler that builds reports. sort organizes inventory. uniq removes duplicates. Pipes (|) are conveyor belts moving text between stations.

grep — Global Regular Expression Print

grep searches files for lines matching a pattern. It's the first tool you reach for when investigating anything on a Linux system. Production engineers use grep constantly — finding error messages, filtering log output, checking configurations across directories.
bash
# Basic usage
grep 'error' /var/log/syslog              # Find all lines containing 'error'
grep -i 'warning' app.log                 # Case-insensitive
grep -v 'debug' app.log                   # Invert match (exclude debug)
grep -c 'timeout' access.log              # Count matches
grep -n 'exception' stacktrace.log        # Show line numbers

# Useful flags
grep -r 'TODO' /src/                      # Recursive search in directory
grep -l 'config' /etc/**/*.conf           # List only filenames with matches
grep -w 'class' schema.sql               # Whole word match (not 'subclass')
grep -A3 -B2 'FATAL' app.log             # Show 2 lines before, 3 after context
grep -E 'error|fail|timeout' log          # Extended regex (multiple patterns)

# Piped grep — filter command output
dmesg | grep -i 'error'                   # Kernel errors
ps aux | grep 'python'                    # Find python processes (but grep itself
matches!)
ps aux | grep '[p]ython'                  # Trick: brackets prevent grep from matching
itself

# Production scenario: count unique error types in a 10GB log
grep -oP 'ERROR \K\w+' app.log | sort | uniq -c | sort -rn
# -o: only matching text, -P: perl regex, \K: discard before
# (This extracts the word after 'ERROR ')

sed — Stream Editor

sed transforms text streams. It's grep's more powerful cousin — find patterns AND modify them. sed is essential for batch edits, text replacement, and pipeline formatting.
bash
# Basic substitution (the most common operation)
sed 's/old/new/' file.txt                 # Replace first occurrence per line
sed 's/old/new/g' file.txt                # Replace ALL occurrences per line (global)
sed 's/old/new/gi' file.txt               # Global + case-insensitive
sed 's/old/new/2' file.txt                # Replace second occurrence only

# In-place editing (careful!)
sed -i 's/foo/bar/g' config.conf          # Edit file in place (Linux version)
sed -i '' 's/foo/bar/g' config.conf       # macOS/BSD: empty extension = no backup
sed -i.bak 's/foo/bar/g' config.conf      # Create backup with .bak extension

# Line addressing
sed '10s/old/new/' file.txt               # Replace on line 10 only
sed '5,10s/old/new/g' file.txt            # Replace on lines 5-10
sed '5,$s/old/new/g' file.txt             # Replace from line 5 to end

# Delete lines
sed '/debug/d' app.log                    # Delete lines matching 'debug'
sed '1,5d' file.txt                       # Delete first 5 lines
sed '/^$/d' file.txt                      # Delete empty lines

# Print specific lines
sed -n '10,20p' file.txt                  # Print lines 10-20 (like head/tail combo)
sed -n '/ERROR/p' /var/log/syslog         # Print only lines matching ERROR

# Multiple commands
sed -e 's/foo/bar/' -e 's/abc/xyz/' file.txt

# Production scenario: mask IP addresses in a log file
sed -E 's/[0-9]{1,3}(\.[0-9]{1,3}){3}/***.***.***.***/g' access.log

awk — Pattern Scanning and Processing Language

awk is a full programming language designed for text processing. It splits lines into fields, processes them with C-like syntax, and is unmatched for extracting columns, computing statistics, and reformatting output. If grep is a scalpel and sed is a Swiss Army knife, awk is a workshop.
bash
# awk's basic structure:
# awk 'pattern { action }' file

# Field splitting (default: whitespace)
awk '{print $1}' access.log              # Print first field (IP address)
awk '{print $1, $NF}' access.log          # First and last field
awk -F: '{print $1}' /etc/passwd          # Custom delimiter (:)

# Patterns
awk '/error/ {print $0}' app.log          # Print lines matching 'error'
awk 'NR > 5 {print}' file.txt             # Skip first 5 lines
awk 'length($0) > 80' file.txt            # Lines longer than 80 characters

# BEGIN and END blocks
awk 'BEGIN {print "Report"} {print $0} END {print "---End---"}' file

# Computations
awk '{sum += $1} END {print sum}' numbers.txt      # Sum of first column
awk '{count++} END {print count}' file.txt          # Line count
awk '{sum += $1} END {print sum/NR}' numbers.txt    # Average

# Formatted output
awk '{printf "%-20s %s\n", $1, $NF}' /etc/passwd

# Multi-line awk scripts
awk '{
    if ($1 ~ /ERROR/) {
        errors++
        print $0 > "errors.log"
    }
} END {
    print "Total errors:", errors
}' app.log

# Production scenario: parse nginx access log (combined format)
# Fields: $1=IP, $6=method, $7=path, $9=status, $10=bytes
awk '{
    ip[$1]++                         # Count requests per IP
    status[$9]++                     # Count HTTP status codes
    total_bytes += $10               # Total bytes served
} END {
    print "=== Top IPs ===";
    for (i in ip) print i, ip[i];
    print "=== Status Codes ===";
    for (s in status) print s, status[s];
    print "Total bytes:", total_bytes;
}' /var/log/nginx/access.log

xargs — Build and Execute Commands

xargs takes input from stdin and passes it as arguments to another command. It's the glue that connects find/grep output to commands like rm, chmod, or cp. Understanding xargs is what separates intermediate from advanced CLI users.
bash
# Basic usage: pass filenames to a command
find /tmp -name '*.tmp' -type f | xargs rm -f    # Remove temp files
find . -name '*.py' | xargs wc -l                 # Count lines in all Python files

# Handle filenames with spaces (use -0 with null-separated)
find . -name '*.log' -print0 | xargs -0 rm

# Parallel execution
find . -name '*.gz' | xargs -P 4 gunzip          # Unzip 4 files at once

# Replace string placeholder
ls *.txt | xargs -I {} cp {} /backup/             # Copy each file to /backup

# Prompt before executing (safe mode)
find . -name '*.swp' | xargs -p rm               # 'y' to confirm each

# Production scenario: batch chmod with confirmation
find /app/deploy -type f | xargs -p -I {} chmod 644 {}

# Count lines across source files
grep -rl 'TODO' /src | xargs wc -l | tail -1     # Total TODO lines

Pipeline-Fu: Combining Everything

The real power comes from combining these tools. These compound pipelines are the kind of thing Production engineers type daily — and they make excellent interview problems.
bash
# Find top 5 slowest requests from an nginx log
# ($NF is request time in seconds)
awk '{print $NF, $0}' access.log | sort -rn | head -5 | awk '{print $2}'

# Find all unique IPs accessing /api, count requests
awk '$7 ~ /^\/api/ {print $1}' access.log | sort | uniq -c | sort -rn

# Report on HTTP status codes with percentage
awk '{
    total++
    status[$9]++
} END {
    print "Total requests:", total;
    for (s in status) printf "%s: %d (%.1f%%)\n", s, status[s], status[s]/total*100
}' access.log

# Find processes with high memory, format as CSV
ps aux --sort=-%mem | awk 'NR>1 {printf "%s,%s,%s\n", $2, $11, $4}' | head -10

# Monitor log file in real-time for error patterns
tail -f /var/log/app.log | grep --line-buffered 'ERROR' | awk '{print $1, $3, $NF}'

# Batch rename files with sed
for f in *.txt; do mv "$f" "$(echo "$f" | sed 's/ /_/g')"; done

# Production scenario: find files modified in last 24h, compress them
find /var/log/myapp -mtime -1 -name '*.log' | xargs tar -czf backup.tar.gz

Interview Q&A

Q: 'Using a one-liner, find the top 10 IP addresses hitting your web server from an nginx access log.'

A: 'awk '{print $1}' access.log | sort | uniq -c | sort -rn | head -10''

Q: 'You need to replace all occurrences of 'http://' with 'https://' in a directory of config files, but only in lines that contain 'api.' Do it without an editor.'

A: 'sed -i '/api/s|http://|https://|g' /etc/nginx/sites-enabled/*' — the /api/ pattern limits sed to lines containing 'api', and I use | as delimiter to avoid escaping slashes.'

Q: 'Parse /etc/passwd and list all users with login shells that aren't /sbin/nologin or /bin/false.'

A: 'awk -F: '$7 !~ /nologin|false/ {print $1, $7}' /etc/passwd''

References & Further Reading

  • GNU Grep Manual
  • GNU Sed Manual
  • GAWK (Awk) Manual — The definitive reference
  • sed & awk — Dale Dougherty & Arnold Robbins (O'Reilly)

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes
Mistake: Checking /proc/meminfo inside containers for memory
Solution: Use cgroup v2 files: /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current, memory.events

Chapter 7

Git Version Control

advancedFoundations · 17 min read · 13 min practice

Git is the version control system that underpins all modern software development. Created by Linus Torvalds for Linux kernel development, Git is now used by every Enterprise company. Understanding Git deeply — not just add/commit/push — is essential for working in large engineering teams.

faang-insightLinuxgitversion-controlinterview

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux cli basics

Learning Objectives

  • Understand git Objects: Blobs, Trees, Commits
  • Understand branching & Merging Strategies
  • Understand interactive Rebase & History Rewriting
  • Understand git in Production: The Enterprise Workflow
  • Understand git Configuration & Productivity Tips

Real-Life Analogy: Git as a Time Machine

Commits are snapshots in time. Branches are parallel timelines. Merge brings timelines together. Rebase rewrites history to make it linear. The working directory is your present. The index (staging) is your packing list for the next snapshot. Remote is a backup vault in another dimension.

Git Objects: Blobs, Trees, Commits

Git is fundamentally a content-addressable filesystem with a VCS UI on top. Every file version is stored as a blob, directories as trees, and snapshots as commits — all referenced by their SHA-1 hash. Understanding this model is the key to mastering Git.
bash
# Git's object model:
# blob  → file content (hash of content)
# tree  → directory listing (maps names → blobs/trees)
# commit → snapshot + metadata (tree hash, parent hash, author, message)

# Explore the object database:
git init myrepo && cd myrepo
echo "hello" > file.txt
git add file.txt
git commit -m "Initial commit"

# What just happened:
git cat-file -p HEAD
# tree <tree-hash>
# author Kallol ...
# committer Kallol ...
# Initial commit

git cat-file -p HEAD^{tree}
# 100644 blob <blob-hash>    file.txt

git cat-file -p <blob-hash>
# hello

# Key insight: Git stores content, not files.
# If you rename a file without changing content, the blob is reused.
# If two files have the same content, they share a blob.
# This is why git is space-efficient for text files.

Branching & Merging Strategies

Branches are lightweight pointers to commits. Creating a branch is nearly instant — just a 41-byte file. Enterprise teams use various branching strategies; understanding the tradeoffs between merge, rebase, and squash is an interview topic.
bash
# Branches are just labels on commits
# main → C3 ← feature → C4
#              ↖         ↗
#               merge commit

git branch              # List local branches
git branch feature      # Create branch from HEAD
git checkout feature    # Switch to branch
git checkout -b feature # Create and switch (one command)
git switch feature      # Modern alternative to checkout

# === Merge ===
git checkout main
git merge feature       # Creates a merge commit
# Pros: preserves history, safe
# Cons: cluttered history with merge commits

# === Rebase ===
git checkout feature
git rebase main         # Replay feature commits on top of main
git checkout main
git merge feature       # Fast-forward merge
# Pros: linear history, clean
# Cons: rewrites history (never rebase shared branches!)

# === Squash ===
git merge --squash feature
git commit -m "Add feature"
# Pros: single commit per feature
# Cons: loses intermediate commits (debugging harder)

# Enterprise common workflows:
# Google: squash-merge (single commit per CL)
# Meta: rebase + merge (linear history)
# Amazon: merge commits (preserve full history)

Interactive Rebase & History Rewriting

Interactive rebase lets you rewrite history before sharing. Never rewrite history on shared/public branches. For personal feature branches, it keeps the commit log clean and reviewable.
bash
# Interactive rebase — reorder, squash, edit, drop commits
git rebase -i HEAD~5     # Rebase last 5 commits

# In the editor, you'll see:
# pick abc1234 First commit
# pick def5678 Second commit
# pick hij9010 Third commit
#
# Commands:
# pick   = use commit as-is
# reword = change commit message
# edit   = stop to amend
# squash = combine with previous commit
# fixup  = squash but discard message
# drop   = delete commit

# Example: squash the last 3 commits into one:
git rebase -i HEAD~3
# Change 'pick' to 'squash' for commits 2 and 3

# Amend the last commit (fix message or add forgotten changes)
git add forgotten-file.txt
git commit --amend --no-edit   # Add to last commit without editing message

# Undo the last commit but keep changes staged
git reset --soft HEAD~1

# Undo the last commit and unstage changes
git reset HEAD~1               # --mixed (default)

# Completely discard last commit and changes
git reset --hard HEAD~1        # DANGER: destructive!

Git in Production: The Enterprise Workflow

At Enterprise, Git workflows enforce code review, CI/CD, and deployment gates. Understanding these patterns helps you navigate large codebases and collaborate effectively.
bash
# === Typical Enterprise Feature Workflow ===

# 1. Start from latest main
git checkout main
git pull --rebase          # Fetch + rebase local changes

# 2. Create feature branch
git checkout -b feat/my-feature

# 3. Make changes, commit often (incrementally)
git add -p                 # Interactive staging (hunk by hunk)
git commit -m "feat: add user auth"

# 4. Keep branch updated with main
git fetch origin
git rebase origin/main     # Rebase instead of merge

# 5. Final cleanup before push
git rebase -i origin/main  # Squash fixup commits
git log --oneline          # Verify clean history

# 6. Push and create pull request
git push -u origin feat/my-feature
# (Open PR in GitHub/GitLab/Bitbucket)

# 7. Address review feedback
# Make changes, then:
git add -p
git commit --amend --no-edit  # Keep single commit (if squashing)
# or: just add more commits (if merging)
git push --force-with-lease   # Safe force-push after rebase

# 8. Merge (via PR button — squash or merge commit)

# === Cherry-pick for hotfixes ===
git checkout release/v2.0
git cherry-pick abc1234    # Pick a specific commit from main

# === Bisect: find the commit that introduced a bug ===
git bisect start
git bisect bad             # Current commit is bad
git bisect good v1.0       # v1.0 is known good
# Git checks out a midpoint commit — test it
git bisect good            # Or: git bisect bad
# Repeat until the first bad commit is found
git bisect reset

Git Configuration & Productivity Tips

A well-configured Git setup saves hours. These are the configurations every Production engineers sets up on day one.
bash
# Global configuration (set once)
git config --global user.name "Kallol Chakraborty"
git config --global user.email "kallol@example.com"
git config --global core.editor vim
git config --global init.defaultBranch main

# Useful aliases
git config --global alias.lg "log --oneline --graph --all --decorate"
git config --global alias.last "log -1 HEAD"
git config --global alias.unstage "reset HEAD --"
git config --global alias.amend "commit --amend --no-edit"
git config --global alias.st "status -sb"

# Better diffing
git config --global diff.algorithm patience
git config --global diff.context 5

# .gitignore essentials for Linux projects:
# *.log
# *.swp
# .DS_Store
# build/
# dist/
# node_modules/
# .env

# === Production scenario: large monorepo ===
git clone --depth 1 <repo>          # Shallow clone (no history)
git clone --filter=blob:none <repo> # Partial clone (no blobs until needed)
git sparse-checkout set /path       # Checkout only specific directories

# === Stashing ===
git stash                    # Save uncommitted changes
git stash -u                 # Include untracked files
git stash pop                # Restore and remove stash
git stash list               # List all stashes
git stash drop stash@{0}     # Delete a stash

Interview Q&A

Q: 'Explain the difference between git merge and git rebase. When would you use each?'

A: 'Merge creates a merge commit that ties two branches together — it preserves history exactly as it happened. Rebase rewrites your feature branch commits on top of the target branch — it creates a linear history. Use merge for shared branches (main, release) to preserve the true sequence of events. Use rebase for personal feature branches to keep history clean before review.'

Q: 'You accidentally committed to the wrong branch. How do you move the commit to the correct branch?'

A: 'git log to find the commit hash. git checkout correct-branch. git cherry-pick . git checkout wrong-branch. git reset --hard HEAD~1. Or use git rebase --onto to move a range of commits.'

Q: 'A junior engineer force-pushed to main and rewrote history. How do you recover?'

A: 'git reflog on the remote (if you have access) to find the original commit. git reset --hard . git push --force-with-lease to restore. Enable branch protection on main to prevent this — main should require PRs and disallow force-push.'

  • Atlassian Git Tutorials — Visual explanations of Git concepts
  • Oh Shit, Git! — Practical recoveries for common Git mistakes

Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
