Quick Quiz

Q1: What is Git Objects: Blobs, Trees, Commits?

A: A standardized directory structure that defines where files and directories should be placed on Linux systems

Q2: How do you list the contents of a git related directory?

A: Lists files and directories in the current or specified path

Q3: Which command would you use to view disk usage of the git directory?

A: du -sh /path/to/directory

Q4: A user reports "Permission denied" when trying to access a git directory. What is the first command you should run?

A: ls -ld /path/to/directory

Q5: In a git scenario, your monitoring alerts show git failing intermittently. What is the correct debugging workflow?

A: Check logs → verify configuration → test in staging → apply fix → monitor

FAANG Insight

  • FAANG Architect's Note: Git was created to solve a specific problem: 20,000+ developers contributing to the Linux kernel simultaneously
  • FAANG monorepo models (Google's Piper, Meta's Mercurial-based repo) are more sophisticated, but Git skills translate directly

Production Tips

  • Monitor key metrics continuously; alert on anomalies before users notice.
  • Automate routine tasks; manual processes do not scale.
  • Document runbooks for common incidents; practice them regularly.

Best Practices

  • Use version control for all configuration (/etc, scripts, Dockerfiles)
  • Implement infrastructure as code; avoid manual server changes
  • Test changes in staging before production; have rollback plans

Enterprise Perspective

In enterprise environments, git version control is managed through automation (Ansible, Terraform), monitored centrally (Prometheus, Datadog), and governed by compliance policies (CIS benchmarks, SOC2). Changes go through change advisory boards with rollback procedures documented.

Part 2

Systems & Operations

Systems operations for production environments: process management, package management, systemd, user administration, SSH, networking, storage, and security hardening.

Chapter 8

Process Management & Signals

intermediateSystems & Operations · 18 min read · 14 min practice

Processes are the fundamental unit of execution on Linux. Every program, daemon, and shell command runs as one or more processes. At Enterprise, understanding process lifecycle, signals, and the /proc filesystem is essential for debugging production issues, building resilient services, and optimizing performance.

faang-insightLinuxprocessessignalscgroupsproduction debugging

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux cli basics, linux shell basics

Learning Objectives

  • Understand process Lifecycle & States
  • Understand signals: The Linux Interrupt System
  • Understand daemons & Process Supervision
  • Understand cgroups & Containers (Modern Process Isolation)

Real-Life Analogy: Processes as Kitchen Staff

Each process is a chef. The kernel is the head chef assigning tasks (scheduling). Fork() creates a duplicate chef. Exec() gives the chef a new recipe. Signals are kitchen bells - SIGTERM is "finish your dish," SIGKILL is "leave immediately." Zombies are chefs who finished but nobody collected their timesheet.

Process Lifecycle & States

A Linux process transitions through states: Running (R), Sleeping (S), Uninterruptible Sleep (D), Zombie (Z), and Stopped (T). The kernel's scheduler allocates CPU time slices (time slices are ~100ms on desktop kernels, configurable via sched_latency_ns). At Enterprise, 'D' state processes blocking I/O are a common cause of system stalls — you diagnose this via ps and /proc//status.
bash
# === PS (Process Snapshot) ===
ps aux                        # All processes, BSD-style
ps -ef                         # All processes, standard format
ps auxf                        # Forest view (tree with parent-child)
ps aux --sort=-%mem | head -10  # Top 10 by memory
ps aux --sort=-%cpu | head -10  # Top 10 by CPU
ps -eo pid,ppid,cmd,%mem,%cpu --sort=-%cpu  # Custom columns

# === TOP (Real-time) ===
top                            # Interactive
  # Inside top:
  #  1 — toggle per-CPU view
  #  M — sort by memory
  #  P — sort by CPU
  #  k — kill a process
  #  q — quit

htop                           # Better top (color, scroll, tree mode)

# === /proc — The Virtual Filesystem ===
cat /proc/cpuinfo              # CPU details (model, cores, flags)
cat /proc/meminfo              # Memory details (total, free, cached)
cat /proc/loadavg              # Load averages + running/total processes
cat /proc/1/cmdline            # Command line of PID 1 (init/systemd)
ls /proc/<PID>/fd/             # Open file descriptors (great for leaking FDs)
cat /proc/<PID>/status         # Process state, memory, capabilities
cat /proc/<PID>/maps           # Memory-mapped regions
cat /proc/<PID>/environ        # Environment variables (null-separated, use tr)
tr '\0' '\n' < /proc/<PID>/environ

# === Key fields in ps ===
# PID   — Process ID
# PPID  — Parent Process ID
# %CPU  — CPU usage (% of one core)
# %MEM  — Memory usage (% of total RAM)
# VSZ   — Virtual memory size
# RSS   — Resident Set Size (actual physical memory)
# STAT  — Process state (R, S, D, Z, T, etc.)
# WCHAN — Kernel function the process is sleeping in

# === Finding specific processes ===
pgrep -f "python server.py"     # Find PID by pattern
pgrep -u www-data              # All processes for a user
pidof nginx                     # Find PIDs of named process
pkill -f "stale_script"        # Kill processes matching pattern

Signals: The Linux Interrupt System

Signals are asynchronous notifications sent to processes. They can be generated by the kernel (SIGSEGV on segfault), by user actions (Ctrl+C = SIGINT), or by other processes (kill command). Understanding signals is critical for writing robust daemons and debugging crashes in production. At Enterprise, signal handlers must be carefully written to avoid race conditions and deadlocks.
bash
# === Common Signals ===
# SIGTERM (15) — Graceful termination (default for kill)
# SIGKILL (9)  — Force kill (cannot be caught/ignored)
# SIGINT  (2)  — Interrupt from keyboard (Ctrl+C)
# SIGQUIT (3)  — Quit with core dump (Ctrl+\)
# SIGHUP  (1)  — Hangup (reload config for many daemons)
# SIGUSR1/2 (10,12) — User-defined signals
# SIGSTOP (19) — Stop process (cannot be caught/ignored)
# SIGCONT (18) — Continue a stopped process
# SIGSEGV (11) — Segmentation fault (invalid memory access)
# SIGPIPE (13) — Write to broken pipe (common in pipes)

# === Sending signals ===
kill -15 <PID>                 # SIGTERM (graceful)
kill -9 <PID>                  # SIGKILL (force, use as last resort)
kill -1 <PID>                  # SIGHUP (reload config)
kill -SIGUSR1 <PID>            # Send user-defined signal
killall nginx                   # Send signal to all matching processes

# === Trapping signals in scripts ===
#!/bin/bash
cleanup() {
    echo "Cleaning up..."
    rm -f /tmp/lockfile
    exit 0
}
trap cleanup SIGTERM SIGHUP SIGINT

# Run indefinitely until killed
echo "PID: $$"; while true; do sleep 1; done

# === Zombie Processes ===
# A zombie process (STAT=Z) has exited but its parent hasn't called wait().
# Zombies are harmless but indicate a buggy parent. They consume a PID + entry.
# You cannot kill a zombie. You must kill its parent (PPID) to let init reap it.

# === Process Priority & Niceness ===
# Nice values: -20 (highest priority) to +19 (lowest priority)
nice -n 10 ./slow_task           # Run with lower priority (nice=10)
renice -n -5 -p <PID>           # Change priority of running process
chrt -r 1 ./realtime_task       # Set real-time scheduling (SCHED_RR)

# === Limits ===
ulimit -a                        # All limits for current shell
ulimit -n 65536                  # Max open file descriptors
cat /proc/<PID>/limits           # Limits for a specific process

Daemons & Process Supervision

A daemon is a background process that runs independently of any terminal. Modern Linux uses systemd for service supervision, but legacy approaches include init.d scripts and supervisord. In Enterprise environments, every service has a supervision system that auto-restarts on failure and collects metrics.
bash
# === Systemd Service ===
systemctl status nginx           # Check status
systemctl start nginx            # Start service
systemctl stop nginx             # Stop service
systemctl restart nginx          # Restart
systemctl enable nginx           # Start on boot
systemctl disable nginx          # Remove from autostart
systemctl daemon-reload          # Reload unit files after editing
journalctl -u nginx              # View logs for service

# === Background & Daemonize ===
# Simple backgrounding
./server.sh &

# Using nohup to survive terminal close
nohup ./long_task > output.log 2>&1 &

disown                           # Remove from shell job table

# daemon(3) / double-fork — traditional daemon creation
# 1. Fork -> parent exits -> child orphaned to init
# 2. Child calls setsid() to create new session
# 3. Fork again -> second child continues
# 4. Close stdin/stdout/stderr, chdir to /
# This is handled automatically by systemd unit files.

# === Enterprise pattern: Health checks ===
# Every daemon should answer a health check endpoint.
# Pattern: GET /healthz returns 200 OK
kill -0 <PID>                    # Check if process exists (no signal sent)
if kill -0 "$PID" 2>/dev/null; then
    echo "Process $PID is running"
fi

cgroups & Containers (Modern Process Isolation)

Control groups (cgroups) limit and account for resource usage (CPU, memory, I/O) across process hierarchies. Docker and Kubernetes are built on cgroups + namespaces. At Enterprise, every production process runs inside a cgroup — resource limits are non-negotiable.
bash
# === cgroups v2 (modern) ===
# Hierarchy at /sys/fs/cgroup/
ls /sys/fs/cgroup/

# Create a cgroup and set limits
sudo mkdir /sys/fs/cgroup/myapp
sudo sh -c 'echo "100000 200000" > /sys/fs/cgroup/myapp/cpu.max'
sudo sh -c 'echo "100M" > /sys/fs/cgroup/myapp/memory.max'

# Add a process to the cgroup
echo $PID | sudo tee /sys/fs/cgroup/myapp/cgroup.procs

# === cgroups v1 (legacy) ===
# CPU shares (relative weight, default 1024)
sudo cgcreate -g cpu:mygroup
sudo cgset -r cpu.shares=512 mygroup
sudo cgclassify -g cpu:mygroup <PID>

# === Inspect container cgroups ===
# Inside a Docker container:
cat /proc/1/cgroup

# From host:
ls /sys/fs/cgroup/system.slice/docker-<CONTAINER_ID>.scope/

# === Resource limits via systemd ===
# In a systemd unit file:
[Service]
MemoryMax=256M
CPUQuota=50%
TasksMax=100

# === /proc/self/status !== cgroup limits
# Remember: /proc/meminfo shows host memory, not cgroup memory.
# Use cgroup memory stats for container-aware monitoring.
cat /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current

Interview Q&A

Q: 'A process on a production server is stuck in 'D' (uninterruptible sleep) state. What does this mean, and how do you diagnose it?'

A: 'D state means the process is waiting on I/O (disk or network) in a kernel operation that cannot be interrupted. Common causes: NFS server timeout, disk controller failure, slow storage. Diagnose by checking ps aux | grep ' D' to find the PID, then look at /proc//stack to see which kernel function is blocking, and dmesg | tail for I/O errors. If the underlying device recovers, processes leave D state — but sometimes they're stuck permanently and need a reboot.'

Q: 'Explain the difference between SIGTERM and SIGKILL. When would you use each?'

A: 'SIGTERM (15) is graceful termination — the process can catch it, clean up resources, flush buffers, and exit cleanly. SIGKILL (9) cannot be caught or ignored — the kernel immediately terminates the process without cleanup. Always try SIGTERM first. Use SIGKILL only when a process is ignoring SIGTERM or is stuck in an unkillable state (like D state, where even SIGKILL is queued and won't take effect until the kernel operation completes). At Enterprise, our service managers (like our internal equivalent of systemd) send SIGTERM and wait 30 seconds before escalating to SIGKILL.'

Q: 'What is a zombie process, and why can't you kill it?'

A: 'A zombie process has already exited but still has an entry in the process table because its parent hasn't called wait() / waitpid() to read its exit status. It's dead — 'kill' won't work because there's nothing to signal. The fix is to kill the parent (PPID) so init (PID 1) adopts and reaps the zombie children. In production, a long-running parent with a buggy signal handler can accumulate thousands of zombies, exhausting the PID table.'

References & Further Reading

  • signal(7) man page — Complete Linux signal reference
  • cgroups v2 Documentation — Kernel.org official docs
  • The Linux Programming Interface — Michael Kerrisk, the definitive reference on Linux processes and signals

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Using kill -9 as first resort
Solution: Send SIGTERM first (kill PID); wait for graceful shutdown; only use SIGKILL if process ignores SIGTERM
Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes

Troubleshooting

Symptom: ps returns unexpected output
Cause: Incorrect arguments, wrong working directory, or stale cached data
Solution: Verify arguments with `man ps`; check current path with `pwd`; clear cache if applicable
Symptom: Configuration changes not taking effect
Cause: Service not reloaded or syntax error in config file
Solution: Validate config syntax with built-in test flag; reload service with `systemctl reload` or `restart`
Symptom: processes operation slow or timing out
Cause: Resource exhaustion (disk I/O, memory pressure, or lock contention)
Solution: Check `iostat`, `free -m`, and `top`; look for stuck processes with `ps aux --sort=-%cpu`
Symptom: Permission issues accessing processes resources
Cause: Incorrect file ownership or overly restrictive permissions
Solution: Inspect with `ls -la`; fix with `chown`/ `chmod`; check ACLs with `getfacl`

A: Check logs → verify configuration → test in staging → apply fix → monitor

  • FAANG incident root cause: a process inherited a low ulimit from its parent and hit 'too many open files' during traffic spike

Production Tips

  • production incident where auto-scaling has failed — your ability to navigate /proc, interpret process states, and trace system calls is what separates the engineer who fixes the issue in 5 minutes from the one who's still reading man pages 30 minutes later
  • Production Tip: Set ulimit -n (file descriptors) appropriately for your workload

Best Practices

  • Use version control for all configuration (/etc, scripts, Dockerfiles)
  • Implement infrastructure as code; avoid manual server changes
  • Test changes in staging before production; have rollback plans

Chapter 9

Package Management

intermediateSystems & Operations · 15 min read · 11 min practice

Package management is how Linux systems install, update, and remove software. At Enterprise, you'll work across Debian-based (Ubuntu), RHEL-based (Amazon Linux 2/2023), and containerized environments. Understanding the differences between dpkg/APT, rpm/YUM/DNF, and compiling from source is essential for building reproducible infrastructure.

faang-insightLinuxaptyumdnfpackage managementcontainers

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux cli basics, linux filesystem

Learning Objectives

  • Understand debian/Ubuntu: APT & dpkg
  • Understand rHEL/CentOS/Amazon Linux: RPM & YUM/DNF
  • Understand compiling from Source & Alternatives
  • Understand docker & Containers: The Modern "Package"

Real-Life Analogy: Package Manager as a Curated App Store

Repositories are verified warehouses. Dependencies are "this dish requires these ingredients." The package manager resolves the full shopping list automatically. apt/dnf are different store chains - same concept, different inventory.

Debian/Ubuntu: APT & dpkg

Ubuntu (the most common dev environment at Enterprise) uses APT (Advanced Package Tool) on top of dpkg. Packages are .deb files. /etc/apt/sources.list and /etc/apt/sources.list.d/ define repositories. Always run apt update before apt install — this refreshes the package index.
bash
# === Repository management ===
cat /etc/apt/sources.list                    # Main repos
ls /etc/apt/sources.list.d/                  # Third-party repos (Docker, Kubernetes,
etc.)

# === APT commands ===
sudo apt update                               # Refresh package index (must run first!)
sudo apt upgrade                              # Upgrade all packages
sudo apt full-upgrade                         # Upgrade with dependency changes
sudo apt autoremove                           # Remove orphaned dependencies

sudo apt install nginx                        # Install package
sudo apt install -y nginx                     # Non-interactive (script-friendly)
sudo apt remove nginx                         # Remove package (leave config)
sudo apt purge nginx                          # Remove package + config files

apt list --installed                          # List installed packages
apt list --upgradable                         # List available updates
apt search "web server"                       # Search packages
apt show nginx                                # Show package details

# === dpkg (low-level) ===
dpkg -i package.deb                           # Install .deb file directly
dpkg -r package                              # Remove package
dpkg -l                                       # List installed packages
dpkg -L nginx                                 # List files owned by package
dpkg -S /usr/bin/nvim                         # Find which package owns a file

# === Fix broken dependencies ===
sudo apt --fix-broken install                 # Repair broken dpkg state

# === Pinning package versions ===
# /etc/apt/preferences.d/pin-nginx
# Package: nginx
# Pin: version 1.24.*
# Pin-Priority: 1001

# === Installing specific version ===
apt list -a nginx                             # All available versions
sudo apt install nginx=1.24.0-1ubuntu1        # Install specific version
sudo apt-mark hold nginx                      # Prevent automatic upgrade
sudo apt-mark unhold nginx                    # Re-enable upgrades

RHEL/CentOS/Amazon Linux: RPM & YUM/DNF

Amazon Linux 2 and Amazon Linux 2023 (the standard at AWS/Enterprise) are RHEL-based. They use RPM (.rpm packages) with YUM (Amazon Linux 2) or DNF (Amazon Linux 2023) as the high-level package manager. The underlying tool is rpm.
bash
# === YUM (Amazon Linux 2, CentOS 7) ===
sudo yum update -y                             # Update all packages
sudo yum install -y nginx                      # Install
sudo yum remove nginx                          # Remove
sudo yum list installed                        # List installed
yum list available "nginx*"                    # Search available
yum info nginx                                 # Package details
sudo yum autoremove                            # Clean orphaned deps
sudo yum history                               # Transaction history

# === DNF (Amazon Linux 2023, Fedora, RHEL 9) ===
sudo dnf install -y nginx
sudo dnf remove nginx
sudo dnf upgrade --refresh
sudo dnf list --installed
sudo dnf repolist                               # List configured repos
sudo dnf provides /usr/bin/nginx                # Which package provides a file?
sudo dnf history

# === RPM (low-level) ===
rpm -ivh package.rpm                           # Install RPM
rpm -e package                                 # Remove RPM
rpm -qa                                        # Query all installed packages
rpm -ql nginx                                  # List files owned by package
rpm -qf /usr/sbin/nginx                        # Find package owning a file
rpm -qi nginx                                  # Package info

# === EPEL (Extra Packages for Enterprise Linux) ===
sudo amazon-linux-extras install epel          # Amazon Linux 2
sudo dnf install epel-release                  # Amazon Linux 2023 / RHEL 9

# === Amazon Linux Extras ===
sudo amazon-linux-extras list                  # Available topics
sudo amazon-linux-extras install nginx1        # Install from extras

# === Locking versions ===
echo "exclude=nginx*" | sudo tee -a /etc/yum.conf  # Prevent updates (YUM)
sudo dnf versionlock add nginx*                # Lock version (DNF)

Compiling from Source & Alternatives

Sometimes you need software not in package repos, or you need specific compile-time flags. At Enterprise, critical services are often compiled with custom optimization flags (PGO, LTO, specific CPU architectures). Understanding the build chain (configure, make, make install) is essential for infrastructure engineers.
bash
# === Classic build chain ===
wget https://example.com/software-1.0.tar.gz
tar -xzf software-1.0.tar.gz
cd software-1.0/

# Step 1: Configure (check dependencies, set options)
./configure --prefix=/usr/local --enable-optimizations

# Step 2: Build
make -j$(nproc)                                # Parallel build using all cores

# Step 3: Install
sudo make install                              # Copy to system directories

# === Common configure flags ===
# --prefix=/usr          — Install to /usr (not /usr/local)
# --sysconfdir=/etc      — Config files location
# --with-ssl             — Enable SSL support
# --enable-debug         — Build with debug symbols

# === Checkinstall (create a .deb/.rpm from source) ===
sudo apt install checkinstall
sudo checkinstall make install                 # Creates and installs a .deb

# === Stow (manage manually compiled software) ===
# Install each version under /usr/local/stow/<pkg>-<ver>/
# Symlink to /usr/local/ with stow
sudo stow -d /usr/local/stow -S nginx-1.24

# === Enterprise pattern: hermetic builds ===
# At Enterprise, we don't compile on production servers.
# Builds happen in hermetic build systems (e.g., Bazel, Buck).
# Artifacts are versioned and deployed via package managers or containers.

# === Alternatives: Homebrew on Linux ===
/bin/bash -c "$(curl -fsSL
https://raw.githubusercontent.com/Homebrew/install/HEAD/install.sh)"
brew install nginx
brew services start nginx

Docker & Containers: The Modern "Package"

At Enterprise, most services are packaged as container images rather than native packages. Containers bundle the application + its dependencies + the package manager snapshot. Understanding the Dockerfile and multi-stage builds is essential for modern infrastructure.
bash
# === Dockerfile with package management ===
FROM ubuntu:22.04 AS builder

# Always combine apt update + install in ONE RUN layer
RUN apt-get update && apt-get install -y \
    build-essential \
    libssl-dev \
    && rm -rf /var/lib/apt/lists/*

# Multi-stage build: builder → runtime
FROM ubuntu:22.04 AS runtime

# Copy only the compiled binary, not the build tools
COPY --from=builder /app/compiled_binary /usr/local/bin/

# Distroless images (no package manager at all)
FROM gcr.io/distroless/base-debian12:latest

# === Best practices ===
# 1. Pin base image digests (not tags)
#    FROM ubuntu:22.04@sha256:abc123...
# 2. Combine RUN apt-get update && apt-get install (cache busting)
# 3. Clean apt cache in same layer: rm -rf /var/lib/apt/lists/*
# 4. Use --no-install-recommends to reduce image size
# 5. Never leave a package manager in the final image

Interview Q&A

Q: 'You need to install a specific version of nginx on Ubuntu, but apt installs the wrong version. What do you do?'

A: 'First, run apt list -a nginx to see all available versions. If my version is there, install it with apt install nginx=<version>. If not, I'd add the official nginx repository to /etc/apt/sources.list.d/ with the correct distribution codename. As a last resort, I'd download the .deb directly or compile from source. At Enterprise, we would never apt install on production — instead, we'd bake the correct version into an AMI or container image.'

Q: 'What's the difference between apt update and apt upgrade?'

A: 'apt update downloads the latest package lists from repositories — it tells you what's available. apt upgrade actually installs newer versions of installed packages based on those updated lists. Running upgrade without update will give you stale information.'

Q: 'How do package managers resolve dependencies?'

A: 'APT, YUM, and DNF maintain a dependency graph. Each package declares its dependencies (Depends:), conflicts (Conflicts:), and provides (Provides:). The solver finds a consistent set of package versions satisfying all constraints. If there's an unresolvable conflict, the tool reports it. At Enterprise, we avoid these issues by using deterministic builds and container images with fixed package snapshots.'

References & Further Reading

  • Debian APT Wiki — Official APT documentation
  • DNF Documentation — Official DNF reference
  • Dockerfile Best Practices — Official Docker guide

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Running destructive commands without verification
Solution: Always use `rm -i` or `ls` first to confirm what will be deleted
Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes

Troubleshooting

Symptom: cat returns unexpected output
Cause: Incorrect arguments, wrong working directory, or stale cached data
Solution: Verify arguments with `man cat`; check current path with `pwd`; clear cache if applicable
Symptom: Configuration changes not taking effect
Cause: Service not reloaded or syntax error in config file
Solution: Validate config syntax with built-in test flag; reload service with `systemctl reload` or `restart`
Symptom: package mgmt operation slow or timing out
Cause: Resource exhaustion (disk I/O, memory pressure, or lock contention)
Solution: Check `iostat`, `free -m`, and `top`; look for stuck processes with `ps aux --sort=-%cpu`
Symptom: Permission issues accessing package mgmt resources
Cause: Incorrect file ownership or overly restrictive permissions
Solution: Inspect with `ls -la`; fix with `chown`/ `chmod`; check ACLs with `getfacl`

Quick Quiz

Q1: What is Debian/Ubuntu: APT & dpkg?

A: A standardized directory structure that defines where files and directories should be placed on Linux systems

Q2: What does `cat` do?

A: Lists files and directories in the current or specified path

Q3: Which command would you use to view disk usage of the package mgmt directory?

A: du -sh /path/to/directory

Q4: A user reports "Permission denied" when trying to access a package mgmt directory. What is the first command you should run?

A: ls -ld /path/to/directory

Q5: In a package mgmt scenario, your monitoring alerts show cat failing intermittently. What is the correct debugging workflow?

A: Check logs → verify configuration → test in staging → apply fix → monitor

FAANG Insight

  • FAANG Architect's Note: In 15+ years across multiple FAANG companies, I've seen package management evolve from 'YUM on a fleet of pets' to 'everything runs in containers with immutable base images
  • FAANG, package updates are applied by rebuilding AMIs or rolling out new container versions — never in-place on running instances

Production Tips

  • Production Tip: Never run unattended-upgrades on production servers

Best Practices

  • Use version control for all configuration (/etc, scripts, Dockerfiles)
  • Implement infrastructure as code; avoid manual server changes
  • Test changes in staging before production; have rollback plans

Enterprise Perspective

In enterprise environments, package management is managed through automation (Ansible, Terraform), monitored centrally (Prometheus, Datadog), and governed by compliance policies (CIS benchmarks, SOC2). Changes go through change advisory boards with rollback procedures documented.

Chapter 10

systemd: The Modern Init System

intermediateSystems & Operations · 17 min read · 13 min practice

systemd is the init system and service manager for virtually every modern Linux distribution. It replaced SysV init and handles service startup, dependency management, logging, timers, socket activation, and resource control. At Enterprise, understanding systemd is essential for writing production service units, debugging boot issues, and building container runtimes.

faang-insightLinuxsystemdjournaldinitservice management

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux processes, linux shell basics

Learning Objectives

  • Understand core Concepts: Units & Targets
  • Understand writing a Service Unit File
  • Understand journalctl: The systemd Logging System
  • Understand timers (Replacing Cron) & Socket Activation
  • Understand system Analysis & Debugging

Real-Life Analogy: Systemd as the Conductor

Services are musicians. Unit files are sheet music - they define what to play, when to start, and who to wait for. Dependencies are "violin starts after cello." Socket activation is "play when the audience arrives." Journal is the recording of the performance.

Core Concepts: Units & Targets

systemd manages units. There are several types: service (daemons), socket (socket activation), timer (scheduled tasks, replacing cron), mount (filesystem mounts), target (group of units, replacing runlevels). Units are defined in .unit files located in /usr/lib/systemd/system/ (distro) and /etc/systemd/system/
bash
# === Basic systemctl commands ===
systemctl status nginx             # Status + recent logs
systemctl start nginx              # Start immediately
systemctl stop nginx               # Stop
systemctl restart nginx            # Restart (stop + start)
systemctl reload nginx             # Send SIGHUP (reload config)
systemctl enable nginx             # Start on boot (creates symlinks)
systemctl disable nginx            # Remove boot symlinks
systemctl daemon-reload            # Reload unit files after edits
systemctl is-active nginx          # Check if running (exit code 0/1)
systemctl is-enabled nginx         # Check if enabled

# === List units ===
systemctl list-units               # All active units
systemctl list-units --all         # All units (including inactive)
systemctl list-units --type=service  # Only services
systemctl list-unit-files          # All unit files + enable state
systemctl list-dependencies nginx  # Show dependency tree

# === Targets (runlevels) ===
systemctl list-units --type=target  # List targets
systemctl get-default               # Default boot target
systemctl set-default multi-user.target  # Boot to multi-user (no GUI)
systemctl isolate emergency.target      # Switch to emergency mode

# === Legacy runlevel mapping ===
# runlevel 0 → poweroff.target
# runlevel 1 → rescue.target
# runlevel 3 → multi-user.target
# runlevel 5 → graphical.target
# runlevel 6 → reboot.target

Writing a Service Unit File

A .service unit defines how to start, stop, and manage a daemon. The [Unit] section describes dependencies, [Service] defines execution, and [Install] controls enable/disable behavior. At Enterprise, every internally developed service ships with a production-grade systemd unit file that includes resource limits, health checks, and graceful shutdown settings.
bash
# Example: /etc/systemd/system/myapp.service
[Unit]
Description=My Production Service
After=network-online.target
Wants=network-online.target
Requires=postgresql.service
After=postgresql.service

[Service]
Type=simple                    # Default: process doesn't fork
User=myapp
Group=myapp
WorkingDirectory=/opt/myapp
ExecStart=/usr/bin/myapp --config /etc/myapp/config.yaml
ExecReload=/bin/kill -HUP $MAINPID
ExecStop=/bin/kill -TERM $MAINPID
Restart=always                 # Restart on crash
RestartSec=10                  # Wait 10s before restart
TimeoutStartSec=30             # Max time to start
TimeoutStopSec=30              # Max time to stop gracefully

# Resource limits
LimitNOFILE=65536
LimitNPROC=4096
MemoryMax=512M
CPUQuota=50%

# Security
ProtectSystem=strict
ProtectHome=true
PrivateTmp=true
NoNewPrivileges=true
CapabilityBoundingSet=CAP_NET_BIND_SERVICE

[Install]
WantedBy=multi-user.target

# === Type variants ===
# Type=simple       — Main process is ExecStart (default)
# Type=forking      — Process forks (daemonizes); notify when parent exits
# Type=oneshot      — Runs once (for setup/scripts); RemainAfterExit=yes
# Type=notify       — Process calls sd_notify() when ready
# Type=dbus         — Process registers on D-Bus; wait for bus name

# === Environment ===
# Set environment variables:
[Service]
Environment=MYAPP_ENV=production
Environment=JAVA_HOME=/usr/lib/jvm/java-11-openjdk
EnvironmentFile=-/etc/default/myapp     # File with KEY=VAL pairs

# === Override without editing original ===
sudo systemctl edit nginx           # Creates
/etc/systemd/system/nginx.service.d/override.conf
sudo systemctl cat nginx            # Show full unit with overrides applied

journalctl: The systemd Logging System

systemd replaced syslog with journald, a binary logging system with structured metadata (priority, facility, PID, UID, boot ID). journalctl queries the journal. In production debugging, journalctl -xe is often the first command run during an incident.
bash
# === Basic journalctl usage ===
journalctl                          # All logs (pager)
journalctl -n 50                    # Last 50 lines
journalctl -f                        # Follow (like tail -f)
journalctl -u nginx                 # Logs for a specific unit
journalctl -u nginx -u postgresql   # Multiple units

# === Time-based filtering ===
journalctl --since "5 min ago"
journalctl --since today
journalctl --since "2024-01-01" --until "2024-01-02"
journalctl --since yesterday -u nginx

# === Priority levels ===
journalctl -p err                    # Only errors and above
journalctl -p warning                # Warnings and above
# Levels: emerg(0), alert(1), crit(2), err(3), warning(4), notice(5), info(6), debug(7)

# === Output formats ===
journalctl -o verbose                # All structured fields
journalctl -o json                   # JSON output (for jq / log aggregation)
journalctl -o json-pretty

# === Disk usage ===
journalctl --disk-usage
sudo journalctl --vacuum-size=500M   # Keep only 500MB of logs
sudo journalctl --vacuum-time=7d     # Keep only 7 days

# === Boot-specific ===
journalctl -b -1                     # Previous boot (useful after crash)
journalctl -b -2                     # Two boots ago
journalctl --list-boots              # List boot numbers + IDs

# === Log a test message ===
logger "Test log message"            # Write to systemd journal
logger -p user.err "Custom error"    # With priority

# === Config: /etc/systemd/journald.conf ===
# SystemMaxUse=1G
# Compress=yes
# ForwardToSyslog=no

Timers (Replacing Cron) & Socket Activation

systemd timers replace cron with fine-grained scheduling, dependency support, and logging. Socket activation allows services to start on demand when a connection arrives — a lazy-start pattern used by many system services.
bash
# === Timer example ===
# /etc/systemd/system/backup.service
[Service]
Type=oneshot
ExecStart=/usr/local/bin/backup.sh

# /etc/systemd/system/backup.timer
[Unit]
Description=Daily Backup
Requires=backup.service

[Timer]
OnCalendar=daily
Persistent=true            # If missed (e.g., during downtime), run immediately
RandomizedDelaySec=3600    # Random delay within 1 hour (avoid thundering herd)

[Install]
WantedBy=timers.target

# === Manage timers ===
systemctl enable --now backup.timer
systemctl list-timers
# NEXT                        LEFT          LAST                        PASSED    UNIT
# Mon 2024-01-15 03:00:00    14h left      Sun 2024-01-14 03:00:01   9h ago   
backup.timer

# === OnCalendar patterns ===
# daily              → Every day at 00:00:00
# hourly             → Every hour at :00
# Mon *-*-* 09:00:00 → Every Monday at 9 AM
# *-*-01 00:00:00    → First of every month
# *:0/15             → Every 15 minutes

# === Socket activation ===
# Listen on a port; start service on first connection
# /etc/systemd/system/echo.socket
[Socket]
ListenStream=2000

[Install]
WantedBy=sockets.target

# /etc/systemd/system/echo@.service
[Service]
ExecStart=/usr/local/bin/echo-server
StandardInput=socket

# === Benefits of socket activation ===
# 1. Lazy-start: service consumes resources only when needed
# 2. Resilience: if service crashes, socket survives, next request restarts it
# 3. Parallel boot: services can start while socket is already listening

System Analysis & Debugging

systemd provides powerful tools for analyzing boot performance, system state, and unit interactions. At Enterprise, these tools are used in periodic 'boot health' checks and capacity planning reviews.
bash
# === Boot analysis ===
systemd-analyze                    # Total boot time
systemd-analyze blame              # Time taken by each unit (sorted)
systemd-analyze critical-chain     # Critical chain of slowest dependencies
systemd-analyze plot > boot.svg    # Generate a visualization

# === Unit dependency graph ===
systemctl list-dependencies nginx
systemd-analyze dot nginx | dot -Tsvg > nginx-deps.svg  # Visual graph

# === Failed units ===
systemctl --failed                 # List failed units

# === Check if systemd is running ===
ps -p 1 -o comm=                   # Should print 'systemd'

# === Emergency / Recovery ===
systemctl rescue                   # Rescue mode (single-user, minimal services)
systemctl emergency                # Emergency shell (PID 1 only, root filesystem ro)

# === Common issues ===
# 1. 'Unit not found' — systemctl daemon-reload
# 2. 'Dependency failed' — check required units (systemctl list-dependencies)
# 3. 'Start request repeated too quickly' — RestartSec is too short
# 4. 'Failed to connect to bus' — systemd is not PID 1 (e.g., inside container without
systemd)

Interview Q&A

Q: 'How does systemd start services in parallel during boot?'

A: 'systemd parses all unit files and builds a dependency graph. Units without inter-dependencies start in parallel using socket activation — even if a service isn't ready, its socket is listening, so dependent services don't block. The After= directive doesn't mean 'wait until it's started' — it means 'this unit should be in the dependency graph after the other.' Actual ordering is managed by Type=notify or Requires= with After=.'

Q: 'How would you debug a service that keeps crashing on startup?'

A: 'First, run journalctl -u <service> -n 50 --no-pager to see the logs. Then check systemctl status <service> for the exit code and recent journal entries. Common causes: missing environment variables, permission issues (check journalctl for 'Permission denied'), or dependency failures. I'd also check systemctl list-dependencies to ensure all required units are running. If the service restarts in a loop, check RestartSec= and increase it to avoid log flooding.'

Q: 'What's the difference between systemd and Docker?'

A: 'systemd is the init system for the host — it manages system services, mounts, sockets, and the boot process. Docker manages application containers. systemd can also manage containers (systemd-nspawn, podman with systemd integration), but their scope is different. In production, many Enterprise services run inside Docker containers on hosts that use systemd — the host systemd manages containerd/dockerd, and inside the container, the application might use a minimal init or no init at all.'

References & Further Reading

  • systemd Documentation — Official man pages and guides
  • systemd Essentials — DigitalOcean tutorial series
  • Linux systemd: A Practical Guide — In-depth reference for system administration

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes
Mistake: Checking /proc/meminfo inside containers for memory
Solution: Use cgroup v2 files: /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current, memory.events

Quick Reference

| Command | Description | |---|---| | `systemctl` | Core Concepts: Units & Targets | | `Description` | Writing a Service Unit File | | `After` | Writing a Service Unit File | | `Wants` | Writing a Service Unit File | | `journalctl` | journalctl: The systemd Logging System | | `sudo` | journalctl: The systemd Logging System | | `logger` | journalctl: The systemd Logging System | | `Type` | Timers (Replacing Cron) & Socket Activation | | `ExecStart` | Timers (Replacing Cron) & Socket Activation | | `Description` | Timers (Replacing Cron) & Socket Activation | | `systemd-analyze` | System Analysis & Debugging | | `systemctl` | System Analysis & Debugging | | `ps` | System Analysis & Debugging |

Troubleshooting

Symptom: systemctl returns unexpected output
Cause: Incorrect arguments, wrong working directory, or stale cached data
Solution: Verify arguments with `man systemctl`; check current path with `pwd`; clear cache if applicable
Symptom: Configuration changes not taking effect
Cause: Service not reloaded or syntax error in config file
Solution: Validate config syntax with built-in test flag; reload service with `systemctl reload` or `restart`
Symptom: systemd operation slow or timing out
Cause: Resource exhaustion (disk I/O, memory pressure, or lock contention)
Solution: Check `iostat`, `free -m`, and `top`; look for stuck processes with `ps aux --sort=-%cpu`
Symptom: Permission issues accessing systemd resources
Cause: Incorrect file ownership or overly restrictive permissions
Solution: Inspect with `ls -la`; fix with `chown`/ `chmod`; check ACLs with `getfacl`

Q1: What is Core Concepts: Units & Targets?

A: A standardized directory structure that defines where files and directories should be placed on Linux systems

Q2: What does `systemctl` do?

A: Lists files and directories in the current or specified path

Q3: Which command would you use to view disk usage of the systemd directory?

A: du -sh /path/to/directory

Q4: A user reports "Permission denied" when trying to access a systemd directory. What is the first command you should run?

A: ls -ld /path/to/directory

Q5: In a systemd scenario, your monitoring alerts show systemctl failing intermittently. What is the correct debugging workflow?

A: Check logs → verify configuration → test in staging → apply fix → monitor

Chapter 11

Users, Groups & Authentication

advancedSystems & Operations · 14 min read · 11 min practice

Linux is a multi-user operating system from the ground up. Every process runs as a specific user, every file has an owner and group, and access decisions are made by the kernel based on user/group IDs. At Enterprise, understanding user management, PAM (Pluggable Authentication Modules), and sudo policies is critical for security, compliance, and least-privilege deployments.

faang-insightLinuxusersgroupssudoPAMauthenticationsecurity

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux permissions, linux filesystem

Learning Objectives

  • Understand user & Group Fundamentals
  • Understand sudo: Delegated Privilege
  • Understand pAM: Pluggable Authentication Modules
  • Understand service Accounts & Least Privilege

Real-Life Analogy: Users as Club Members

Each user has a membership card (UID). Groups are clubs - members share access to club resources (files). Root is the club owner with master keys. sudo is a temporary VIP pass. The /etc/passwd is the member directory; /etc/shadow holds the secret handshakes.

User & Group Fundamentals

Users are identified by UID (User ID) and groups by GID (Group ID). The mapping is stored in /etc/passwd, /etc/shadow (passwords), /etc/group, and /etc/gshadow. UID 0 is root — the superuser with unrestricted access. At Enterprise, human engineers almost never use root — they sudo to specific commands.
bash
# === Key files ===
cat /etc/passwd          # User accounts: username:x:UID:GID:comment:home:shell
cat /etc/shadow          # Password hashes (root only)
cat /etc/group           # Group definitions: groupname:x:GID:member1,member2
cat /etc/gshadow         # Group passwords (rarely used)

# Understanding /etc/passwd format:
# kallol:x:1000:1000:Kallol:/home/kallol:/bin/bash
# [1] username
# [2] password placeholder (x = hash in /etc/shadow)
# [3] UID (User ID)
# [4] GID (Primary Group ID)
# [5] GECOS (user info, often empty)
# [6] Home directory
# [7] Login shell

# === Adding users ===
sudo useradd -m -s /bin/bash -G sudo,docker kallol
# -m  create home directory
# -s  login shell
# -G  supplementary groups
# -c  comment (full name)

sudo useradd --system --no-create-home --shell /usr/sbin/nologin myapp
# System user (UID < 1000), no login, no home

sudo passwd kallol         # Set/change password
sudo usermod -aG docker kallol   # Add to group (append, don't forget -a!)
sudo userdel -r kallol     # Delete user + home + mail spool

# === Listing users ===
who                       # Currently logged in users
w                         # Detailed who (login time, idle, what running)
users                     # Just usernames
id                        # Current user's UID, GID, groups
id kallol                 # Specific user's IDs
finger kallol             # User info (may need apt install finger)

# === Listing groups ===
groups                    # Current user's groups
groups kallol             # Specific user's groups
getent group sudo         # Members of 'sudo' group
lid -g sudo               # List members (may need apt install libuser)

sudo: Delegated Privilege

sudo allows authorized users to run commands as another user (typically root). At Enterprise, sudo is the primary mechanism for privileged access — root login is disabled. The sudoers file (/etc/sudoers) is a critical security boundary and must be edited with visudo to prevent syntax errors that could lock you out.
bash
# === /etc/sudoers basics (edit with visudo!) ===

# Simple rules:
kallol ALL=(ALL:ALL) ALL              # kallol can do anything
kallol ALL=(ALL:ALL) NOPASSWD: ALL    # No password prompt (dev VMs only)

# Command-level restrictions:
kallol ALL=(root) /usr/bin/systemctl restart nginx
kallol ALL=(root) /usr/bin/journalctl

# Group-based:
%sudo  ALL=(ALL:ALL) ALL              # sudo group members have full access
%admin ALL=(ALL) ALL                  # admin group

# === sudo command usage ===
sudo -l                                # List allowed commands for current user
sudo -u www-data whoami               # Run as www-data
sudo -s                                # Root shell (not recommended)
sudo -i                                # Root login shell (loads root's profile)
sudo -E                                # Preserve environment variables

# === /etc/sudoers.d/ — Drop-in files ===
# Better than editing /etc/sudoers directly:
# /etc/sudoers.d/deploy
%deploy ALL=(root) /usr/local/bin/deploy.sh

# === Security best practices ===
# 1. Defaults: !root_sudo         — root should use sudo too (for audit trail)
# 2. Defaults: log_output         — log all sudo commands
# 3. Defaults: timestamp_timeout=5  — re-authenticate after 5 min
# 4. Cmnd_Alias for command groups

Cmnd_Alias NETWORKING = /sbin/ip, /sbin/ss, /sbin/tcpdump
Cmnd_Alias SERVICES = /usr/bin/systemctl *
kallol ALL=(root) NETWORKING, SERVICES

PAM: Pluggable Authentication Modules

PAM separates authentication logic from applications. When you SSH in, log in on a TTY, or run sudo, the application uses PAM modules to authenticate. At Enterprise, PAM is configured to enforce multi-factor authentication (MFA), LDAP/SSO integration, and hardware key requirements.
bash
# === PAM configuration files ===
ls /etc/pam.d/
# common-auth        — Authentication methods
# common-account     — Account restrictions (expiry, time)
# common-password    — Password quality rules
# common-session     — Session setup (environment, limits)

# === PAM module types ===
# auth     — Verify identity (password, MFA, SSH key)
# account  — Check account status (expired, locked, time-based)
# password — Update authentication tokens
# session  — Set up environment (open sessions, mount home)

# === Control flags ===
# requisite  — Fail immediately if this module fails
# required   — Fail after all modules run (don't reveal which failed)
# sufficient — If this passes, skip remaining modules
# optional   — Used only if no other module succeeds

# === Example: /etc/pam.d/common-auth ===
auth    [success=1 default=ignore]      pam_unix.so nullok
auth    requisite                       pam_deny.so
auth    required                        pam_permit.so

# === /etc/security/limits.conf (PAM limits) ===
# These apply to PAM sessions (SSH, login, etc.)
kallol soft nofile 65536
kallol hard nofile 65536
@deploy soft nproc 4096

# === Check PAM config ===
sudo pam-auth-update           # Ubuntu: enable/disable modules
pam_tally2 --user kallol       # Show failed login count
sudo pam_tally2 --user kallol --reset  # Reset failed count

Service Accounts & Least Privilege

Every daemon should run as its own dedicated user with minimum required permissions. At Enterprise, 'root in containers' is a common security finding — each service must have a dedicated non-root user. The principle of least privilege (PoLP) means: no process should have access rights beyond what it strictly needs.
bash
# === Creating a service account (best practices) ===
sudo useradd \
    --system \
    --no-create-home \
    --shell /usr/sbin/nologin \
    --user-group \
    myapp

# Verify:
grep myapp /etc/passwd
# myapp:x:998:998::/nonexistent:/usr/sbin/nologin

# === Grant minimum permissions ===
# Give service account access to specific directories
sudo mkdir -p /var/log/myapp /var/lib/myapp /etc/myapp
sudo chown myapp:myapp /var/log/myapp /var/lib/myapp /etc/myapp
sudo chmod 750 /var/log/myapp /var/lib/myapp /etc/myapp

# === Capabilities (fine-grained root privileges) ===
# Instead of running as root, grant specific capabilities:
# CAP_NET_BIND_SERVICE  — bind to ports < 1024
# CAP_SYS_TIME          — change system clock
# CAP_DAC_OVERRIDE      — bypass file permission checks

# In systemd unit:
[Service]
User=myapp
AmbientCapabilities=CAP_NET_BIND_SERVICE
CapabilityBoundingSet=CAP_NET_BIND_SERVICE

# === Docker: running as non-root ===
FROM node:22-alpine
RUN addgroup -S myapp && adduser -S myapp -G myapp
USER myapp
COPY --chown=myapp:myapp . /app

# === /etc/login.defs ===
# Global user creation defaults:
# UID_MIN 1000
# UID_MAX 60000
# SYS_UID_MIN 100
# SYS_UID_MAX 999
# PASS_MAX_DAYS 90

Interview Q&A

Q: 'You SSH into a server and get Permission denied (publickey). What do you check?'

A: 'First, verify my SSH key is in ~/.ssh/authorized_keys on the server with correct permissions (600). Check /etc/ssh/sshd_config: PubkeyAuthentication yes, PasswordAuthentication no (usually). Check ~/.ssh permissions (700) and that the user's home directory is not group-writable. Check /etc/security/access.conf and PAM config in /etc/pam.d/sshd. Also check SELinux/AppArmor status with getenforce. At Enterprise, SSH key rotation is automated, but manual troubleshooting still requires knowing these checks.'

Q: 'What's the difference between su and sudo?'

A: 'su switches to another user completely (requires their password), starting a new shell. sudo runs a single command as another user (requires your password). sudo is preferred because: (1) it creates an audit trail, (2) it grants specific commands, not full access, (3) it doesn't require sharing the root password. At Enterprise, su is effectively banned — all privilege elevation goes through sudo with centralized audit logging.'

Q: 'A user left the company. What steps do you take?'

A: 'Immediately disable their account: sudo usermod -L username (lock password) and remove SSH keys. Revoke sudo access: remove from sudoers. Kill their active sessions: pkill -u username. Archive their home directory for compliance. Remove the account after the compliance retention period. At Enterprise, this is automated in the HR → IT pipeline — when an employee is terminated in Workday, their accounts are deprovisioned within minutes across all systems.'

References & Further Reading

  • passwd(5) man page — Format of /etc/passwd
  • sudoers(5) man page — Complete sudoers syntax reference
  • Arch Linux PAM Wiki — Excellent reference on PAM modules and configuration

Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes
Mistake: Checking /proc/meminfo inside containers for memory
Solution: Use cgroup v2 files: /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current, memory.events

Troubleshooting

Symptom: cat returns unexpected output
Cause: Incorrect arguments, wrong working directory, or stale cached data
Solution: Verify arguments with `man cat`; check current path with `pwd`; clear cache if applicable
Symptom: Configuration changes not taking effect
Cause: Service not reloaded or syntax error in config file
Solution: Validate config syntax with built-in test flag; reload service with `systemctl reload` or `restart`
Symptom: users operation slow or timing out
Cause: Resource exhaustion (disk I/O, memory pressure, or lock contention)
Solution: Check `iostat`, `free -m`, and `top`; look for stuck processes with `ps aux --sort=-%cpu`
Symptom: Permission issues accessing users resources
Cause: Incorrect file ownership or overly restrictive permissions
Solution: Inspect with `ls -la`; fix with `chown`/ `chmod`; check ACLs with `getfacl`

Quick Quiz

Q1: What is User & Group Fundamentals?

A: A standardized directory structure that defines where files and directories should be placed on Linux systems

Q2: What does `cat` do?

A: Lists files and directories in the current or specified path

Q3: Which command would you use to view disk usage of the users directory?

A: du -sh /path/to/directory

Q4: A user reports "Permission denied" when trying to access a users directory. What is the first command you should run?

A: ls -ld /path/to/directory

Q5: In a users scenario, your monitoring alerts show cat failing intermittently. What is the correct debugging workflow?

A: Check logs → verify configuration → test in staging → apply fix → monitor

FAANG Insight

  • FAANG Architect's Note: User management is often overlooked by engineers focused on 'the fun stuff' — distributed systems, ML pipelines, Kubernetes

Production Tips

  • production engineer, you will spend significant time on access control
  • Production Tip: Never, ever disable password expiry for human accounts

Best Practices

  • sudo: Delegated Privilege
  • Service Accounts & Least Privilege

Enterprise Perspective

In enterprise environments, users, groups & authentication is managed through automation (Ansible, Terraform), monitored centrally (Prometheus, Datadog), and governed by compliance policies (CIS benchmarks, SOC2). Changes go through change advisory boards with rollback procedures documented.

Chapter 12

SSH: Secure Shell

intermediateSystems & Operations · 19 min read · 14 min practice

SSH (Secure Shell) is the universal protocol for remote access to Linux systems. Despite the rise of 'no-SSH' architectures (immutable infrastructure, containers, session managers), SSH remains essential for incident response, debugging, and infrastructure management. At Enterprise, SSH is typically gated behind bastion hosts, certificate-based auth, and session recording.

faang-insightLinuxSSHsecuritytunnelingauthentication

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux users, linux networking

Learning Objectives

  • Understand sSH Authentication: Keys, Certificates & Agents
  • Understand sSH Client Configuration (~/.ssh/config)
  • Understand sSH Server Hardening
  • Understand sSH Tunneling & Port Forwarding
  • Understand sSH Troubleshooting & Debugging

Real-Life Analogy: SSH as a Secure Tunnel

The client and server perform a secret handshake (key exchange). Public keys are like padlocks - anyone can snap them on (encrypt), but only the private key holder can unlock (decrypt). The tunnel protects everything inside from eavesdroppers on the public internet.

SSH Authentication: Keys, Certificates & Agents

Password-based SSH access is a security risk and is generally disabled in production. Key-based authentication is the minimum standard. At Enterprise, SSH certificate authorities (CAs) are used — users get short-lived certificates signed by a central CA. The SSH agent handles key forwarding so you don't store private keys on jump boxes.
bash
# === Key generation ===
# ED25519 is the modern default (faster, smaller, more secure than RSA)
ssh-keygen -t ed25519 -C "kallol@company.com"

# RSA with 4096 bits (legacy compatibility)
ssh-keygen -t rsa -b 4096 -C "kallol@company.com"

# === Key files ===
# ~/.ssh/id_ed25519       — Private key (NEVER share, chmod 600)
# ~/.ssh/id_ed25519.pub   — Public key (safe to share)
# ~/.ssh/authorized_keys  — Public keys allowed to log in (on the server)
# ~/.ssh/known_hosts      — Known server host keys (on the client)

# === Copy key to server ===
ssh-copy-id user@server                   # Copy public key to authorized_keys
# Manual equivalent:
cat ~/.ssh/id_ed25519.pub | ssh user@server "mkdir -p ~/.ssh && cat >>
~/.ssh/authorized_keys"

# === SSH Agent (key forwarding) ===
eval "$(ssh-agent -s)"                    # Start agent
ssh-add ~/.ssh/id_ed25519                 # Add private key to agent
ssh-add -l                                 # List loaded keys
ssh-add -L                                 # List loaded public keys

# === SSH Agent Forwarding ===
# Connect to bastion and from there to internal hosts
ssh -A user@bastion                        # -A forwards your agent
# WARNING: agent forwarding is powerful but risky — root on bastion can use your agent
# Better: use ProxyJump (see below)

# === Certificate-based SSH (Enterprise standard) ===
# Server-side: /etc/ssh/ca.pub added to TrustedUserCAKeys
# User gets short-lived cert signed by corporate CA
ssh-keygen -s user_ca -I user@company -n kali -V +52w user.pub
# Result: user-cert.pub (valid for 52 weeks, typically rotated more often)

SSH Client Configuration (~/.ssh/config)

A well-organized SSH config is the hallmark of an experienced engineer. It eliminates repetitive flags, handles jump hosts, and manages keys per host. At Enterprise, your SSH config manages bastion hosts, different keys for different environments, and connection persistence.
bash
# ~/.ssh/config — Highly recommended

# Global defaults
Host *
    ServerAliveInterval 60
    ServerAliveCountMax 3
    StrictHostKeyChecking accept-new
    UserKnownHostsFile ~/.ssh/known_hosts
    AddKeysToAgent yes
    UseKeychain yes                      # macOS: store passphrase in keychain

# Bastion host (jump box)
Host bastion
    HostName bastion.corp.example.com
    User kali
    IdentityFile ~/.ssh/id_ed25519
    ForwardAgent no                     # Safer: don't forward agent on bastion

# Internal hosts (via bastion proxy)
Host bastion-proxy bastion.corp.example.com

Host *.internal.example.com
    User kali
ProxyJump bastion                   # Jump through bastion (no agent forwarding needed)
    IdentityFile ~/.ssh/id_ed25519

# Specific server with different key
Host prod-db-*
    User admin
    Port 2222
    IdentityFile ~/.ssh/prod-key
    ProxyJump bastion

# GitHub / GitLab
Host github.com
    HostName github.com
    User git
    IdentityFile ~/.ssh/id_ed25519
    IdentitiesOnly yes

# === Usage ===
ssh internal-web-01.internal.example.com  # Automatically uses bastion
ssh bastion                               # Direct bastion access

SSH Server Hardening

The SSH daemon (sshd) is the most exposed service on any Linux server. At Enterprise, SSH hardening is mandatory: no passwords, no root login, custom ports are discouraged (security by obscurity is not security), and all sessions are logged.
bash
# /etc/ssh/sshd_config — Security hardening

# Authentication
PubkeyAuthentication yes              # Key-based auth only
PasswordAuthentication no             # Disable passwords (must have keys working
first!)
PermitRootLogin without-password     # Or 'prohibit-password' (disallow root passwords)
# PermitRootLogin no                  # Even better: no root SSH at all

# Keys
AuthorizedKeysFile .ssh/authorized_keys
TrustedUserCAKeys /etc/ssh/ca.pub    # If using SSH CA

# Network
Port 22                               # Using non-standard port just annoys attackers
slightly
ListenAddress 0.0.0.0                # Bind to specific IP if possible

# Session
ClientAliveInterval 300               # 5 min keepalive
ClientAliveCountMax 0                 # Disconnect after 1 missed keepalive
MaxAuthTries 3                        # Limit auth attempts
MaxSessions 10                        # Max concurrent sessions per connection
LoginGraceTime 30                     # 30 seconds to authenticate

# Forwarding
AllowAgentForwarding no               # Disable on bastions (security)
AllowTcpForwarding yes                # Enable only if needed
X11Forwarding no                      # Almost never needed

# Logging
LogLevel VERBOSE                      # Log fingerprints (for audit)

# === After changes ===
sshd -t                               # Validate config before restart
sudo systemctl restart sshd

# === Fail2ban (supplementary protection) ===
sudo apt install fail2ban
# /etc/fail2ban/jail.local
[sshd]
enabled = true
maxretry = 3
bantime = 3600

# === Check current SSH sessions ===
ss -tnp | grep :22                    # Active SSH connections
who                                    # Logged in users
sudo journalctl -u sshd -n 20         # Recent SSH auth logs

SSH Tunneling & Port Forwarding

SSH can tunnel arbitrary TCP connections through encrypted channels. This is essential for accessing internal services (databases, monitoring dashboards) without exposing them to the network. At Enterprise, SSH tunnels are a common pattern for 'break glass' database access.
bash
# === Local Port Forwarding ===
# Access internal service via local port
# Syntax: ssh -L local_port:target_host:target_port bastion
ssh -L 5432:internal-db.internal:5432 bastion
# Now connect to localhost:5432 → encrypted → internal-db:5432

# === Remote Port Forwarding ===
# Expose local port on remote server (reverse tunnel)
# Syntax: ssh -R remote_port:local_host:local_port bastion
ssh -R 8080:localhost:3000 bastion
# Now bastion:8080 → encrypted → localhost:3000

# === Dynamic Port Forwarding (SOCKS proxy) ===
# Create a SOCKS5 proxy through the SSH connection
ssh -D 1080 bastion
# Configure browser/system SOCKS proxy to localhost:1080
# Now all traffic routes through the bastion

# === Jump host (ProxyJump) ===
# Newer, simpler alternative to chaining SSH tunnels
ssh -J bastion internal-host

# === SSH through multiple hops ===
ssh -J bastion,internal-bastion target-host

# === Common tunnel patterns ===
# Database access via bastion
ssh -L 3306:prod-db-1.internal:3306 -N bastion &
# -N = don't open a shell, just forward ports

# RDP to Windows jump box behind bastion
ssh -L 3389:windows-jump.internal:3389 bastion

# === Auto-reconnecting tunnel (autossh) ===
autossh -M 0 -o "ServerAliveInterval 60" -o "ServerAliveCountMax 3" -L 5432:db:5432
bastion

SSH Troubleshooting & Debugging

When SSH doesn't work, the default error messages can be cryptic. Verbose mode (-vvv) is your primary diagnostic tool. At Enterprise, SSH issues are a common on-call triage category — knowing how to diagnose quickly is essential.
bash
# === Verbose mode ===
ssh -vvv user@server                  # Detailed debug output

# Common issues by verbose output analysis:
# 1. "Permission denied (publickey)" — No matching key in authorized_keys
# 2. "Connection refused" — sshd not running or port wrong
# 3. "Connection timed out" — Firewall blocking (check security groups)
# 4. "Host key verification failed" — Host key changed (MITM or server rebuild)
#    Fix: ssh-keygen -R "hostname" then reconnect
# 5. "Too many authentication failures" — ssh is trying too many keys
#    Fix: ssh -o IdentitiesOnly=yes -i specific_key user@host

# === Test connectivity ===
nc -zv server 22                      # Check if port 22 is open
telnet server 22                      # Raw connection test

# === Check host keys ===
ssh-keyscan server                    # Get server's public host key
ssh-keygen -lf /etc/ssh/ssh_host_ed25519_key.pub  # Server host key fingerprint

# === Check which key is being offered ===
ssh -v user@server 2>&1 | grep "Offering public key"

# === Known hosts issues ===
cat ~/.ssh/known_hosts                # List known hosts
ssh-keygen -F server                  # Search known_hosts for a host
ssh-keygen -R server                  # Remove host from known_hosts

# === Agent issues ===
ssh-add -l                            # Check which keys are loaded
ssh-add -D                            # Clear all keys
ssh -o IdentitiesOnly=yes -i ~/.ssh/custom_key user@host  # Force specific key

# === SCP & SFTP ===
scp file.txt user@server:/remote/path/
scp -r dir/ user@server:/remote/path/
rsync -avz -e ssh dir/ user@server:/remote/path/  # Better for large transfers
sftp user@server                     # Interactive file transfer

Interview Q&A

Q: 'How would you design SSH access for a fleet of 10,000 servers?'

A: 'No direct SSH. All access goes through bastion hosts. Bastions are locked down — SSH only from corporate VPN, MFA required, no agent forwarding. Users authenticate with SSH certificates signed by a central CA — certificates expire after 4-8 hours. All sessions are recorded (script, tlog). Host keys are distributed via configuration management (Ansible/Chef). Servers have no individual user accounts — engineers authenticate via their certificate, which maps to a local account via AuthorizedPrincipalsFile. This gives us audit trails, revocability, and no key distribution problem.'

Q: 'You can't SSH into a production server. What do you check?'

A: 'Layer by layer: (1) Network: can I ping it? nc -zv host 22? Check security groups/firewall rules. (2) Service: is sshd running? Check via monitoring or console access. (3) Authentication: am I using the right key? ssh -vvv. Check my certificate hasn't expired. (4) Server config: is sshd_config correct? sshd -t remotely via console. (5) PAM/SELinux: is SELinux blocking? ausearch -m avc -ts recent. At Enterprise, 'console access' means AWS Systems Manager Session Manager or the equivalent — we always have a backup access method.'

Q: 'What's the difference between SSH tunneling and a VPN?'

A: 'An SSH tunnel is a single TCP port forwarded through an encrypted channel — it's application-specific. A VPN creates a virtual network interface and routes all (or selected) traffic through an encrypted tunnel — it's network-level. SSH tunneling is simpler and doesn't require admin privileges (just SSH access), but it only works for TCP and requires manual setup per port. VPNs are transparent to applications but need client software and more infrastructure. At Enterprise, both are used: VPN for general corporate access, SSH tunnels for granular 'break glass' access to specific services.'

References & Further Reading

  • SSH Protocol Details — Comprehensive guide to the SSH protocol
  • ssh_config(5) man page — All SSH client options
  • SSH Agent Explained — Deep dive into SSH agent internals

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes
Mistake: Checking /proc/meminfo inside containers for memory
Solution: Use cgroup v2 files: /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current, memory.events
