FAANG Insight

  • FAANG Architect's Note: The 'no SSH' trend in infrastructure (immutable servers, containers, 'cattle not pets') is real — at FAANG, the vast majority of interactions with servers are automated through deployment pipelines, orchestration systems, and API-driven management
  • FAANG is that SSH access is audited, limited, and temporary — but it still exists, and knowing how to use it effectively is a survival skill

Production Tips

  • Production Tip: Always test SSH config changes with a second session before closing the first
  • production outage scenario: an engineer edits sshd_config, restarts sshd, and the new config has a syntax error that kills all active sessions — locking everyone out

Best Practices

  • Use version control for all configuration (/etc, scripts, Dockerfiles)
  • Implement infrastructure as code; avoid manual server changes
  • Test changes in staging before production; have rollback plans

Enterprise Perspective

In enterprise environments, ssh: secure shell is managed through automation (Ansible, Terraform), monitored centrally (Prometheus, Datadog), and governed by compliance policies (CIS benchmarks, SOC2). Changes go through change advisory boards with rollback procedures documented.

Chapter 13

Linux Networking

intermediateSystems & Operations · 20 min read · 15 min practice

Linux networking is a vast topic that every infrastructure engineer must understand. From interface configuration to packet filtering, DNS resolution to socket debugging, the tools and concepts covered here are used daily at Enterprise — whether you're debugging a production incident, tuning a load balancer, or designing a VPC topology.

faang-insightLinuxnetworkingtcpdumpDNSiptablestroubleshooting

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux cli basics, linux processes

Learning Objectives

  • Understand network Interfaces & Configuration
  • Understand socket Diagnostics: ss, netstat & lsof
  • Understand dNS Resolution on Linux
  • Understand packet Filtering: iptables & nftables
  • Understand packet Capture: tcpdump & wireshark

Real-Life Analogy: Networking as a Postal System

IP addresses are street addresses. Ports are apartment numbers. DNS is the phone book translating names to addresses. Routes are the roads. Firewalls (iptables/nftables) are security checkpoints. TCP is registered mail (guaranteed delivery). UDP is a postcard (fire and forget).

Network Interfaces & Configuration

Every Linux machine has at least one network interface. Modern Linux uses ip (from iproute2) instead of the deprecated ifconfig and route. At Enterprise, server networking is configured via netplan, cloud-init, or networkd — never by manually editing /etc/network/interfaces on ephemeral instances. Understanding interfaces, addresses, and routing is the foundation.
bash
# === Modern: ip commands (iproute2) ===
ip addr                              # All interfaces + IPs (equivalent to ifconfig)
ip addr show eth0                    # Single interface
ip -br addr                          # Brief output (compact)
ip link                              # Interface status (up/down, MAC, MTU)
ip route                             # Routing table
ip route show default                # Default gateway
ip neigh                             # ARP table (neighbor cache)

# === Interface management ===
sudo ip link set eth0 up             # Bring interface up
sudo ip link set eth0 down           # Bring interface down
sudo ip addr add 10.0.0.5/24 dev eth0   # Add IP address
sudo ip addr del 10.0.0.5/24 dev eth0   # Remove IP address
sudo ip route add default via 10.0.0.1  # Set default gateway
sudo ip route add 192.168.0.0/16 via 10.0.0.1  # Add static route

# === Legacy ===
ifconfig                             # Interface info (deprecated, still present)
route -n                             # Routing table (deprecated)
netstat -rn                          # Routing table (deprecated)
arp -a                               # ARP table (deprecated)

# === Persistent config (Ubuntu: netplan) ===
# /etc/netplan/00-installer-config.yaml
network:
  version: 2
  ethernets:
    eth0:
      addresses:
        - 10.0.0.5/24
      routes:
        - to: default
          via: 10.0.0.1
      nameservers:
        addresses: [8.8.8.8, 1.1.1.1]

# Apply:
sudo netplan apply                    # Apply netplan config

# === MTU (Maximum Transmission Unit) ===
# Standard: 1500 bytes. Jumbo frames: 9000 bytes.
# Check: ip link | grep mtu
# Set: sudo ip link set eth0 mtu 9000
# At Enterprise, internal networks often use jumbo frames for storage traffic.

Socket Diagnostics: ss, netstat & lsof

Understanding what ports are listening and what connections are active is essential for debugging. ss (socket statistics) is the modern replacement for netstat — it's faster and shows more information. At Enterprise, these commands are used constantly: 'Is my service listening?', 'Who's connected?', 'Is there a port conflict?'
bash
# === ss (Socket Statistics) ===
ss -tlnp                             # TCP listening sockets (with process names)
ss -ulnp                             # UDP listening
ss -tanp                             # All TCP connections (including established)
ss -tup                              # All TCP + UDP with processes
ss -s                                # Socket summary (counts by state)
ss -tan state established            # Only established connections
ss -4                                # Only IPv4
ss -6                                # Only IPv6

# === Key fields ===
# Recv-Q, Send-Q  — Queue sizes (non-zero indicates backlog)
# Local Address:Port  — What the socket is bound to
# Peer Address:Port  — Who's connected

# === netstat (legacy, often requires apt install net-tools) ===
netstat -tlnp                         # TCP listening
netstat -tanp                         # All TCP
netstat -i                            # Interface statistics
netstat -s                            # Protocol statistics

# === lsof (list open files) ===
sudo lsof -i :80                      # What's listening on port 80?
sudo lsof -i :443
sudo lsof -p <PID>                    # Open files/sockets for a process
lsof -i -P -n | grep LISTEN           # All listening processes

# === Socket states ===
# LISTEN       — Waiting for connection
# ESTABLISHED  — Active connection
# TIME_WAIT    — Connection closing (waiting for remaining packets)
# CLOSE_WAIT   — Remote closed; local hasn't closed yet
# SYN_SENT     — Trying to connect
# FIN_WAIT1/2  — Closing sequence in progress

# === Common debugging ===
ss -tanp | grep -c ESTABLISHED       # Count active connections
ss -tanp | grep -c TIME_WAIT          # Count TIME_WAIT (high is normal for proxies)
ss -tanp | head -20                   # Check for weird connections

DNS Resolution on Linux

DNS is how hostnames become IP addresses. Linux uses /etc/resolv.conf for nameserver configuration, /etc/hosts for local overrides, and getent hosts for queries. At Enterprise, internal DNS is critical — services are discovered by name, and DNS failures cause cascading outages.
bash
# === Name resolution order (nsswitch.conf) ===
grep hosts /etc/nsswitch.conf
# hosts: files dns
# This means: check /etc/hosts first, then DNS

# === /etc/resolv.conf ===
cat /etc/resolv.conf
# nameserver 127.0.0.53     # systemd-resolved stub
# nameserver 8.8.8.8
# search internal.example.com  # Auto-append domain for short names
# options rotate         # Round-robin between nameservers
# options timeout:2      # 2-second timeout per query
# options attempts:3     # Retry 3 times before failing

# === DNS queries ===
getent hosts google.com              # System resolver (uses nsswitch)
host google.com                      # Simple DNS lookup
nslookup google.com                  # Legacy query tool
dig google.com                       # Detailed DNS query (best for debugging)

# === dig examples ===
dig google.com                       # A record (IPv4)
dig google.com AAAA                  # IPv6 record
dig google.com MX                    # Mail exchange record
dig google.com NS                    # Nameserver records
dig google.com CNAME                 # Canonical name (alias)
dig -x 8.8.8.8                      # Reverse lookup (PTR)
dig @8.8.8.8 google.com             # Query specific nameserver

# === /etc/hosts ===
cat /etc/hosts
# 127.0.0.1 localhost
# ::1       localhost
# 127.0.0.1 dev-server.internal    # Local override for development

# === systemd-resolved ===
resolvectl status                     # DNS config via systemd-resolved
resolvectl query google.com           # Query via resolved

# === Check which service is using DNS ===
sudo ss -tlnp | grep 53              # DNS listener (systemd-resolved or dnsmasq)

# === DNS troubleshooting ===
dig +trace google.com                 # Trace full resolution path
dig google.com +short                # Just the IP
whois 8.8.8.8                        # IP ownership info

Packet Filtering: iptables & nftables

Linux has built-in packet filtering via Netfilter. iptables is the legacy interface; nftables is the modern replacement. At Enterprise, cloud security groups handle most firewall rules at the hypervisor level, but host-level iptables/nftables rules are still used for node-local policies, traffic shaping, and Kubernetes network policies.
bash
# === iptables (legacy, still widely used) ===

# List rules by chain
sudo iptables -L                        # Filter table (INPUT, FORWARD, OUTPUT)
sudo iptables -t nat -L                 # NAT table (PREROUTING, POSTROUTING)
sudo iptables -L -n -v                  # With packet/byte counters

# === Common patterns ===
# Allow SSH
sudo iptables -A INPUT -p tcp --dport 22 -j ACCEPT

# Allow established connections
sudo iptables -A INPUT -m state --state ESTABLISHED,RELATED -j ACCEPT

# Drop all other inbound
sudo iptables -P INPUT DROP
sudo iptables -P FORWARD DROP
sudo iptables -P OUTPUT ACCEPT

# Port forwarding (NAT)
sudo iptables -t nat -A PREROUTING -p tcp --dport 80 -j REDIRECT --to-port 8080

# Rate limiting (anti-DoS)
sudo iptables -A INPUT -p tcp --dport 80 -m limit --limit 100/minute -j ACCEPT
sudo iptables -A INPUT -p tcp --dport 80 -j DROP

# === nftables (modern) ===
# /etc/nftables.conf
#!/usr/sbin/nft -f

table inet filter {
    chain input {
        type filter hook input priority 0;
        policy drop;

        ct state established,related accept
        iif lo accept
        tcp dport 22 accept
        tcp dport {80,443} accept
        ip protocol icmp accept
    }
    chain forward {
        type filter hook forward priority 0;
        policy drop;
    }
    chain output {
        type filter hook output priority 0;
        policy accept;
    }
}

# nft commands
sudo nft list ruleset                  # Show all rules
sudo nft add rule inet filter input tcp dport 8080 accept
sudo nft flush ruleset                 # Clear all rules

# === Save/restore ===
sudo iptables-save > /etc/iptables/rules.v4   # Save iptables rules
sudo iptables-restore < /etc/iptables/rules.v4
sudo nft list ruleset > /etc/nftables.conf     # Save nftables rules

Packet Capture: tcpdump & wireshark

When you need to see exactly what's on the wire, packet capture is the ultimate debugging tool. tcpdump is the CLI standard — it's installed on virtually every Linux system. At Enterprise, tcpdump is used for: verifying TLS handshakes, debugging connection resets, analyzing protocol behavior, and security incident investigation.
bash
# === Basic tcpdump ===
sudo tcpdump -i eth0                  # Capture all traffic on interface
sudo tcpdump -i any                   # Capture on all interfaces
sudo tcpdump -n                       # Don't resolve hostnames (faster, safer)
sudo tcpdump -nn                      # Don't resolve ports either

# === Expresssions (filters) ===
sudo tcpdump -n host 10.0.0.1         # Traffic to/from a specific IP
sudo tcpdump -n src 10.0.0.1         # Source IP
sudo tcpdump -n dst 10.0.0.1         # Destination IP
sudo tcpdump -n port 80              # Specific port
sudo tcpdump -n port 80 or port 443  # Multiple ports
sudo tcpdump -n tcp                  # Only TCP
sudo tcpdump -n udp                  # Only UDP
sudo tcpdump -n icmp                 # Only ICMP (ping)

# === Output control ===
sudo tcpdump -c 100                   # Stop after 100 packets
sudo tcpdump -s 0                     # Capture full packet (default: 68 bytes snaplen)
sudo tcpdump -w capture.pcap          # Save to file (for Wireshark analysis)
sudo tcpdump -r capture.pcap          # Read from file

# === Advanced ===
sudo tcpdump -n 'tcp[tcpflags] & tcp-syn != 0 and tcp[tcpflags] & tcp-ack == 0'  # SYN
packets only
sudo tcpdump -n 'tcp port 443 and (tcp[13] & 0x02 != 0)'  # HTTPS SYN packets

# === One-liner for debugging ===
sudo tcpdump -ni any port 80 -c 100 | grep -i "HTTP/"  # Show HTTP requests

# === tcpdump + Wireshark ===
# Capture on server, analyze on laptop:
# Server: sudo tcpdump -w capture.pcap -i eth0
# Download: scp user@server:capture.pcap .
# Analyze: open in Wireshark

# === Other network debug tools ===
ping -c 5 google.com                  # Basic connectivity test
mtr google.com                        # traceroute + ping in real time (my traceroute)
traceroute google.com                 # Show route path
tracepath google.com                  # Similar, no root needed
curl -v http://example.com            # HTTP debug (verbose headers)
wget --debug http://example.com       # HTTP debug

# === Bandwidth testing ===
iperf3 -c server                      # Network throughput test
iperf3 -s                             # Server mode
nc -vz host 22                        # TCP port connectivity test (netcat)

Interview Q&A

Q: 'A user reports that 'network is slow.' How do you diagnose?'

A: 'Start broad, then narrow. Check ping for latency and packet loss. Check mtr for the path — is the bottleneck at a specific hop? Check ss -s for socket stats — are there many retransmits or TIME_WAIT connections? Check ip -s link for interface errors (dropped packets, overruns). Check sar -n DEV 1 for bandwidth utilization. If it's an application issue, use tcpdump to analyze the specific traffic. At Enterprise, 'slow network' is almost never the network — it's usually application-level problems (slow queries, insufficient connections, serialization bottlenecks) or DNS issues.'

Q: 'What happens when you type google.com in a browser on Linux?'

A: '(1) Browser checks its cache, then OS DNS cache (systemd-resolved/nscd). (2) /etc/nsswitch.conf says 'files dns' — check /etc/hosts first (no match). (3) Query sent to nameserver in /etc/resolv.conf. (4) If nameserver is 127.0.0.53 (systemd-resolved stub), resolved handles caching and forwarding. (5) DNS resolution returns an IP (e.g., 142.250.80.4). (6) SYN → SYN-ACK → ACK (three-way handshake via connect() syscall). (7) TLS handshake. (8) HTTP request. This is a classic interview question — the depth of your answer shows your understanding of the stack.'

Q: 'What's the difference between iptables and security groups?'

A: 'Security groups are a cloud hypervisor-level firewall — traffic is filtered before it reaches the instance. iptables runs on the instance itself. Security groups are stateless (for Network ACLs) or stateful (for Security Groups in AWS), while iptables is stateful by default with conntrack. At Enterprise, we use security groups for broad traffic policies (e.g., 'only allow HTTP from load balancers') and iptables for fine-grained host-level policies (e.g., 'only allow this specific user to connect to this port'). They complement each other — defense in depth.'

References & Further Reading

  • ip(8) man page — Complete iproute2 reference
  • Arch Linux Network Configuration — Comprehensive networking guide
  • tcpdump man page — Official tcpdump documentation

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes
Mistake: Checking /proc/meminfo inside containers for memory
Solution: Use cgroup v2 files: /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current, memory.events

Practice Labs

Run `ip` and examine the output. Note the structure, key fields, and patterns you observe. Try different flags to see how the output changes.

Explore: Socket Diagnostics: ss, netstat & lsof

Run `sudo` and examine the output. Note the structure, key fields, and patterns you observe. Try different flags to see how the output changes.

Run `ifconfig` and examine the output. Note the structure, key fields, and patterns you observe. Try different flags to see how the output changes.

Quick Reference

| Command | Description | |---|---| | `ip` | Network Interfaces & Configuration | | `sudo` | Network Interfaces & Configuration | | `ifconfig` | Network Interfaces & Configuration | | `ss` | Socket Diagnostics: ss, netstat & lsof | | `netstat` | Socket Diagnostics: ss, netstat & lsof | | `sudo` | Socket Diagnostics: ss, netstat & lsof | | `grep` | DNS Resolution on Linux | | `cat` | DNS Resolution on Linux | | `getent` | DNS Resolution on Linux | | `sudo` | Packet Filtering: iptables & nftables | | `table` | Packet Filtering: iptables & nftables | | `chain` | Packet Filtering: iptables & nftables | | `sudo` | Packet Capture: tcpdump & wireshark | | `ping` | Packet Capture: tcpdump & wireshark | | `mtr` | Packet Capture: tcpdump & wireshark |

Troubleshooting

Symptom: ip returns unexpected output
Cause: Incorrect arguments, wrong working directory, or stale cached data
Solution: Verify arguments with `man ip`; check current path with `pwd`; clear cache if applicable
Symptom: Configuration changes not taking effect
Cause: Service not reloaded or syntax error in config file
Solution: Validate config syntax with built-in test flag; reload service with `systemctl reload` or `restart`
Symptom: networking operation slow or timing out
Cause: Resource exhaustion (disk I/O, memory pressure, or lock contention)
Solution: Check `iostat`, `free -m`, and `top`; look for stuck processes with `ps aux --sort=-%cpu`
Symptom: Permission issues accessing networking resources
Cause: Incorrect file ownership or overly restrictive permissions
Solution: Inspect with `ls -la`; fix with `chown`/ `chmod`; check ACLs with `getfacl`

Quick Quiz

Q1: What is Network Interfaces & Configuration?

A: A standardized directory structure that defines where files and directories should be placed on Linux systems

Q2: What does `ip` do?

A: Lists files and directories in the current or specified path

Q3: Which command would you use to view disk usage of the networking directory?

A: du -sh /path/to/directory

Q4: A user reports "Permission denied" when trying to access a networking directory. What is the first command you should run?

A: ls -ld /path/to/directory

Q5: In a networking scenario, your monitoring alerts show ip failing intermittently. What is the correct debugging workflow?

A: Check logs → verify configuration → test in staging → apply fix → monitor

FAANG Insight

  • FAANG Architect's Note: Networking is the most common 'unknown unknown' for engineers moving to infrastructure roles

Production Tips

  • production
  • Production Tip: Always run ss -tlnp (not netstat) for socket debugging

Best Practices

  • Use version control for all configuration (/etc, scripts, Dockerfiles)
  • Implement infrastructure as code; avoid manual server changes
  • Test changes in staging before production; have rollback plans

Enterprise Perspective

In enterprise environments, linux networking is managed through automation (Ansible, Terraform), monitored centrally (Prometheus, Datadog), and governed by compliance policies (CIS benchmarks, SOC2). Changes go through change advisory boards with rollback procedures documented.

Chapter 14

Storage, Filesystems & Disk Management

beginnerSystems & Operations · 20 min read · 15 min practice

Storage on Linux spans a hierarchy: physical disks → partitions → LVM (logical volumes) → filesystems → mount points. At Enterprise, storage is abstracted through multiple layers: local NVMe SSD, EBS volumes, network filesystems (NFS/EFS), and distributed filesystems (Ceph, JuiceFS). Understanding the Linux storage stack is essential for capacity planning, performance tuning, and data integrity.

faang-insightLinuxstoragefilesystemLVMdisk managementmonitoring

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux filesystem, linux permissions

Learning Objectives

  • Understand disk Discovery & Partitioning
  • Understand filesystems: ext4, XFS & Modern Options
  • Understand mounting & /etc/fstab
  • Understand lVM: Logical Volume Manager
  • Understand monitoring: I/O, Space & Inodes

Real-Life Analogy: Storage as a Filing Cabinet

Block devices are empty drawers. Filesystems (ext4, XFS) are the filing system - they organize how papers are stored. LVM adds flexible drawers that can grow. Mount points are labels on the drawer fronts telling you what is inside. RAID is keeping duplicate copies in multiple cabinets.

Disk Discovery & Partitioning

The first step in storage management is discovering what disks are available. Modern Linux uses lsblk and fdisk/parted for partitioning. At Enterprise, disks are typically provisioned via cloud APIs (EBS volumes, persistent disks) and formatted automatically by init scripts — manual partitioning is rare but understanding it is expected for system-level roles.
bash
# === Disk discovery ===
lsblk                                # Tree view of all block devices
lsblk -f                             # Include filesystem info (FSTYPE, UUID, LABEL)
lsblk -o NAME,SIZE,TYPE,MOUNTPOINT,FSTYPE,UUID  # Custom columns

sudo fdisk -l                        # Detailed partition table
sudo parted -l                       # Alternative partition tool

# Device naming:
# /dev/sda, /dev/sdb, ...  — SCSI/SATA disks (also virtio-blk, EBS)
# /dev/nvme0n1, /dev/nvme1n1, ... — NVMe SSDs
# /dev/vda, /dev/vdb, ...  — VirtIO disks (KVM)
# /dev/mapper/*            — LVM logical volumes

# === Partitioning with parted (GPT) ===
sudo parted /dev/sdb mklabel gpt                      # Create GPT label
sudo parted /dev/sdb mkpart primary ext4 0% 100%      # Use entire disk
sudo parted /dev/sdb mkpart primary ext4 1MiB 10GiB   # Specific size
sudo parted /dev/sdb print                            # Show partition table

# === Partitioning with fdisk (MBR) ===
sudo fdisk /dev/sdb                                   # Interactive
# n → new partition, p → primary, w → write

# === Partition tables: MBR vs GPT ===
# MBR (DOS): max 2TB, 4 primary partitions
# GPT:       up to 8ZiB, 128 partitions, redundant header
# Most modern systems use GPT

# === Wipe a disk ===
sudo wipefs -a /dev/sdb              # Wipe all filesystem signatures
sudo dd if=/dev/zero of=/dev/sdb bs=1M count=100  # Overwrite first 100MB

# === Kernel re-scan ===
sudo partprobe                        # Inform kernel of partition table changes
echo 1 | sudo tee /sys/block/sdb/device/rescan

Filesystems: ext4, XFS & Modern Options

The filesystem is the layer that organizes data on disk. ext4 is the default on Ubuntu/Debian — reliable and well-tested. XFS is the default on RHEL/Amazon Linux — excellent for large files and parallel I/O. At Enterprise, the choice of filesystem depends on workload: XFS for large distributed databases, ext4 for general-purpose, and specialised FS for specific use cases (ZFS for data integrity, Btrfs for snapshots).
bash
# === Creating filesystems ===
sudo mkfs.ext4 /dev/sdb1              # Format as ext4
sudo mkfs.ext4 -L mydata /dev/sdb1    # With label
sudo mkfs.xfs /dev/sdb1               # Format as XFS
sudo mkfs.xfs -L mydata /dev/sdb1     # XFS with label
sudo mkfs.btrfs /dev/sdb1             # Btrfs

# === ext4 tuning ===
sudo mkfs.ext4 -m 0 /dev/sdb1         # 0% reserved for root (was 5%, good for data
volumes)
sudo mkfs.ext4 -E lazy_itable_init=0 /dev/sdb1  # Initialize inode table (faster first
mount? Actually slower now, faster later)
# tune2fs: tune an existing ext2/3/4 filesystem
sudo tune2fs -l /dev/sdb1             # List filesystem features
sudo tune2fs -m 1 /dev/sdb1           # Change reserved block percentage

# === XFS tuning ===
# XFS is optimized for large files and parallel I/O
# Key mkfs.xfs options:
# -d agcount=4     — Allocation group count (more = better parallelism)
# -f               — Force (overwrite existing FS)
# -m crc=1         — Enable metadata CRC (default since RHEL 7)

# === Filesystem features comparison ===
# Feature           ext4            XFS             Btrfs
# Max file size     16TB            8EB             16EB
# Max volume size   1EB             8EB             16EB
# Default fsck      e2fsck          xfs_repair      btrfs check
# Snapshots         no              no              yes
# Compression       no              no              yes
# Online defrag     no              yes (xfs_fsr)   yes

# === Check filesystem ===
sudo fsck /dev/sdb1                  # Check and repair (must be unmounted!)
sudo fsck.ext4 -f /dev/sdb1          # Force check even if clean
sudo xfs_repair /dev/sdb1            # XFS repair (unmounted)
sudo xfs_check /dev/sdb1             # XFS check (unmounted)

Mounting & /etc/fstab

Mounting attaches a filesystem to a directory. The /etc/fstab file defines persistent mounts that are automatically mounted at boot. A misconfigured fstab can prevent a server from booting — always test mount options and keep a backup.
bash
# === Mount basics ===
sudo mount /dev/sdb1 /mnt/data       # Mount device to directory
sudo umount /mnt/data                # Unmount
sudo mount -a                        # Mount everything in /etc/fstab

# === /etc/fstab format ===
# <device>    <mountpoint>    <fstype>    <options>    <dump>    <pass>

# Examples:
UUID=abc123-...  /                 ext4    defaults,errors=remount-ro  0       1
UUID=def456-...  /boot             ext4    defaults                   0       2
UUID=ghi789-...  /mnt/data        ext4    defaults,noatime           0       2

# /dev/sdb1      /mnt/data        ext4    defaults,nofail             0       2
# ^ nofail means: if this fails to mount, still boot

# === Common mount options ===
# defaults       — rw, suid, dev, exec, auto, nouser, async
# noatime        — Don't update access time (big performance gain)
# nodiratime     — Don't update directory access times
# noexec         — Don't allow executables on this filesystem
# nosuid         — Ignore SUID bits
# ro             — Read-only
# nofail         — Don't fail boot if mount fails (important for removable/optional
volumes)
# discard        — Enable TRIM for SSDs (or better: periodic fstrim)

# === Bind mounts ===
# Mount a directory to another location
mount --bind /var/log /mnt/log-backup
# /etc/fstab:
# /var/log  /mnt/log-backup  none   bind   0   0

# === tmpfs (RAM-backed filesystem) ===
mount -t tmpfs -o size=1G tmpfs /mnt/ramdisk
# /etc/fstab:
# tmpfs  /mnt/ramdisk  tmpfs  size=1G  0  0

# === Find by UUID ===
blkid                                # List all UUIDs and LABELs
sudo mount UUID="abc123" /mnt/data   # Mount by UUID (preferred, immune to device name
changes)

# === Check mounts ===
mount                                # List all mounted filesystems
df -hT                               # Disk free, human-readable + type
findmnt                              # Tree view of mounts

LVM: Logical Volume Manager

LVM provides a flexible abstraction layer between physical disks and filesystems. With LVM, you can resize volumes without repartitioning, take snapshots for backups, and span volumes across multiple disks. At Enterprise, LVM is used on on-premise servers but cloud instances typically use cloud block storage directly (EBS volumes) — though understanding LVM is still expected for infrastructure roles.
bash
# === LVM Architecture ===
# Physical Volumes (PVs) → Volume Group (VG) → Logical Volumes (LVs)

# === Create LVM ===
# 1. Mark disk as Physical Volume
sudo pvcreate /dev/sdb /dev/sdc

# 2. Create Volume Group
sudo vgcreate vg_data /dev/sdb /dev/sdc

# 3. Create Logical Volume
sudo lvcreate -L 100G -n lv_data vg_data
sudo lvcreate -l 100%FREE -n lv_data vg_data     # Use all free space

# 4. Format and mount
sudo mkfs.ext4 /dev/vg_data/lv_data
sudo mkdir /mnt/data
sudo mount /dev/vg_data/lv_data /mnt/data

# === LVM commands ===
pvs                                    # List physical volumes
pvs -o+pv_used                         # With usage info
vgs                                    # List volume groups
vgs -o+vg_free                         # With free space
lvs                                    # List logical volumes
lvdisplay                              # Detailed LV info

# === Resize LVM ===
# Extend PV (if underlying disk grew)
sudo pvresize /dev/sdb

# Extend VG (add new disk)
sudo vgextend vg_data /dev/sdd

# Extend LV (then resize filesystem)
sudo lvextend -L +50G /dev/vg_data/lv_data
sudo lvextend -l +100%FREE /dev/vg_data/lv_data

# Resize filesystem (ext4)
sudo resize2fs /dev/vg_data/lv_data

# Resize filesystem (xfs)
sudo xfs_growfs /mnt/data               # XFS grows online (must be mounted)

# === LVM Snapshots ===
# Create a snapshot (for backup consistency)
sudo lvcreate -L 10G -s -n lv_data_snap /dev/vg_data/lv_data
# Mount snapshot read-only:
sudo mount -o ro /dev/vg_data/lv_data_snap /mnt/backup
# Remove snapshot:
sudo lvremove /dev/vg_data/lv_data_snap

# === Remove LVM ===
sudo umount /mnt/data
sudo lvremove /dev/vg_data/lv_data
sudo vgremove vg_data
sudo pvremove /dev/sdb /dev/sdc

Monitoring: I/O, Space & Inodes

Storage monitoring is critical for production reliability. Out of disk space is the #1 cause of predictable production incidents — it's almost always a log file or database that wasn't rotated. At Enterprise, disk monitoring is built into the observability stack, but you still need to know the command-line tools for ad-hoc debugging.
bash
# === Disk space ===
df -h                                # Human-readable disk usage
df -hT                               # With filesystem type
df -i                                # Inode usage (filesystem can be full even with
free space!)

# === Directory size ===
du -sh /var/log                      # Total size of directory
du -sh /*                            # Size of top-level directories
du -sh /var/log/* | sort -rh | head -10  # Largest items in /var/log

# === I/O performance ===
iostat -x 1                          # I/O stats by device (1s intervals)
# Key fields:
# %util      — % of time device was busy (warning at >80%)
# await      — Average I/O latency (ms)
# r/s, w/s   — Read/write IOPS
# rkB/s, wkB/s — Throughput

iostat -x 1 5                        # 5 samples, 1 second apart

# === I/O top (per-process) ===
iotop -oP                            # Show only processes doing I/O

# === Find large files ===
find /var/log -type f -size +100M    # Files > 100MB
find / -xdev -type f -size +1G -exec ls -lh {} \;  # Files > 1GB on root FS

# === Find and remove old logs ===
find /var/log -name "*.log" -mtime +30 -delete  # Delete logs > 30 days

# === TRIM for SSDs ===
sudo fstrim -v /                     # Manually TRIM a filesystem
sudo fstrim -av                      # TRIM all mounted FS that support it
# Systemd timer: systemctl status fstrim.timer

# === SMART disk health ===
sudo apt install smartmontools
sudo smartctl -H /dev/sda            # Overall health status
sudo smartctl -a /dev/sda            # Full SMART data
sudo smartctl -t short /dev/sda      # Run short self-test

# === dd benchmark ===
dd if=/dev/zero of=/tmp/test bs=1M count=1000 oflag=direct  # Write test
dd if=/tmp/test of=/dev/null bs=1M count=1000 iflag=direct   # Read test
# Replace with fio for production-grade benchmarks

# === fio (Flexible I/O Tester) ===
sudo apt install fio
fio --randrepeat=1 --ioengine=libaio --direct=1 --gtod_reduce=1 \
   --name=test --bs=4k --iodepth=64 --size=1G --readwrite=randread \
   --filename=/tmp/fio-test

Interview Q&A

Q: 'The database server has no space, but df shows 50% free. What's wrong?'

A: 'Check df -i — the filesystem may be out of inodes, not space. This happens when millions of tiny files (like Docker overlay layers, mail spool files, or a runaway process writing temp files) exhaust the inode table. Inode count is set at filesystem creation and can't be changed without reformatting. The fix: find and remove the unnecessary small files, or create a new filesystem with more inodes using mkfs.ext4 -N <count>.'

Q: 'How do you choose between ext4 and XFS for a new filesystem?'

A: 'It depends on the workload. For general-purpose, ext4 is fine. For large files (>2TB), high concurrency of parallel I/O, or workloads with many large files (media, logs, databases), XFS performs better — especially because it supports online defragmentation (xfs_fsr) and has better allocation groups for parallelism. For metadata-heavy workloads (many small files, like a mail server or git repository), ext4 is typically better. At Enterprise, the standard is often XFS on RHEL/Amazon Linux for production and ext4 on Ubuntu for development.'

Q: 'You receive an alert: EBS volume is at 95% capacity. Walk through your response.'

A: 'First, acknowledge the alert. SSH into the instance (or use session manager). Run df -h to confirm which volume. Run du -sh /* | sort -rh | head -10 to find the largest directories. Identify the culprit (logs, database WAL files, temp files). If it's logs: rotate/compress with logrotate or delete old logs. If it's predictable growth, extend the EBS volume (increase size in cloud console), then sudo growpart /dev/xvda 1 (extend partition) and sudo resize2fs /dev/xvda1 (extend filesystem). File a ticket to increase monitoring thresholds or add auto-scaling. Document the incident.'

References & Further Reading

  • Arch Linux Filesystems Wiki — Comprehensive filesystem comparison
  • LVM Administration (RHEL 9) — Red Hat's official LVM guide
  • mkfs.ext4(8) man page — All ext4 formatting options

Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled

Advanced Topics

Deep dives into the Linux kernel, memory management, I/O stack, performance tuning, eBPF, observability, GPU computing, and build toolchains.

Chapter 15

Kernel Architecture & Syscalls

advancedAdvanced · 17 min read · 13 min practice

The Linux kernel is the core of the operating system — it manages processes, memory, hardware, and provides the syscall interface that user-space programs use to interact with the system. At Enterprise, kernel knowledge is essential for debugging production incidents, building high-performance systems, and understanding container isolation.

faang-insightLinuxkernelsyscallsschedulerkernel modules

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux processes, linux memory

Learning Objectives

  • Understand kernel Architecture: User Space vs Kernel Space
  • Understand system Calls Deep Dive
  • Understand the Kernel Scheduler & Context Switching
  • Understand kernel Modules & Device Drivers

Real-Life Analogy: Kernel as the OS Brain

The kernel is the central nervous system. System calls are nerve signals from body (userspace) to brain. Interrupts are reflexes - hardware taps. Scheduler decides which thought gets CPU time. Memory manager allocates brain space. Modules are loadable skills you can learn without rebooting the brain.

Kernel Architecture: User Space vs Kernel Space

The kernel runs in a privileged mode (ring 0 on x86). User-space programs run in an unprivileged mode (ring 3). System calls (syscalls) are the only way user-space can request kernel services. The /proc and /sys filesystems expose kernel data structures to user space. At Enterprise, engineers working on infrastructure must understand this boundary — performance issues, security vulnerabilities, and debugging often hinge on crossing the kernel-user boundary.
bash
# === Check kernel version ===
uname -a                            # Full kernel version and system info
uname -r                            # Just kernel release (e.g., 6.8.0-1015-aws)
cat /proc/version                   # Kernel version with gcc info

# === List loaded kernel modules ===
lsmod                               # All loaded modules
modinfo <module>                    # Module details (author, license, params)

# === Load/unload modules ===
sudo modprobe <module>              # Load module with dependency resolution
sudo insmod /path/to/module.ko      # Load module directly (no deps)
sudo rmmod <module>                 # Unload module
sudo modprobe -r <module>           # Unload with dependency cleanup

# === Kernel parameters (sysctl) ===
sysctl -a                           # List all kernel parameters
sysctl net.ipv4.tcp_tw_reuse         # Single parameter
sudo sysctl -w net.ipv4.ip_forward=1  # Set parameter (runtime, non-persistent)
cat /etc/sysctl.conf                 # Persistent kernel parameters
sudo sysctl -p                       # Reload config from file

# === Key kernel subsystems visible via /proc ===
cat /proc/cpuinfo                   # CPU details (model, cache, flags)
cat /proc/meminfo                   # Memory details (Total, Free, Buffers, Cached)
cat /proc/zoneinfo                  # Memory zone info (NUMA)
cat /proc/interrupts                # IRQ distribution across CPUs
cat /proc/softirqs                  # Softirq stats
cat /proc/sched_debug               # Scheduler debug info

# === cgroups v2 (kernel resource control) ===
ls /sys/fs/cgroup/                  # Cgroup controllers
cat /sys/fs/cgroup/cpu.stat         # CPU accounting
cat /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current   # Current memory usage

# === /sys — kernel objects ===
ls /sys/class/block/                # Block devices
ls /sys/class/net/                  # Network interfaces
cat /sys/class/net/eth0/device/vendor  # NIC vendor ID
cat /sys/block/sda/queue/scheduler   # I/O scheduler (mq-deadline, none, kyber)

# === Kernel ring buffer ===
dmesg                               # Kernel log messages
dmesg -w                            # Follow kernel log (like tail -f)
dmesg | grep -i error               # Kernel errors
dmesg -T                            # Human-readable timestamps

System Calls Deep Dive

Syscalls are the API between user-space and the kernel. Common syscalls: read, write, open, close, mmap, fork, execve, ioctl, epoll. Understanding syscalls is critical for debugging performance issues and understanding what your program is actually doing. At Enterprise, strace is one of the most valuable debugging tools for understanding 'why is this process slow?'
bash
# === strace — trace system calls ===
strace ls                           # Trace all syscalls for 'ls'
strace -e openat,read ls            # Filter specific syscalls
strace -p 1234                      # Attach to running process
strace -c ls                        # Count syscalls (summary, great for profiling)
strace -f -c nginx                  # Include child processes
strace -tt -T ls                    # Show timestamps + syscall duration

# === Key syscalls to know ===
# Process:
#   fork()    — Create child process
#   execve()  — Replace process image
#   exit()    — Terminate process
#   wait()    — Wait for child to exit
ey
#   clone()   — Create thread (or process with CLONE_VM)
#
# File I/O:
#   open() / openat() — Open a file
#   read()            — Read from fd
#   write()           — Write to fd
#   close()           — Close fd
#   lseek()           — Reposition file offset
#   mmap()            — Memory-map a file
#
# Networking:
#   socket()   — Create endpoint
#   bind()     — Bind to address
#   listen()   — Listen for connections
#   accept()   — Accept connection
#   connect()  — Connect to remote
#   send()/recv() — Send/receive data
#   epoll_create() / epoll_wait() — Event-driven I/O

# === Syscall count per process ===
strace -c -p $(pgrep -f nginx | head -1) 2>&1 | head -20

# === Check syscall availability (seccomp) ===
# List syscalls allowed for a process
cat /proc/$(pgrep -f nginx | head -1)/status | grep -i seccomp
# 0 = disabled, 1 = strict, 2 = filter (seccomp-bpf)

# === /proc/sys/kernel/ — tune syscall behavior ===
cat /proc/sys/kernel/pid_max        # Max PID (default 32768, increase for large
servers)
cat /proc/sys/kernel/threads-max    # Max threads system-wide
cat /proc/sys/fs/file-max           # Max open files system-wide

# === Syscall table ===
# Architecture-specific (x86_64 vs arm64 vs aarch64)
# List: /usr/include/x86_64-linux-gnu/asm/unistd_64.h
# Online: https://github.com/torvalds/linux/blob/master/arch/x86/entry/syscalls/syscall_
64.tbl

The Kernel Scheduler & Context Switching

The Completely Fair Scheduler (CFS) allocates CPU time to threads. Each thread gets a proportion of CPU based on its nice value and cgroup cpu.shares. The scheduler runs every context switch — when one thread is de-scheduled and another is scheduled. Context switches are not free — each one has overhead (TLB flush, cache miss). At Enterprise, high context-switch rates are a common performance issue in over-threaded applications.
bash
# === Scheduler statistics ===
cat /proc/schedstat                 # Raw scheduler stats
cat /proc/sched_debug               # Detailed scheduler info

# === Context switches per process ===
cat /proc/<PID>/status | grep -i "ctxt_switches"
# voluntary_ctxt_switches — process gave up CPU (I/O wait, sleep)
# nonvoluntary_ctxt_switches — kernel preempted process (time slice expired)

# === System-wide context switches ===
vmstat 1 5                          # cs column = context switches/sec
# High context switches (>100k/sec per core) often indicate threading issues

# === Check scheduler policy ===
chrt -p <PID>                       # Show scheduling policy + priority
# SCHED_OTHER (0) — Default fair scheduling
# SCHED_FIFO (1)  — Real-time, first-in-first-out
# SCHED_RR (2)    — Real-time, round-robin
# SCHED_BATCH (3) — Batch scheduling (non-interactive)
# SCHED_IDLE (5)  — Lowest priority (idle-like)

# === CPU affinity ===
taskset -p <PID>                    # Show CPU affinity mask
taskset -cp <PID>                   # Show CPU affinity (list)
sudo taskset -cp 0-3 <PID>          # Pin to CPUs 0-3

# === NUMA (Non-Uniform Memory Access) ===
nnumactl --hardware                  # NUMA topology
numastat -p <PID>                   # Memory allocation per NUMA node
# On multi-socket servers: accessing memory on remote socket is slower
# Enterprise pattern: pin processes + memory to same NUMA node for performance

# === IRQ affinity ===
# Distribute hardware interrupts across CPUs
cat /proc/irq/<IRQ>/smp_affinity     # CPU mask for this IRQ
# Set: echo 3 > /proc/irq/32/smp_affinity (send to CPUs 0+1)
# Or use irqbalance daemon for automatic distribution

# === /proc/loadavg ===
cat /proc/loadavg
# Load average = number of processes in running or uninterruptible state
# Over 1-minute, 5-minute, 15-minute windows
# Rule of thumb: load < #cores is normal; load > 2x#cores indicates overload

Kernel Modules & Device Drivers

Kernel modules are pieces of code that can be loaded/unloaded without rebooting. Most hardware drivers, filesystems, and network protocols are modules. At Enterprise, custom kernel modules are rare (most use the standard distribution kernel), but understanding the module system is essential for infosec (kernel rootkits load as modules) and for debugging hardware issues.
bash
# === Module management ===
lsmod                               # All loaded modules
# Format: Module, Size, Used by

# Module details
modinfo nvme                        # Show info (author, description, params, licenses)
modinfo -p nvme                     # Just parameters

# Module dependency map
depmod                              # Build modules.dep
cat /lib/modules/$(uname -r)/modules.dep  # Module dependencies

# === Blacklist a module ===
echo "blacklist nouveau" | sudo tee /etc/modprobe.d/blacklist-nvidia.conf

# === Module parameters ===
cat /sys/module/nvme/parameters/    # Runtime module parameters

echo "options nvme_core io_timeout=100" | sudo tee /etc/modprobe.d/nvme.conf

# === Check for kernel oops/crash ===
dmesg | grep -i "oops\|panic\|BUG"
ls /var/crash/                       # Crash dumps (kernel-core)

# === Kernel TLS / kTLS ===
lsmod | grep tls                     # Check if kTLS module loaded
# kTLS offloads TLS encryption to kernel (performance optimization)

# === Build a simple kernel module ===
# /usr/src/linux-headers-$(uname -r)/ must be installed
cat > hello.c << 'EOF'
#include <linux/module.h>
#include <linux/kernel.h>
#include <linux/init.h>

static int __init hello_init(void) {
    printk(KERN_INFO "Hello, Enterprise!\n");
    return 0;
}

static void __exit hello_exit(void) {
    printk(KERN_INFO "Goodbye, Enterprise!\n");
}

module_init(hello_init);
module_exit(hello_exit);
MODULE_LICENSE("GPL");
MODULE_AUTHOR("Production engineers");
EOF

# Makefile:
obj-m += hello.o
all:
	make -C /lib/modules/$(shell uname -r)/build M=$(PWD) modules
clean:
	make -C /lib/modules/$(shell uname -r)/build M=$(PWD) clean

# Build & test
make
sudo insmod hello.ko
dmesg | tail                        # See 'Hello, Enterprise!'
sudo rmmod hello
dmesg | tail                        # See 'Goodbye, Enterprise!'

Interview Q&A

Q: 'What happens when you type a command like 'ls' in a shell?'

A: '(1) Shell reads the line and parses it. (2) Shell searches PATH for 'ls' binary (via execve syscall). (3) Shell calls fork() to create a child process. (4) Child calls execve('/usr/bin/ls', ['ls'], envp) — this replaces the child's process image with the ls binary. (5) The kernel loads the ELF binary, maps memory segments, sets up the stack. (6) ls runs, calls openat()/getdents64() to read directory entries. (7) ls calls write() to print results. (8) ls calls exit(0). (9) Shell's wait() returns with exit status. (10) Shell prints the next prompt.'

Q: 'What is a kernel panic and how do you debug it?'

A: 'A kernel panic is when the kernel encounters a fatal error it cannot recover from (e.g., null pointer dereference in kernel code, hardware error, or corrupted kernel data structure). The kernel prints a message to the console and either hangs or reboots. Debug via: (1) Check dmesg from the next boot — look for 'Oops' or 'panic' messages. (2) Enable kdump/kexec to capture a crash dump for analysis with crash utility. (3) Check /var/crash/ for vmcore files. (4) Use 'crash' utility to analyze: crash /usr/lib/debug/boot/vmlinux-$(uname -r) /var/crash/vmcore. At Enterprise, kernel panics are rare but critical — the response involves the Linux kernel team and hardware vendors.'

Q: 'What is the difference between a process and a thread in the kernel?'

A: 'In the Linux kernel, both processes and threads are implemented as 'tasks' (struct task_struct). The difference: threads share the same address space (mm_struct), file descriptors (files_struct), and signal handlers via the clone() syscall with CLONE_VM | CLONE_FILES | CLONE_SIGHAND flags. Processes have independent address spaces created via fork() or clone() without CLONE_VM. To the scheduler, they're all tasks — the kernel doesn't distinguish between processes and threads. This is why 'lightweight processes' (LWPs) is an accurate description of Linux threads.'

References & Further Reading

  • Linux Kernel Documentation — Official kernel docs (admin-guide, core-api, process)
  • Linux Kernel Development — Robert Love, the best practical kernel guide
  • Linux Syscall Table — Interactive x86_64 syscall reference

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes
Mistake: Checking /proc/meminfo inside containers for memory
Solution: Use cgroup v2 files: /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current, memory.events

Troubleshooting

Symptom: uname returns unexpected output
Cause: Incorrect arguments, wrong working directory, or stale cached data
Solution: Verify arguments with `man uname`; check current path with `pwd`; clear cache if applicable
Symptom: Configuration changes not taking effect
Cause: Service not reloaded or syntax error in config file
Solution: Validate config syntax with built-in test flag; reload service with `systemctl reload` or `restart`
Symptom: kernel operation slow or timing out
Cause: Resource exhaustion (disk I/O, memory pressure, or lock contention)
Solution: Check `iostat`, `free -m`, and `top`; look for stuck processes with `ps aux --sort=-%cpu`
Symptom: Permission issues accessing kernel resources
Cause: Incorrect file ownership or overly restrictive permissions
Solution: Inspect with `ls -la`; fix with `chown`/ `chmod`; check ACLs with `getfacl`

Quick Quiz

Q1: What is Kernel Architecture: User Space vs Kernel Space?

A: A standardized directory structure that defines where files and directories should be placed on Linux systems

Q2: What does `uname` do?

A: Lists files and directories in the current or specified path

Q3: Which command would you use to view disk usage of the kernel directory?

A: du -sh /path/to/directory

Q4: A user reports "Permission denied" when trying to access a kernel directory. What is the first command you should run?

A: ls -ld /path/to/directory

Q5: In a kernel scenario, your monitoring alerts show uname failing intermittently. What is the correct debugging workflow?

A: Check logs → verify configuration → test in staging → apply fix → monitor

FAANG Insight

  • FAANG Architect's Note: You don't need to be a kernel developer to work at FAANG, but you need to understand what the kernel does
  • FAANG, kernel upgrades are rolled out in canary phases: 1% of hosts, then 10%, then 50%, then full rollout, with automated rollback on any metric regression

Production Tips

  • Production Tip: Before deploying a new kernel version at scale, always test on a non-production instance first

Best Practices

  • Use version control for all configuration (/etc, scripts, Dockerfiles)
  • Implement infrastructure as code; avoid manual server changes
  • Test changes in staging before production; have rollback plans

Enterprise Perspective

In enterprise environments, kernel architecture & syscalls is managed through automation (Ansible, Terraform), monitored centrally (Prometheus, Datadog), and governed by compliance policies (CIS benchmarks, SOC2). Changes go through change advisory boards with rollback procedures documented.

Chapter 16

Memory Management & OOM Handling

advancedAdvanced · 19 min read · 14 min practice

Memory management is one of the most critical subsystems in Linux. The kernel manages physical RAM, virtual address spaces, page caches, swapping, and out-of-memory (OOM) handling. At Enterprise, memory-related issues (OOM kills, memory leaks, page cache pressure) are among the most common production incidents.

faang-insightLinuxmemoryOOMpage cachehuge pagesperformance

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux kernel, linux processes

Learning Objectives

  • Understand virtual Memory & Address Spaces
  • Understand page Cache & Buffers
  • Understand oOM Killer: Out-Of-Memory Management
  • Understand huge Pages & Memory Performance

Real-Life Analogy: Memory as a Library with a Cache

RAM is the reading room - fast but limited. Swap is the basement archive - slow but vast. Page cache keeps recently read books on the desk. OOM killer is the librarian who ejects readers when the room is full. Huge pages are oversized reference books that stay open on the desk.

Virtual Memory & Address Spaces

Each process sees a virtual address space (typically 47 bits on x86_64 = 128TB). The kernel and hardware (MMU/TLB) translate virtual addresses to physical frames via page tables. This isolation is why one process can't read another's memory — and why mmap is so efficient (it maps files directly into the address space without copying).
bash
# === Process memory map ===
cat /proc/<PID>/maps                 # Full memory map of a process
# Format: address-range  perms  offset  dev  inode  pathname

# Example interpretation:
# 555555554000-555555558000 r--p 00000000 fd:01 123456 /usr/bin/ls
# 555555558000-55555555c000 r-xp 00004000 fd:01 123456 /usr/bin/ls  (code)
# 7ffff7d80000-7ffff7f80000 rw-p 00000000 00:00 0                 (heap)
# 7ffffffde000-7ffffffff000 rw-p 00000000 00:00 0                 (stack)

# === Memory regions ===
# r-xp — Code segment (read, execute)
# r--p — Read-only data
# rw-p — Read-write data (heap, global vars)
# rw-p [stack] — Thread stack
# rw-p [heap]  — Heap (brk/mmap allocations)

# === /proc/<PID>/status memory fields ===
cat /proc/<PID>/status | grep -i vm
# VmPeak: peak virtual memory size
# VmSize: current virtual memory size
# VmLck:  locked (mlock) memory
# VmPin:  pinned memory
# VmHWM:  peak resident set size
# VmRSS:  current resident set size (= actual physical RAM used)
# RssAnon: anonymous memory (heap, stack, mmap without file)
# RssFile: file-backed memory (mmap of files, shared libraries)
# RssShmem: shared memory
# VmSwap: swapped memory

# === pmap — detailed process memory ===
pmap -x <PID>                        # Extended memory map with RSS/DClean
pmap -XX <PID>                       # Very detailed (kernel flags)

# === Total memory usage ===
free -h                              # Total, used, free, shared, buff/cache, available
cat /proc/meminfo                    # Detailed breakdown
# MemTotal: total physical RAM
# MemFree: completely free
# Buffers: block device cache
# Cached: page cache + tmpfs + shmem
# SwapTotal: total swap
# SwapFree: free swap
# Available: estimated memory available for starting new apps
#             (= MemFree + reclaimable page cache + reclaimable slab)

# === Slab allocator (kernel memory) ===
cat /proc/slabinfo                   # Kernel slab allocations
# Slab is the kernel's memory allocator for small objects (inodes, dentries, etc.)
# High slab usage can indicate kernel memory leak
sudo slabtop -s c                    # Sort by cache size

Page Cache & Buffers

Linux uses all available free RAM for page cache (file data cached from disk) and buffers (block device metadata). This is not 'wasted' memory — it improves performance dramatically. When an application needs memory, the kernel reclaims page cache pages. At Enterprise, a system with '99% memory used' is normal and healthy — what matters is the 'available' metric.
bash
# === Page cache basics ===
# When you read a file, the kernel caches it in page cache
# Subsequent reads are served from RAM (fast) instead of disk (slow)

# === Drop caches (for benchmarking only!) ===
# NEVER do this in production — it destroys performance temporarily
sudo sync && echo 3 | sudo tee /proc/sys/vm/drop_caches
# 1 = free page cache, 2 = free dentries/inodes, 3 = free both

# === Page cache statistics ===
cat /proc/vmstat | grep -E "(pgfault|pgmajfault|pgfree)"
# pgfault   — Minor page faults (page was in memory, just needed mapping)
# pgmajfault — Major page faults (page had to be read from disk)
# High major faults = slow I/O, insufficient memory

# === File-backed memory vs anonymous ===
# file-backed: mmap'd files, executable code, shared libraries
# anonymous: heap, stack, anonymous mmap (mallloc uses this)
# Key difference: file-backed can be reclaimed (write dirty pages to disk);
#   anonymous must be swapped to disk (slow!) or the process must be killed

# === vmtouch — page cache analysis ===
sudo apt install vmtouch
vmtouch /var/log/syslog              # How much of a file is in cache
vmtouch /usr/bin/*                   # Page cache stats for directory
# Files that are frequently accessed stay in cache (good)
# Cache can be 'poisoned' by a one-time large read (bad)

# === fincore — which pages are cached ===
# (part of linux-tools-common)
# fincore --pages=false --summarize /var/log/syslog

# === Tune page cache behavior ===
# /etc/sysctl.conf:
# vm.vfs_cache_pressure=100          # Default: reclaim slab at same rate as page cache
#   Lower = keep more dentry/inode cache (good for many small files)
#   Higher = reclaim slab more aggressively
# vm.min_free_kbytes=65536            # Reserve memory for critical allocations
# vm.dirty_ratio=20                   # Max dirty pages % before blocking writers
# vm.dirty_background_ratio=10        # Background writeback starts at this % dirty
# vm.swappiness=10                    # Only swap when absolutely necessary (default:
60)

OOM Killer: Out-Of-Memory Management

When the system runs out of memory, the kernel's OOM killer selects a process to kill to free memory. Understanding OOM behavior is critical — it's often the root cause of mysterious service failures. At Enterprise, OOM kills are monitored closely, and services are configured with cgroup memory limits to prevent a single service from starving the system.
bash
# === OOM logs ===
dmesg | grep -i "oom_killer\|OOM"    # Find OOM events
# Output includes:
# - Total memory, free memory
# - Which process was killed (PID, name, score)
# - Memory footprint of top processes

# === OOM score ===
cat /proc/<PID>/oom_score            # OOM score (higher = more likely to be killed)
# Score is based on: RSS + swap + page table + (total memory * factor)
cat /proc/<PID>/oom_score_adj        # Adjust score (-1000 to 1000)
# Services you want to protect: echo -1000 > /proc/<PID>/oom_score_adj
# Services you want killed first: echo 1000 > /proc/<PID>/oom_score_adj

# === OOM in systemd units ===
[Service]
# Protect critical services:
OOMScoreAdjust=-500

# === OOM in cgroups ===
# cgroups v2 memory.max will trigger OOM kill within the cgroup,
# without affecting other cgroups. This is the PREFERRED approach at Enterprise.
echo "100M" > /sys/fs/cgroup/myapp/memory.max

# === How the OOM killer selects a victim ===
# 1. Calculates oom_score for each process
#    - Based on RSS, swap, page table size
#    - Root processes get a 3% bonus (less likely to be killed)
# 2. Selects the process with the highest score
# 3. Sends SIGKILL
# 4. If memory still insufficient, repeats

# === Prevent OOM: reserve swap ===
# Swap is slower than RAM but provides a safety buffer
# Without swap, OOM killer activates sooner
# At Enterprise, swap is often disabled — memory limits are enforced via cgroups
# But understanding swap is still essential:
swapon --show                        # Check swap status
sudo swapon /swapfile                # Enable swap file

# === Memory overcommit ===
cat /proc/sys/vm/overcommit_memory
# 0 = heuristic overcommit (default — conservative)
# 1 = always overcommit (dangerous!)
# 2 = no overcommit (vm.overcommit_ratio controls %)
# At Enterprise: usually 0 or 2, never 1

# === Check for OOM in systemd journal ===
journalctl -k | grep -i "oom\|killed"
journalctl -u myapp | grep -i "killed\|memory"

Huge Pages & Memory Performance

The default page size is 4KB. Huge pages (2MB or 1GB) reduce TLB misses and improve performance for memory-intensive applications (databases, JVMs, packet processing). At Enterprise, databases and JVM-based services commonly use huge pages. There are two types: Transparent Huge Pages (THP) and explicit Huge Pages (hugetlbfs).
bash
# === Huge page sizes ===
cat /proc/meminfo | grep Huge
# HugePages_Total: 256           # 256 × 2MB = 512MB allocated
# HugePages_Free: 128
# Hugepagesize: 2048 kB

# === Configure explicit huge pages ===
echo 256 | sudo tee /proc/sys/vm/nr_hugepages
# Persistent: vm.nr_hugepages=256 in /etc/sysctl.conf

# === Mount hugetlbfs ===
sudo mkdir /mnt/huge
sudo mount -t hugetlbfs hugetlbfs /mnt/huge
# Now applications can mmap files in /mnt/huge with 2MB pages

# === Transparent Huge Pages (THP) ===
cat /sys/kernel/mm/transparent_hugepage/enabled
# [always] madvise never
# always — kernel tries to use 2MB pages automatically
# madvise — applications request huge pages via madvise()
# never — disable THP

# === THP and databases ===
# THP can cause latency spikes (background defragmentation)
# Many databases (MongoDB, PostgreSQL) recommend disabling THP

# To disable THP:
sudo sh -c 'echo never > /sys/kernel/mm/transparent_hugepage/enabled'
sudo sh -c 'echo never > /sys/kernel/mm/transparent_hugepage/defrag'

# === Per-process hugepages ===
# JVM: -XX:+UseLargePages -XX:LargePageSizeInBytes=2m
# PostgreSQL: huge_pages = on
# MongoDB: disable THP (uses its own WiredTiger cache management)

# === Memory access patterns ===
# Sequential access: page cache prefetches ahead (readahead)
# Random access: high TLB miss rate, benefits from huge pages
# NUMA-local access: always prefer allocating memory on the same socket
numactl --membind 0 --cpunodebind 0 ./myapp  # Bind memory + CPU to node 0

Interview Q&A

Q: 'Free shows 99% memory used but the system is running fine. Is this a problem?'

A: 'Not necessarily. Linux uses free memory for page cache and buffers — this is not 'used' memory in the traditional sense. The key metric is 'available' (in /proc/meminfo or free -h), which estimates memory reclaimable without swapping. If 'available' is low and swap usage is growing, then you have a problem. At Enterprise, we monitor 'available memory' rather than 'free memory' and set alerts when it drops below a threshold (e.g., 10% of total).'

Q: 'A process was killed by OOM. How do you investigate?'

A: 'Check dmesg | grep -i oom to find the OOM event — it shows the victim (PID, name), total memory, and the top memory consumers at the time. Check if the process had cgroup memory limits that were too low. Check if there was a memory leak using ps aux --sort=-%mem | head before and after restart. Check /var/log/ for any evidence of the leak. If it's a Java process, analyze the heap dump. Root cause is almost always: (1) memory leak in application code, (2) cgroup limit set too low, or (3) traffic spike causing legitimate increased memory usage.'

Q: 'What is the difference between buffers and cache in /proc/meminfo?'

A: 'Buffers are metadata associated with block devices (e.g., filesystem metadata like superblock, inode tables). Cache is the page cache — actual file data cached from disk. The practical difference: 'Buffers' is relatively small and stable, while 'Cached' can be gigabytes and fluctuates based on file access patterns. Both are reclaimable, which is why 'available' combines them with free memory. In modern kernels (5.x+), the distinction is less meaningful than it used to be, but the fields are kept for compatibility.'

References & Further Reading

  • Kernel VM Documentation — All vm sysctl parameters explained
  • Understanding the Linux Kernel — O'Reilly, chapters on memory management
  • Linux Memory Management (Mel Gorman) — Classic deep-dive into the VM subsystem

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes
Mistake: Checking /proc/meminfo inside containers for memory
Solution: Use cgroup v2 files: /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current, memory.events

Quick Reference

Troubleshooting

Symptom: cat returns unexpected output
Cause: Incorrect arguments, wrong working directory, or stale cached data
Solution: Verify arguments with `man cat`; check current path with `pwd`; clear cache if applicable
Symptom: Configuration changes not taking effect
Cause: Service not reloaded or syntax error in config file
Solution: Validate config syntax with built-in test flag; reload service with `systemctl reload` or `restart`
Symptom: memory operation slow or timing out
Cause: Resource exhaustion (disk I/O, memory pressure, or lock contention)
Solution: Check `iostat`, `free -m`, and `top`; look for stuck processes with `ps aux --sort=-%cpu`
Symptom: Permission issues accessing memory resources
Cause: Incorrect file ownership or overly restrictive permissions
Solution: Inspect with `ls -la`; fix with `chown`/ `chmod`; check ACLs with `getfacl`

Q1: What is Virtual Memory & Address Spaces?

A: A standardized directory structure that defines where files and directories should be placed on Linux systems

Q2: What does `cat` do?

A: Lists files and directories in the current or specified path

Q3: Which command would you use to view disk usage of the memory directory?

A: du -sh /path/to/directory

Q4: A user reports "Permission denied" when trying to access a memory directory. What is the first command you should run?

A: ls -ld /path/to/directory

Q5: In a memory scenario, your monitoring alerts show cat failing intermittently. What is the correct debugging workflow?

A: Check logs → verify configuration → test in staging → apply fix → monitor

  • FAANG, every service runs in a cgroup with a memory limit that's 80-90% of its expected max

  • Implement infrastructure as code; avoid manual server changes
  • Test changes in staging before production; have rollback plans

Enterprise Perspective

In enterprise environments, memory management & oom handling is managed through automation (Ansible, Terraform), monitored centrally (Prometheus, Datadog), and governed by compliance policies (CIS benchmarks, SOC2). Changes go through change advisory boards with rollback procedures documented.

Chapter 17

I/O Stack & Filesystem Performance

advancedAdvanced · 18 min read · 14 min practice

The Linux I/O stack spans multiple layers: application → syscall → VFS → filesystem → block layer → I/O scheduler → device driver → disk hardware. At Enterprise, I/O performance is critical for databases, log ingestion, and data pipelines. Understanding each layer helps you diagnose latency, tune throughput, and design storage systems that scale.

faang-insightLinuxI/Ostorage performancefilesystemiostatbenchmarking

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux kernel, linux storage

Learning Objectives

  • Understand the Linux I/O Stack
  • Understand measure I/O Latency & Throughput
  • Understand filesystem Performance Tuning
  • Understand storage Performance at Enterprise scale

Real-Life Analogy: I/O as a Bucket Brigade

Applications pass buckets (buffers) to the kernel. The kernel passes them to the device driver. The driver hands them to hardware. DMA lets hardware fill buckets directly without CPU. Async I/O (io_uring) is a conveyor belt - submit many buckets, collect them later.

The Linux I/O Stack

When an application calls read() or write(), the request travels through: 1. VFS (Virtual Filesystem Switch — dispatches to the right FS), 2. Filesystem (ext4/XFS — translates file offset to block number), 3. Page Cache (cache hit = return immediately; cache miss = read from disk), 4. Block Layer (manages I/O requests, merging, sorting), 5. I/O Scheduler (reorders requests for efficiency), 6. Device Driver (NVMe, SATA, virtio), 7. Disk Hardware. Each layer adds latency — total latency = sum of all layers.
bash
# === I/O stack visibility ===
# At each layer, you can observe with:
# Application:     strace -e read,write,fsync
# VFS/Page Cache:  /proc/meminfo (Cached), /proc/vmstat (pgfault)
# Filesystem:      /proc/fs/<fstype>/, xfs_info/stat
# Block Layer:     iostat -x, /sys/block/<dev>/stat
# I/O Scheduler:   /sys/block/<dev>/queue/scheduler
# Device:          smartctl, nvme-cli

# === I/O schedulers ===
cat /sys/block/sda/queue/scheduler    # Current + available schedulers
# [mq-deadline] — Good for SSDs with mixed workloads
# none          — No scheduling (NVMe devices, each app manages its own I/O)
# kyber         — Latency-optimized for fast SSDs
# bfq           — Desktop/soft real-time (fairness-focused)

# Change scheduler:
echo kyber | sudo tee /sys/block/nvme0n1/queue/scheduler

# === Block layer merging ===
# The block layer merges adjacent I/O requests for efficiency
cat /sys/block/sda/queue/nr_requests    # Max I/O requests in queue (default: 128)
cat /sys/block/sda/queue/max_sectors_kb # Max I/O size per request

# === Direct I/O vs Buffered I/O ===
# Buffered I/O — goes through page cache (default)
#   dd if=bigfile of=/dev/null bs=1M count=1000    # Uses page cache
# Direct I/O — bypasses page cache (database, O_DIRECT)
#   dd if=bigfile of=/dev/null bs=1M count=1000 oflag=direct
# O_DIRECT is faster for large sequential access (no double-copy)
# O_DIRECT is worse for small random access (no cache benefit)

# === fsync — flushing to disk ===
# fsync() ensures data is on stable storage (crucial for databases)
# Each fsync has a cost — group commits reduce this
strace -e fsync mysql              # Watch fsync calls
# For PostgreSQL: fsync=on (default)
# For MySQL/InnoDB: innodb_flush_log_at_trx_commit=1

# === I/O depth (queue depth) ===
# How many I/O requests can be in-flight at once
# High queue depth = more parallelism, higher latency per request
# Low queue depth = less parallelism, lower latency per request
cat /sys/block/nvme0n1/queue/nr_requests  # Linux queue depth (default: 128)
# NVMe drives can handle queue depth of 65536
# SATA SSDs usually saturate at queue depth ~32

Measure I/O Latency & Throughput

I/O performance has two dimensions: latency (how long each operation takes) and throughput (operations per second / bytes per second). At Enterprise, tail latency (p99, p99.9) is more important than average — a single slow I/O can hold up thousands of requests in a database.
bash
# === iostat — the essential I/O tool ===
iostat -x 1                          # Extended stats, refresh every second
# Key columns:
# %util     — % of time device was busy servicing I/O (>80% = degraded)
# r/s, w/s  — Read/write IOPS
# rkB/s, wkB/s — Throughput
# await     — Average I/O latency (service time + queue time) in ms
# svctm     — Actual service time (iowait + hardware time)
# aqu-sz    — Average queue length

# === ioping — measure latency ===
sudo apt install ioping
ioping -c 10 /var/lib/postgresql     # Measure disk latency to directory
ioping -RD /dev/sda                  # Measure random read latency

# Typical latencies:
# NVMe SSD:       ~0.01-0.1 ms
# SATA SSD:       ~0.1-0.5 ms
# HDD:            ~5-15 ms
# Network block (EBS): ~0.5-5 ms (gp3), ~0.1-2 ms (io2)

# === fio — benchmark I/O ===
# Random read test (database-like)
fio --name=randread --ioengine=libaio --iodepth=32 --rw=randread \
    --bs=4k --direct=1 --size=1G --runtime=30 --filename=/tmp/test

# Sequential write test (log-like)
fio --name=seqwrite --ioengine=libaio --iodepth=64 --rw=write \
    --bs=1M --direct=1 --size=10G --runtime=60 --filename=/tmp/test

# === blktrace — block layer tracing ===
sudo blktrace -d /dev/nvme0n1 -o - | blkparse -i -
# Tracks every I/O request through the block layer
# Use for: identifying I/O merges, queue depth, latency breakdown

# === perf — I/O profiling ===
sudo perf stat -e block:block_rq_issue,block:block_rq_complete sleep 10
# Count I/O requests issued vs completed

# === iowatcher — visualize I/O ===
# Creates a timeline graph of I/O activity
sudo blktrace -d /dev/sda -o trace
# Then: iowatcher -t trace/sda.blktrace.* -o io.svg

Filesystem Performance Tuning

Each filesystem has different performance characteristics. ext4 is reliable and well-tested. XFS excels at large files and parallel I/O. Btrfs offers snapshots and checksums. At Enterprise, the choice depends on workload: XFS for large-scale databases on RHEL/Amazon Linux, ext4 for general-purpose Ubuntu instances.
bash
# === Mount options for performance ===
# /etc/fstab performance optimizations:
# noatime — Don't update access times (biggest single perf win)
# nodiratime — Also skip directory access time updates
# nobarrier — Skip write barriers (DANGEROUS for data integrity!)
# data=ordered — ext4: write data before metadata (default, safe)
# data=writeback — ext4: no ordering (faster, crash-unsafe without journal)

# Best practice mount:
# UUID=xxx /data ext4 defaults,noatime,errors=remount-ro 0 2

# === ext4 tuning ===
# Reserve blocks for root (default 5% — wasted on data volumes)
sudo tune2fs -m 0 /dev/sdb1          # 0% reserved on data volume

# Enable flex_bg (flexible block groups) — better large-file perf
sudo mkfs.ext4 -O flex_bg /dev/sdb1

# === XFS tuning ===
# Allocation groups — more = better parallelism for multi-threaded I/O
sudo mkfs.xfs -d agcount=8 /dev/sdb1

# XFS log size — larger = better for write-heavy workloads
sudo mkfs.xfs -l size=256m /dev/sdb1

# === Filesystem fragmentation ===
# ext4: check fragmentation
sudo fsck.ext4 -fn /dev/sdb1         # -n = no modify, -f = force

# XFS: check and defragment
xfs_db -c frag -r /dev/sdb1          # Fragmentation report
sudo xfs_fsr /dev/sdb1               # Online defragmentation

# === Write cache control ===
# For SSD: enable write cache (performance), ensure power loss protection
cat /sys/block/sda/queue/write_cache  # 'write back' or 'write through'
# write back: faster, risk of data loss on power failure
# write through: slower, safer

# === I/O priority (ionice) ===
# CFQ/bfq scheduler only
ionice -c 2 -n 0 -p <PID>           # Best-effort, highest priority
ionice -c 3 -p <PID>                # Idle I/O (only when no other I/O)
# Real-time I/O class (use with caution!):
ionice -c 1 -n 0 ./heavy_io_program

Storage Performance at Enterprise scale

At Enterprise, storage is abstracted through multiple layers: local NVMe (fast, ephemeral), EBS (persistent, network-attached), EFS/NFS (shared filesystem), and distributed filesystems (Ceph, JuiceFS, Luster). Each has different performance profiles and failure modes. Understanding the Linux I/O stack helps you make the right choice and debug issues at any layer.
bash
# === Local NVMe ===
# /dev/nvme0n1, /dev/nvme1n1, ...
# Latency: ~10-100μs
# Throughput: 3-7 GB/s per device
# ✗ Ephemeral — data lost on instance stop/termination
# ✓ Best for: caches, temp data, high-throughput processing

# === EBS (Elastic Block Store) ===
# Network-attached block devices
# gp3: 3000 IOPS base, 16K IOPS burst, 125 MB/s per volume
# io2: up to 256K IOPS, 1000 MB/s, 99.999% durability
# ✓ Persistent — survives instance stop/termination
# ✓ Best for: databases, any persistent data
# ✗ Network latency: ~0.5-5ms (vs NVMe's 0.01-0.1ms)

# === EBS-optimized instances ===
# Dedicated network bandwidth for EBS traffic
# Check: cat /sys/block/xvda/device/queue_depth
# EBS Nitro: uses NVMe interface, no more xvda

# === NFS (Network File System) ===
# Shared filesystem accessed via network
mount -t nfs4 -o vers=4.2,rsize=1048576,wsize=1048576,hard,timeo=600,retrans=2 \
    nfs-server:/export /mnt/nfs

# NFS tuning:
# rsize/wsize: 1MB for modern networks (1048576)
# hard: retry indefinitely until server responds (vs soft: return error on timeout)
# timeo: timeout in deciseconds (600 = 60s before retransmit)
# retrans: 2 retries before major timeout

# === Check I/O errors ===
dmesg | grep -i "I/O error\|buffer I/O"
cat /sys/block/sda/device/state       # 'running' = healthy
sudo smartctl -H /dev/sda             # SMART health check

# === /sys/block stats ===
cat /sys/block/sda/stat
# Field: read I/Os, read merges, read sectors, read ticks,
#        write I/Os, write merges, write sectors, write ticks,
#        in-flight I/Os, I/O ticks, time in queue

# Calculate average latency:
# latency_ms = (read_ticks + write_ticks) / (read_ios + write_ios) / 1000

Interview Q&A

Q: 'The database is running slow. iostat shows 99% util on the data disk. What do you check?'

A: '99% util means the device is saturated — I/O requests are queuing. First, check iostat -x 1 for await and aqu-sz. If await is high but svctm is low, the bottleneck is in the queue (too many concurrent requests), not the device. If both are high, the device itself is slow. Next, check which process is generating the I/O: iotop -oP. Look at the query plan in the database — a missing index causing a full table scan is the most common cause. If the workload is legitimate, either: (1) increase IOPS (if EBS), (2) add read replicas, (3) optimize the query, or (4) add caching (Redis, page cache).'

Q: 'What's the difference between I/O wait and I/O util?'

A: 'CPU iowait (%iowait in top) is the percentage of time the CPU is idle while waiting for I/O. It's a CPU-centric metric. I/O util (%util in iostat) is the percentage of time the device was busy — it's a device-centric metric. High iowait with low util means processes are waiting on a device that's not fully utilized (e.g., contention on a slow file, or a network filesystem). High util with low iowait means the device is busy but the CPUs are doing other work (parallelism). At Enterprise, we monitor both: high iowait + high util = device bottleneck; high iowait + low util = contention or NFS issue.'

Q: 'A developer says 'the filesystem is slow.' How do you systematically debug this?'

A: 'Rule out layers from bottom to top. (1) Check SMART stats — is the disk failing? (2) Run ioping to measure raw device latency. (3) Run fio to benchmark device throughput. (4) Check filesystem mount options — maybe data=writeback was accidentally set? (5) Check iostat -x for queue depth and await. (6) Check page cache hit rate with cachestat (from bcc/bpftrace). (7) Check application behavior with strace -e pread64,pwrite64,fsync -p <PID>. In 90% of cases, the 'slow filesystem' is either an application pattern (too many fsyncs, no index, serial I/O) or a saturated device.'

References & Further Reading

  • Block Layer Stats — Understanding /sys/block/*/stat fields
  • Red Hat: I/O Performance Tuning — RHEL storage performance guide
  • Linux Storage Stack Deep Dive — Tristan Hume's excellent visual guide
