Chapter 18

Performance Tuning & Profiling

advancedAdvanced · 16 min read · 12 min practice

Performance tuning is the art of identifying and eliminating bottlenecks. Linux provides powerful tools — perf, flamegraphs, ftrace, and BPF — for profiling CPU, memory, I/O, and network. At Enterprise, performance engineering is a dedicated role, and every infrastructure engineer should be able to use perf to find the bottleneck in any system.

faang-insightLinuxperfprofilingperformanceftraceflamegraphs

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux kernel, linux memory, linux processes

Learning Objectives

  • Understand perf: The Universal Profiler
  • Understand brendan Gregg's USE Method & Methodology
  • Understand ftrace: Kernel Function Tracing
  • Understand cPU & Memory Profiling in Practice

Real-Life Analogy: Performance as a Detective Story

perf is the magnifying glass. Flame graphs are the crime scene map - wide towers are suspects. USE method (Utilization, Saturation, Errors) is the investigation checklist. The culprit is usually not the CPU - it is waiting (I/O, locks, cache misses).

perf: The Universal Profiler

The perf tool (part of linux-tools-common) uses hardware performance counters and kernel tracepoints to profile system performance with minimal overhead. perf can answer: 'Where is my program spending CPU time?', 'Why are there cache misses?', 'What is the kernel doing right now?' At Enterprise, perf is the first tool reached for CPU performance investigations.
bash
# === Install perf ===
sudo apt install linux-tools-common linux-tools-$(uname -r)

# === CPU sampling ===
# Record CPU samples for a running process (30 seconds):
sudo perf record -F 99 -p <PID> --sleep 30
# -F 99: sample at 99 Hz (avoids aliasing with 100Hz scheduler ticks)
# -p <PID>: target process

# View the profile:
sudo perf report                    # Interactive TUI report
sudo perf report --stdio            # Text output

# === Call graph (stack traces) ===
sudo perf record -F 99 -p <PID> -g -- sleep 30  # -g = call graphs
sudo perf report -g                 # Show call graph

# === Live profiling ===
sudo perf top                       # Real-time hottest functions (system-wide)
sudo perf top -p <PID>              # Real-time per process
sudo perf top -K                    # Kernel functions only
sudo perf top -U                    # User-space functions only

# === Static tracing ===
# Count specific events:
sudo perf stat -e cycles,instructions,cache-misses,cache-references ls
# IPC (instructions per cycle) > 1 is good; < 0.5 indicates inefficiency

# === Perf events ===
# Hardware events: cpu-cycles, instructions, cache-misses, branches, branches-misses
# Software events: context-switches, page-faults, cpu-migrations
# Tracepoint events: sched:sched_switch, block:block_rq_complete

# === perf for flamegraphs ===
sudo perf record -F 99 -a -g -- sleep 60     # System-wide call graph
sudo perf script > out.perf                  # Export for flamegraph script
# Then use Brendan Gregg's FlameGraph tools:
# git clone https://github.com/brendangregg/FlameGraph
# stackcollapse-perf.pl out.perf > out.folded
# flamegraph.pl out.folded > flamegraph.svg

# === perf diff — compare profiles ===
# After deploying a code change or config change:
sudo perf record -F 99 -p <PID> -g -o perf.before -- sleep 30
# (make change)
sudo perf record -F 99 -p <PID> -g -o perf.after -- sleep 30
perf diff perf.before perf.after     # Compare profiles

Brendan Gregg's USE Method & Methodology

The USE Method (Utilization, Saturation, Errors) is a systematic approach to performance analysis. For every resource (CPU, memory, disk, network), check: utilization (how busy?), saturation (how much queue?), errors (any failures?). This method, developed by Brendan Gregg, is the standard approach at Enterprise for performance investigations.
bash
# === CPU ===
# Utilization:
mpstat -P ALL 1                      # Per-CPU utilization
# Saturation:
vmstat 1                             # 'r' column = run queue (processes waiting for
CPU)
# Rule: r < #cores is normal; r > 2x#cores means CPU saturation
# Errors:
cat /proc/stat | grep -i "cpu"       # Look for strange counters

# === Memory ===
# Utilization:
free -h                               # Total, used, available
cat /proc/meminfo                     # Detailed breakdown
# Saturation:
vmstat 1                              # 'si' = swap in, 'so' = swap out (>0 = swapping!)
# Errors:
cat /proc/vmstat | grep -E "(oom|fail|error)"
dmesg | grep -i oom                   # OOM kills

# === Disk ===
# Utilization:
iostat -x 1                           # %util > 80% = degraded
# Saturation:
iostat -x 1                           # 'aqu-sz' > 0 = requests queued
# Errors:
iostat -x 1                           # Look at 'rrqm/s', 'wrqm/s' — merges
cat /sys/block/sda/device/state       # 'running' = ok

# === Network ===
# Utilization:
sar -n DEV 1                          # Bandwidth usage vs interface capacity
# Saturation:
ip -s link                           # 'overruns', 'dropped', 'backlog'
ss -s                                 # Socket stats
# Errors:
ip -s -s link                        # Errors, dropped, overruns
netstat -i                            # Interface error counters

# === Quick triage: 60-second checklist (Brendan Gregg) ===
# 1. uptime                             — Load averages
# 2. dmesg | tail                       — Kernel errors
# 3. vmstat 1                           — Run queue, swapping, context switches
# 4. mpstat -P ALL 1                    — Per-CPU balance
# 5. pidstat 1                          — Per-process CPU
# 6. iostat -xz 1                       — Disk I/O
# 7. free -m                            — Memory
# 8. sar -n DEV 1                       — Network
# 9. sar -n TCP,ETCP 1                  — TCP stats
# 10. top                               — Overview

ftrace: Kernel Function Tracing

ftrace is the kernel's built-in tracer. It can trace function calls, interrupts, scheduling, and latency events with zero overhead when disabled and very low overhead when enabled. It's available on every Linux system without installing additional tools.
bash
# === ftrace basics ===
# Mount tracefs:
mount -t tracefs tracefs /sys/kernel/tracing

# === Available tracers ===
cat /sys/kernel/tracing/available_tracers
# function      — Trace function calls (high overhead)
# function_graph — Trace with call graph (nested function calls)
# preemptirqsoff — Trace preemption and IRQ-off latency
# wakeup       — Trace scheduling latency
# nop          — No tracing (default)

# === Function tracing ===
# Trace a specific function:
echo function > /sys/kernel/tracing/current_tracer
echo do_sys_open > /sys/kernel/tracing/set_ftrace_filter
echo 1 > /sys/kernel/tracing/tracing_on
# ... run your test ...
echo 0 > /sys/kernel/tracing/tracing_on
cat /sys/kernel/tracing/trace        # View trace output

# === Latency tracing ===
# Measure preemption-off latency:
echo preemptirqsoff > /sys/kernel/tracing/current_tracer
echo 0 > /sys/kernel/tracing/tracing_max_latency
# Wait for events...
cat /sys/kernel/tracing/tracing_max_latency  # Show max latency (μs)
cat /sys/kernel/tracing/trace        # Show what caused it

# === trace-cmd (front-end for ftrace) ===
sudo apt install trace-cmd
trace-cmd record -p function_graph -g do_sys_open ls
trace-cmd report                     # View recorded trace

# === wakeup latency ===
# Measures how long it takes after a task is woken up for it to actually run
echo wakeup > /sys/kernel/tracing/current_tracer
echo <PID> > /sys/kernel/tracing/set_ftrace_pid

CPU & Memory Profiling in Practice

When an application is slow, profiling identifies exactly where time is spent. CPU profiling samples what functions are on the CPU. Off-CPU profiling (missing in traditional profilers) shows what the process is waiting for. At Enterprise, we use both on-CPU and off-CPU analysis to understand the full picture.
bash
# === On-CPU profiling ===
# Where is the CPU spending time?
perf record -F 99 -p <PID> -g -- sleep 30
perf report -g --stdio

# === Off-CPU profiling (why is the process blocked?) ===
# Use offcputime from bcc/BPF:
sudo offcputime -p <PID> 30          # Show stack traces + blocked time

# === Memory profiling ===
# /usr/bin/time -v — detailed memory usage
/usr/bin/time -v myprogram 2>&1 | grep -E "(Maximum resident|Minor|Major)"
# Maximum resident set size — peak memory
# Minor page faults — page already in cache
# Major page faults — had to read from disk (BAD for performance)

# === Cache misses ===
perf stat -e cache-misses,cache-references,LLC-loads,LLC-load-misses ./myapp
# Cache miss rate > 5% may indicate poor data locality

# === Branch mispredictions ===
perf stat -e branches,branch-misses ./myapp
# High branch miss rate (>5%) can indicate unpredictable code paths

# === IPC (Instructions Per Cycle) ===
perf stat -e cycles,instructions ./myapp
# IPC > 2: excellent (highly optimized)
# IPC 1-2: good
# IPC < 1: stalled (cache misses, branch mispredicts, memory latency)

# === Context switch profiling ===
perf stat -e context-switches,cpu-migrations,page-faults ./myapp
# High context switches: over-threaded, bad thread pool sizing
# High cpu-migrations: scheduler moving threads between CPUs (bad cache locality)

# === GDB (GNU Debugger) for performance ===
# If a process is stuck:
sudo gdb -p <PID> -batch -ex "thread apply all bt" 2>&1
# Shows all thread stack traces — great for deadlock detection

Interview Q&A

Q: 'A service is using 100% CPU but throughput is low. What do you do?'

A: 'Run perf top -p to see which function is consuming CPU. This usually tells you the problem immediately. Common causes: (1) busy-waiting/polling loop, (2) inefficient algorithm (O(n²) instead of O(n log n)), (3) excessive string operations (regex, JSON parsing), (4) thread contention (spinlocks, mutex contention). If perf points to a library function, look at the call chain with perf record -g. A flamegraph makes this even clearer. At Enterprise, we run perf in staging before every major deployment to catch performance regressions.'

Q: 'How would you profile a 10-second latency spike that happens once per hour?'

A: 'This is the hardest profiling problem — an intermittent issue. Approach: (1) Set up continuous profiling (perf with rotating buffers, or BPF tools) that captures profiles and keeps them in a ring buffer. When the latency spike occurs, save the buffer. (2) Use latency tracing via ftrace to capture the longest kernel preemption-off interval during the spike. (3) Correlate with other metrics — I/O latency, network latency, GC logs. (4) The cause is often: cron job (logrotate, backup), GC pause, network retransmission, or kernel interference (kswapd, journal commit). At Enterprise, we use distributed tracing (Jaeger, Zipkin) that captures individual request latency breakdowns across services.'

Q: 'Your application uses more memory than expected. How do you find the leak?'

A: 'First, confirm it's a memory leak and not just normal growth. Run ps aux --sort=-%mem over time. Use /usr/bin/time -v to track peak RSS. For heap analysis: (1) JVM: enable -XX:+HeapDumpOnOutOfMemoryError and analyze the .hprof file with Eclipse MAT. (2) Node.js: use --inspect and Chrome DevTools heap snapshot. (3) Python: use tracemalloc or objgraph. (4) Native code: use Valgrind (memcheck) for precise leak detection, or perf mem for sampling. At Enterprise, memory profiling is part of the CI pipeline — we track RSS trends across builds and alert on regressions.'

References & Further Reading

  • Brendan Gregg's Linux Performance — The definitive resource (tools, methodologies, talks)
  • Systems Performance: Enterprise and the Cloud — Brendan Gregg, the bible of performance analysis
  • perf Wiki — Official perf documentation and examples

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes
Mistake: Checking /proc/meminfo inside containers for memory
Solution: Use cgroup v2 files: /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current, memory.events

Troubleshooting

Symptom: sudo returns unexpected output
Cause: Incorrect arguments, wrong working directory, or stale cached data
Solution: Verify arguments with `man sudo`; check current path with `pwd`; clear cache if applicable
Symptom: Configuration changes not taking effect
Cause: Service not reloaded or syntax error in config file
Solution: Validate config syntax with built-in test flag; reload service with `systemctl reload` or `restart`
Symptom: perf operation slow or timing out
Cause: Resource exhaustion (disk I/O, memory pressure, or lock contention)
Solution: Check `iostat`, `free -m`, and `top`; look for stuck processes with `ps aux --sort=-%cpu`
Symptom: Permission issues accessing perf resources
Cause: Incorrect file ownership or overly restrictive permissions
Solution: Inspect with `ls -la`; fix with `chown`/ `chmod`; check ACLs with `getfacl`

Chapter 19

BPF & eBPF Deep Dive

advancedAdvanced · 16 min read · 12 min practice

BPF (Berkeley Packet Filter), evolved into eBPF (extended BPF), is a revolutionary kernel technology that allows running sandboxed programs in the kernel without changing kernel code or loading modules. eBPF enables safe, efficient, and programmable observability, networking, and security. At Enterprise, eBPF powers critical infrastructure: Cilium (Kubernetes networking), Falco (security), Pixie (observability), and custom monitoring tools.

faang-insightLinuxeBPFBPFbpftracebccXDPobservabilitynetworking

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux kernel, linux networking

Learning Objectives

  • Understand eBPF Architecture & Concepts
  • Understand bpftrace: High-Level eBPF Tracing
  • Understand bcc: The BPF Compiler Collection
  • Understand xDP & eBPF Networking

Real-Life Analogy: eBPF as X-Ray Vision

eBPF lets you safely run custom programs inside the kernel - like inserting a microscope into a living cell. No kernel modules, no crashes. You attach probes to kernel functions (kprobes), tracepoints, or USDT. The verifier ensures your program cannot crash the kernel.

eBPF Architecture & Concepts

eBPF programs are written in a restricted C, compiled to BPF bytecode, verified by the kernel (safety checks: no loops with unbounded iterations, valid memory access, max instruction count), and attached to events (syscalls, tracepoints, kprobes, network interfaces). The in-kernel verifier is what makes eBPF safe — it rejects any program that could crash or hang the kernel. At Enterprise, eBPF is used for: performance monitoring (bcc/bpftrace tools), network observability (Cilium), security auditing (Falco), and custom container monitoring.
bash
# === Check eBPF support ===
cat /proc/sys/kernel/unprivileged_bpf_disabled  # 0 = enabled, 1 = disabled
# Check if BPF syscall is available:
grep bpf /proc/kallsyms | head -5

# === eBPF program types ===
# Kprobes     — Dynamic kernel function tracing
# Tracepoints — Static kernel tracepoints
# Perf events — PMC-based sampling
# XDP         — eXpress Data Path (network driver level)
# TC          — Traffic Control (network stack level)
# cgroup      — cgroup operations (device, socket, sysctl)

# === The eBPF lifecycle ===
# 1. Write BPF program (C / Rust / Python / Lua)
# 2. Compile to BPF bytecode (LLVM/clang)
# 3. Load into kernel (bpf() syscall)
# 4. Kernel verifier checks safety
# 5. JIT compiler translates to native code
# 6. Attach to event (kprobe, tracepoint, etc.)
# 7. Program runs on event, writes to BPF maps
# 8. User-space reads maps via bpf() syscall

# === BPF maps (data structures) ===
# Hash maps, arrays, per-CPU arrays, stacks, queues, ring buffers
# Shared between kernel BPF program and user-space
# Used for: aggregated metrics, configuration, state

# === Limitations ===
# - Max 4096 instructions (can be extended to 1M with tail calls)
# - No loops until recently (bounded loops added in Linux 5.17)
# - No kernel function calls (must be inlined) until BPF helpers
# - Stack limited to 512 bytes

bpftrace: High-Level eBPF Tracing

bpftrace is a high-level tracing language for Linux eBPF. It's like awk for kernel tracing — you write one-liners to observe kernel and user-space events. At Enterprise, bpftrace is the go-to tool for ad-hoc performance investigations and root cause analysis.
bash
# === Install bpftrace ===
sudo apt install bpftrace

# === File open tracing ===
bpftrace -e 'tracepoint:syscalls:sys_enter_openat { printf("%s %s\n", comm,
str(args->filename)); }'
# Shows every file opened, by which process

# === New process tracing ===
bpftrace -e 'tracepoint:syscalls:sys_enter_execve { printf("%s -> %s\n", comm,
str(args->filename)); }'

# === Kill tracing (who is sending SIGKILL?) ===
bpftrace -e 'tracepoint:syscalls:sys_enter_kill { printf("%s -> PID %d SIG %d\n", comm,
args->pid, args->sig); }'

# === OOM tracing ===
bpftrace -e 'kprobe:oom_kill_process { printf("OOM killed %s (%d)\n",
str(args->task->comm), args->task->pid); }'

# === Count syscalls by process ===
bpftrace -e 'tracepoint:syscalls:sys_enter_* { @[comm] = count(); }'

# === Block I/O tracing ===
bpftrace -e 'kprobe:blk_account_io_start { printf("I/O: %s %s\n", comm, args->rwbs); }'

# === TCP connection tracing ===
bpftrace -e 'kprobe:tcp_connect { printf("%s connected to %s:%d\n", comm,
ntop(args->sk->__sk_common->skc_daddr), args->sk->__sk_common->skc_dport); }'

# === One-shot latency histogram (read syscall) ===
bpftrace -e 'kprobe:vfs_read { @start[tid] = nsecs; } kretprobe:vfs_read /@start[tid]/ {
@us = hist((nsecs - @start[tid]) / 1000); delete(@start[tid]); }'

# === Profile: CPU sampling ===
bpftrace -e 'profile:hz:99 { @[kstack, comm] = count(); }'  # Kernel stack
bpftrace -e 'profile:hz:99 { @[ustack, comm] = count(); }'  # User stack

# === Built-in tools ===
sudo bpftrace -l                     # List all probes
sudo bpftrace -l 'tracepoint:syscalls:*'  # List syscall tracepoints
# Many pre-built tools come with bpftrace:
ls /usr/share/bpftrace/tools/
# opensnoop.bt, killsnoop.bt, execsnoop.bt, tcplife.bt, ...

# Run a pre-built tool:
sudo bpftrace /usr/share/bpftrace/tools/opensnoop.bt

bcc: The BPF Compiler Collection

bcc (BPF Compiler Collection) provides Python- and Lua-based tools for eBPF tracing. It's more flexible than bpftrace (full programming language support) but also more complex. bcc tools are production-ready — many are used daily at Enterprise for performance monitoring.
bash
# === Install bcc ===
sudo apt install bpfcc-tools   # Ubuntu package name
# Tools are prefixed with -bpfcc:

# === Essential bcc tools ===
# opensnoop: trace file opens
sudo opensnoop-bpfcc

# execsnoop: trace new processes
sudo execsnoop-bpfcc

# biotop: block I/O by process
sudo biotop-bpfcc

# tcplife: TCP connection lifecycle
sudo tcplife-bpfcc

# tcptop: TCP throughput by process
sudo tcptop-bpfcc

# cpuunclaimed: CPU not claimed by scheduler
sudo cpuunclaimed-bpfcc

# offcputime: trace blocked time (off-CPU analysis)
sudo offcputime-bpfcc -K 30

# runqlat: scheduler run-queue latency
sudo runqlat-bpfcc

# profile: CPU sampling with frames
sudo profile-bpfcc -af 30

# cachestat: page cache hit rate
sudo cachestat-bpfcc

# llcstat: Last-level cache misses
sudo llcstat-bpfcc

# === Custom bcc Python script (minimal example) ===
cat > trace_open.py << 'EOF'
from bcc import BPF

# BPF C program
bpf_program = """
int kprobe__do_sys_openat2(struct pt_regs *ctx, int dfd, const char __user *filename,
int flags) {
    bpf_trace_printk("openat: %s\\n", filename);
    return 0;
}
"""

b = BPF(text=bpf_program)
b.trace_print()
EOF

sudo python3 trace_open.py

XDP & eBPF Networking

XDP (eXpress Data Path) is an eBPF-based packet processing framework that runs at the network driver level — before the kernel's network stack processes the packet. This enables DDoS mitigation, load balancing, and packet filtering at line rate (millions of packets per second). At Enterprise, XDP is used for: DDoS protection (Cloudflare), Kubernetes service load balancing (Cilium), and custom network filtering.
bash
# === XDP program flow ===
# 1. Packet arrives at NIC
# 2. XDP program runs (driver level, before skb allocation)
# 3. Actions: XDP_DROP, XDP_PASS, XDP_TX, XDP_REDIRECT
# 4. Result: drop packet (fast), pass to kernel stack (normal),
#            transmit back out (hairpin), or redirect to another NIC

# === XDP vs kernel bypass (DPDK) ===
# XDP: kernel is still in control, safe, programmable, no userspace changes
# DPDK: bypasses kernel entirely, userspace driver, fastest possible,
#       but requires dedicated CPUs and application changes

# === Cilium (Kubernetes CNI powered by eBPF) ===
# Cilium uses eBPF for:
# - Service load balancing (replacing kube-proxy)
# - Network policies (L3/L4/L7)
# - Observability (Hubble)
# - Transparent encryption

# === Check if XDP is supported ===
ip link show eth0                     # Check features: xdp
# Enable XDP on interface:
sudo ip link set dev eth0 xdp obj my_xdp_prog.o sec xdp

# === XDP example: DDoS blacklist ===
# Simple XDP program to drop packets from blacklisted IPs
# (Conceptual — real XDP uses BPF maps for dynamic blacklists)

# === eBPF for security: Falco ===
# Falco uses eBPF tracepoints/kprobes to detect:
# - Container escape attempts
# - Unauthorized file access
# - Suspicious network connections
# - Privilege escalation

# Install Falco (using eBPF probe):
# falco driver-loader bpf
# falco --bpf

Interview Q&A

Q: 'How does eBPF differ from a kernel module?'

A: 'A kernel module can access any kernel function, data structure, or memory — it can crash the entire system. eBPF programs are verified by the kernel's BPF verifier before loading — the verifier checks for: (1) valid memory access (no null pointers), (2) bounded loops (termination guaranteed), (3) maximum instruction count (4096 default, up to 1M with tail calls), (4) valid helper function calls. If verification fails, the program is rejected. This safety guarantee is what makes eBPF deployable in production — a buggy eBPF program can't crash the kernel like a buggy module can.'

Q: 'What's a real-world use case for eBPF at Enterprise?'

A: 'One example: Cilium at scale. At a Enterprise company using Kubernetes with 10,000+ nodes, Cilium uses eBPF to replace kube-proxy for service load balancing — instead of iptables rules (which become O(n) with number of services), eBPF programs are updated per-service and run at the XDP or TC layer. This eliminates the iptables scaling bottleneck. Another example: Datadog's continuous profiler uses eBPF to sample stack traces without any application changes — no agent code, no recompilation, no JVM flags.'

Q: 'What are the limitations of eBPF?'

A: '(1) Instruction limit: 4096 by default (can be extended). (2) Stack limit: 512 bytes — you can't allocate large local data structures. (3) No unbounded loops (before Linux 5.17). (4) Limited helper functions — you can call only a fixed set of BPF helpers, not arbitrary kernel functions. (5) Verifier complexity — the verifier can be slow for complex programs. (6) No floating-point arithmetic. (7) Kernel version dependence — newer BPF features require newer kernels. At Enterprise, we run custom kernels (or at least Linux 5.10+) specifically to get modern eBPF features.'

References & Further Reading

  • What is eBPF? — Official eBPF foundation introduction
  • bcc GitHub — Tools and examples for BPF Compiler Collection
  • bpftrace GitHub — One-liners, tools, and documentation
  • Cilium — Kubernetes CNI powered by eBPF

Chapter 20

Monitoring & Observability

advancedAdvanced · 16 min read · 12 min practice

Monitoring and observability are how you understand what your systems are doing. Monitoring tells you something is wrong; observability lets you ask why. At Enterprise, every service emits metrics, logs, and traces. This guide covers the Linux-native tooling for system monitoring and the concepts that scale from single-server debugging to fleet-wide observability.

faang-insightLinuxmonitoringobservabilityPrometheusloggingSRE

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux processes, linux networking, linux systemd

Learning Objectives

  • Understand the Three Pillars: Metrics, Logs, Traces
  • Understand prometheus & Node Exporter
  • Understand logging: journald, syslog & Logrotate
  • Understand distributed Tracing & Observability Concepts

Real-Life Analogy: Monitoring as Vital Signs

Metrics are heart rate and blood pressure. Logs are the patient history. Traces are the journey of a request through the body. Alerts are the alarm when vitals go critical. The goal is not to collect data - it is to know when to intervene.

The Three Pillars: Metrics, Logs, Traces

Metrics are numeric measurements (CPU, memory, requests per second, error rates) — best for alerting and dashboards. Logs are discrete events with timestamps — best for debugging specific incidents. Traces follow a request across services — best for understanding distributed system behavior. At Enterprise, the 'three pillars' are served by specialized systems: Prometheus (metrics), Elasticsearch/Splunk (logs), Jaeger (traces). On a single Linux system, the same concepts apply with local tools.
bash
# === CPU Metrics ===
cat /proc/stat                         # Overall CPU stats (user, nice, system, idle,
iowait, irq, softirq, steal)
mpstat -P ALL 1                        # Per-CPU breakdown
sar -u 1 5                             # Historical CPU stats (from sysstat package)

# === Memory Metrics ===
cat /proc/meminfo                      # Detailed memory state
free -h                                # Quick summary
sar -r 1 5                             # Historical memory stats

# === Disk Metrics ===
iostat -x 1 5                          # Device-level I/O metrics
sar -b 1 5                             # Historical I/O stats
sar -d 1 5                             # Per-device I/O stats

# === Network Metrics ===
sar -n DEV 1 5                         # Per-interface bandwidth
sar -n TCP,ETCP 1 5                    # TCP stats (connections, retransmits, segments)
ip -s link                             # Interface errors, drops, overruns

# === System Load ===
cat /proc/loadavg                      # Load average (1/5/15 min)
# Load is NOT CPU % — it's the number of processes in running + uninterruptible state
# On an 8-core system: load 8 = fully utilized (each core has 1 task)

# === Top/htop ===
top -b -n 1 | head -20                 # Batch mode (non-interactive)
htop                                   # Interactive with better UX

# === Collect all metrics with a script ===
cat > quick_health.sh << 'EOF'
#!/bin/bash
echo "=== UPTIME ==="
uptime
echo "=== CPU ==="
grep 'cpu ' /proc/stat | awk '{usage=($2+$4)*100/($2+$4+$5)} END {print usage "%"}'
echo "=== MEMORY ==="
free -h | grep Mem
echo "=== DISK ==="
df -h / /var/lib /data 2>/dev/null
echo "=== TOP PROCESSES ==="
ps aux --sort=-%cpu | head -6
echo "=== NETWORK ==="
ip -s link | grep -E "(eth|ens|enp)" -A 2
EOF

Prometheus & Node Exporter

Prometheus is the industry standard for metric collection and alerting. It scrapes HTTP endpoints and stores time-series data. Node Exporter exposes Linux system metrics in Prometheus format. At Enterprise, Prometheus is the default metrics system — every service exposes a /metrics endpoint, and node_exporter runs on every server for hardware/OS metrics.
bash
# === Node Exporter ===
# Install:
sudo apt install prometheus-node-exporter
# Or download from https://prometheus.io/download/

# Node exporter exposes metrics at :9100/metrics
# Key metric groups:
# node_cpu_seconds_total     — CPU time by mode
# node_memory_MemTotal_bytes — Memory stats
# node_disk_io_time_seconds_total — Disk I/O
# node_network_receive_bytes_total — Network
# node_filesystem_avail_bytes — Disk space
# node_load1, node_load5, node_load15 — Load averages

# === Check node exporter metrics ===
curl -s localhost:9100/metrics | head -30

# === PromQL (Prometheus Query Language) examples ===
# CPU utilization (1m average):
# 100 - (avg by(instance) (rate(node_cpu_seconds_total{mode="idle"}[1m])) * 100)

# Memory utilization:
# (1 - node_memory_MemAvailable_bytes / node_memory_MemTotal_bytes) * 100

# Disk space > 80%:
# node_filesystem_avail_bytes{mountpoint="/"} /
node_filesystem_size_bytes{mountpoint="/"} < 0.2

# === Prometheus configuration (prometheus.yml) ===
# scrape_configs:
#   - job_name: 'node'
#     static_configs:
#       - targets: ['localhost:9100', 'server1:9100', 'server2:9100']

# === Installation ===
sudo apt install prometheus
# Web UI at localhost:9090

# === Alertmanager rules ===
# groups:
#   - name: node_alerts
#     rules:
#       - alert: DiskSpaceLow
#         expr: node_filesystem_avail_bytes{mountpoint="/"} /
node_filesystem_size_bytes{mountpoint="/"} < 0.1
#         for: 5m
#         labels: { severity: critical }
#         annotations: { summary: "Disk space < 10% on {{ $labels.instance }}" }

Logging: journald, syslog & Logrotate

Linux has two logging systems: the legacy syslog (text files in /var/log/) and the modern journald (binary, structured). At Enterprise, logs are shipped to centralized systems (Splunk, Elasticsearch, Datadog) — but the local management of logs (rotation, size limits, compression) is still critical to prevent filling the disk.
bash
# === journald ===
# Centralized logging with structured metadata
# Config: /etc/systemd/journald.conf

# Key settings:
# SystemMaxUse=1G        — Limit total journal disk usage
# MaxRetentionSec=2week  — Auto-delete old logs
# Compress=yes           — Compress old journal files
# ForwardToSyslog=no     — Don't duplicate to syslog

# === logrotate ===
# Rotate, compress, and delete old log files
# Config: /etc/logrotate.conf + /etc/logrotate.d/

# Example: /etc/logrotate.d/nginx
/var/log/nginx/*.log {
    daily
    missingok
    rotate 14           # Keep 14 days
    compress
    delaycompress       # Don't compress newest file
    notifempty
    create 0640 www-data adm
    sharedscripts
    postrotate
        systemctl reload nginx  # Signal nginx to reopen logs
    endscript
}

# Test logrotate:
sudo logrotate -d /etc/logrotate.d/nginx  # Dry-run (debug)
sudo logrotate -f /etc/logrotate.d/nginx  # Force run

# === /var/log/ key files ===
# /var/log/syslog          — General system log (Debian/Ubuntu)
# /var/log/messages        — General system log (RHEL/CentOS)
# /var/log/auth.log        — Authentication logs (SSH, sudo)
# /var/log/kern.log        — Kernel messages
# /var/log/dmesg           — Kernel ring buffer
# /var/log/nginx/          — Web server logs
# /var/log/mysql/          — Database logs

# === Centralized logging ===
# rsyslog — forward logs to central server
# /etc/rsyslog.conf:
# *.* @logserver.example.com:514     # Send all logs via UDP
# *.* @@logserver.example.com:514    # Send all logs via TCP (reliable)
# Use TLS for production:
# *.* @@logserver.example.com:6514

# === Log format for machine parsing ===
# JSON logs are preferred at Enterprise (easier to parse)
# Example: logger '{"event":"deploy","service":"myapp","status":"ok"}'

# === Structured logging in applications ===
# KEY=value format for grep-ability:
# 2024-01-15T10:30:00 [INFO] user_id=abc123 action=login duration_ms=42

# Journald structured fields:
journalctl -o verbose | grep -E "(MESSAGE=|SYSLOG_IDENTIFIER=|_PID=)"

Distributed Tracing & Observability Concepts

On a single server, you track a request through the OS. In a distributed system, you track it across services. Distributed tracing propagates a trace ID across service boundaries, allowing you to reconstruct the full path of a request. At Enterprise, tracing is a first-class concern — every framework and infrastructure component propagates trace context.
bash
# === Single-server tracing (low-level) ===
strace -f -e trace=network -p <PID>     # Trace network syscalls
perf trace -p <PID>                     # Trace syscalls with perf

# === OpenTelemetry (industry standard) ===
# OpenTelemetry provides:
# - APIs and SDKs for generating traces, metrics, logs
# - Exporters to send data to backends (Jaeger, Prometheus, Datadog)
# - Context propagation (W3C TraceContext, Baggage)

# === Trace context propagation ===
# Trace ID is passed via HTTP headers:
# traceparent: 00-0af7651916cd43dd8448eb211c80319c-b7ad6b7169203331-01
#   version-trace_id-span_id-trace_flags

# === Key metrics from tracing ===
# Latency: time spent in each service
# Error rate: failed requests / total requests
# Saturation: utilization of each service
# Apdex: user satisfaction score based on latency thresholds

# === RED Method (for service monitoring) ===
# Rate: requests per second
# Errors: failed requests per second
# Duration: latency (average, p50, p95, p99)

# === USE Method (for resource monitoring) ===
# Utilization: % busy
# Saturation: queue length
# Errors: error count

# === Four Golden Signals (Google SRE) ===
# 1. Latency: time to serve a request
# 2. Traffic: demand on the system
# 3. Errors: rate of failed requests
# 4. Saturation: how 'full' the system is

Interview Q&A

Q: 'You're on call and get paged for high CPU on a production server. Walk through your response.'

A: 'Acknowledge the page. SSH in (or use session manager). Run uptime to see load averages — is it a spike or sustained? mpstat -P ALL 1 — is one core pegged or all cores? ps aux --sort=-%cpu | head — which process is using the CPU? perf top -p <PID> — which function is hot? If it's a known pattern (e.g., GC in Java, V8 garbage collection in Node), check the application logs. If it's a new pattern, capture a perf record and create a flamegraph. Check if a deploy just happened — rollback if it's a regression. If the process is legitimately busy (traffic spike), add capacity (horizontal scaling) or check if upstream throttling was removed. File a postmortem ticket.'

Q: 'How do you set up monitoring for a new Linux service?'

A: 'First, identify the four golden signals. Install node_exporter to collect OS-level metrics. Add a /metrics endpoint to the application exposing RED metrics (rate, errors, duration). Set up Prometheus to scrape both. Create a Grafana dashboard with: (1) request rate and latency, (2) error rate by status code, (3) CPU/memory/disk of the service process, (4) database connection pool usage if applicable. Set alerts for: p99 latency > 500ms for 5 minutes, error rate > 1% for 5 minutes, disk > 80% full. Test the alerts by simulating the failure conditions. At Enterprise, every service has a 'service level objective' (SLO) — a target for availability or latency — and monitoring measures against that SLO.'

Q: 'What's the difference between monitoring and observability?'

A: 'Monitoring is tracking known failure modes — CPU > 90%, disk full, process not running. It answers 'what is broken?' Observability is the ability to ask arbitrary questions about your system without knowing the answers in advance — 'why is this specific request slow?', 'what is this process waiting for?'. Monitoring requires predefined dashboards and alerts. Observability requires structured data (traces, structured logs, high-cardinality metrics) and tools to explore it. At Enterprise, we invest heavily in observability because the most impactful outages are rarely the things you thought to monitor in advance.'

References & Further Reading

  • Prometheus Documentation — Official Prometheus docs and best practices
  • OpenTelemetry Documentation — Industry standard for observability
  • Google SRE Book: Monitoring — The four golden signals and monitoring philosophy

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes
Mistake: Checking /proc/meminfo inside containers for memory
Solution: Use cgroup v2 files: /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current, memory.events

Quick Reference

| Command | Description | |---|---| | `cat` | The Three Pillars: Metrics, Logs, Traces | | `mpstat` | The Three Pillars: Metrics, Logs, Traces | | `sar` | The Three Pillars: Metrics, Logs, Traces | | `sudo` | Prometheus & Node Exporter | | `curl` | Prometheus & Node Exporter | | `daily` | Logging: journald, syslog & Logrotate | | `missingok` | Logging: journald, syslog & Logrotate | | `rotate` | Logging: journald, syslog & Logrotate | | `strace` | Distributed Tracing & Observability Concepts | | `perf` | Distributed Tracing & Observability Concepts |

Troubleshooting

Symptom: cat returns unexpected output
Cause: Incorrect arguments, wrong working directory, or stale cached data
Solution: Verify arguments with `man cat`; check current path with `pwd`; clear cache if applicable
Symptom: Configuration changes not taking effect
Cause: Service not reloaded or syntax error in config file
Solution: Validate config syntax with built-in test flag; reload service with `systemctl reload` or `restart`
Symptom: monitoring operation slow or timing out
Cause: Resource exhaustion (disk I/O, memory pressure, or lock contention)
Solution: Check `iostat`, `free -m`, and `top`; look for stuck processes with `ps aux --sort=-%cpu`
Symptom: Permission issues accessing monitoring resources
Cause: Incorrect file ownership or overly restrictive permissions
Solution: Inspect with `ls -la`; fix with `chown`/ `chmod`; check ACLs with `getfacl`

Quick Quiz

Q1: What is The Three Pillars: Metrics, Logs, Traces?

A: A standardized directory structure that defines where files and directories should be placed on Linux systems

Q2: What does `cat` do?

A: Lists files and directories in the current or specified path

Q3: Which command would you use to view disk usage of the monitoring directory?

A: du -sh /path/to/directory

Q4: A user reports "Permission denied" when trying to access a monitoring directory. What is the first command you should run?

A: ls -ld /path/to/directory

Q5: In a monitoring scenario, your monitoring alerts show cat failing intermittently. What is the correct debugging workflow?

A: Check logs → verify configuration → test in staging → apply fix → monitor

FAANG Insight

  • FAANG Architect's Note: The most important skill in monitoring is knowing what to monitor
  • FAANG, we don't monitor everything — we monitor the things that tell us whether the system is healthy

Production Tips

  • Production Tip: Never alert on average latency

Best Practices

  • Use version control for all configuration (/etc, scripts, Dockerfiles)
  • Implement infrastructure as code; avoid manual server changes
  • Test changes in staging before production; have rollback plans

Enterprise Perspective

In enterprise environments, monitoring & observability is managed through automation (Ansible, Terraform), monitored centrally (Prometheus, Datadog), and governed by compliance policies (CIS benchmarks, SOC2). Changes go through change advisory boards with rollback procedures documented.

Chapter 21

GPU Computing on Linux

advancedAdvanced · 17 min read · 13 min practice

GPU computing on Linux is the backbone of ML/AI at Enterprise. Understanding the GPU driver stack — from kernel modules to CUDA runtime to container integration — is essential for ML engineers, infrastructure engineers, and anyone deploying GPU workloads at scale. The Linux ecosystem for GPUs spans NVIDIA (dominant), AMD (ROCm), and Intel (oneAPI).

faang-insightLinuxGPUCUDANVIDIAMIGmachine learningprofiling

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux kernel, linux memory

Learning Objectives

  • Understand gPU Stack: From Kernel to Application
  • Understand gPU Performance & Profiling
  • Understand gPU Multi-Tenancy: MIG, Time-Slicing & MPS
  • Understand nVIDIA Container Toolkit & Kubernetes GPU

Real-Life Analogy: GPU as a Massive Assembly Line

CPU is a few master craftsmen (serial). GPU is thousands of apprentices (parallel). Each apprentice does the same simple task on different data. CUDA/OpenCL are the work instructions. Memory transfer (CPU<->GPU) is the loading dock - the bottleneck.

GPU Stack: From Kernel to Application

The GPU software stack has multiple layers: kernel drivers (nvidia.ko, nvidia-uvm.ko), user-space libraries (libcuda.so, libcudart.so), compute frameworks (CUDA, cuDNN, TensorRT), and orchestration (NVIDIA container toolkit, MIG). At Enterprise, this stack is baked into custom AMIs and container images — engineers don't install it manually, but understanding it is essential for debugging.
bash
# === Check GPU hardware ===
lspci | grep -i nvidia               # List NVIDIA GPUs
lspci -v -s 01:00.0                  # Detailed GPU info

# === NVIDIA driver modules ===
lsmod | grep nvidia                  # nvidia, nvidia_uvm, nvidia_drm, nvidia_modeset
# nvidia.ko       — Core driver
# nvidia-uvm.ko   — Unified Virtual Memory (CUDA unified memory)
# nvidia-drm.ko   — Direct Rendering Manager (display)
# nvidia-modeset.ko — Mode setting (display)

# === nvidia-smi (the single most important GPU command) ===
nvidia-smi                           # Summary: GPUs, processes, memory, temp
nvidia-smi -L                        # List all GPUs with UUIDs
nvidia-smi
--query-gpu=index,name,temperature.gpu,utilization.gpu,memory.used,memory.total
--format=csv
nvidia-smi pmon -c 1                 # Per-process GPU usage
nvidia-smi dmon -c 1                 # Per-device monitoring
nvidia-smi topo -m                   # GPU topology (NVLink, PCIe)

# === GPU health ===
nvidia-smi -q -d TEMPERATURE         # Temperature details
nvidia-smi -q -d POWER               # Power draw and limits
nvidia-smi -q -d CLOCK               # Clock speeds (graphics, memory, SM)

# === ECC (Error Correcting Code) ===
nvidia-smi -q -d ECC                 # ECC errors (memory bit flips)
# ECC is ON by default on datacenter GPUs (A100, H100, V100)
# ECC OFF on consumer GPUs (RTX 4090) — trades reliability for speed

# === GPU persistence daemon ===
sudo nvidia-persistenced --verbose    # Keep GPU initialized between processes
# Without it: first CUDA call on each GPU takes ~1 second (driver init)
# With it: driver stays loaded, zero startup latency

# === Reset GPU (if hung) ===
sudo nvidia-smi --gpu-reset 0         # Reset GPU 0 (only on Tesla/Quadro)

GPU Performance & Profiling

GPU profiling identifies bottlenecks: is your kernel compute-bound (SM utilization high), memory-bound (memory bandwidth high), or latency-bound (kernel launch overhead)? At Enterprise, every ML training run is profiled — it's part of the CI pipeline for model training. Tools: nvidia-smi, NSight Systems, NSight Compute, and PyTorch profiler.
bash
# === Real-time GPU monitoring ===
watch -n 0.5 nvidia-smi              # Refresh every 500ms

# === GPU utilization patterns ===
# 0-30%:   Underutilized (small batch size, CPU bottleneck, data loading issue)
# 30-70%:  Moderate (typical for distributed training with comm overhead)
# 70-95%:  Good utilization (efficient training)
# 95-100%: Saturated (GPU is the bottleneck — ideal for throughput)

# === Key metrics to watch ===
# Volatile GPU-Util — SM utilization (not a perfect metric, hides underutilization)
# Memory-Usage — How much GPU memory is used
# Temperature — Throttling starts at ~85°C for most GPUs
# Power Draw — If below TDP, GPU may be power-limited or underutilized

# === CUDA-MEMCHECK (memory errors in GPU kernels) ===
cuda-memcheck ./my_cuda_program       # Check for out-of-bounds, misaligned access

# === NSight Systems (system-level profiling) ===
# Profile CPU + GPU interactions:
nsys profile --stats=true -o profile python train.py
# Shows: CPU kernel time, GPU kernel time, CUDA API calls, memory operations

# === NSight Compute (kernel-level profiling) ===
ncu --target-processes all python train.py  # Profile individual CUDA kernels

# === PyTorch profiler ===
# python -m torch.utils.bottleneck train.py
# Or:
# with torch.profiler.profile(activities=[...]) as prof:
#     run_training()
# print(prof.key_averages().table(sort_by="cuda_time_total"))

# === NCCL (NVIDIA Collective Communications Library) ===
# Used for multi-GPU and multi-node communication
# Debug: NCCL_DEBUG=INFO, NCCL_DEBUG_SUBSYS=ALL
# Performance: NCCL_ALGO=Ring, NCCL_PROTO=Simple
nccl-tests/build/all_reduce_perf -b 128M -e 8G -f 2 -g 8  # Benchmark NCCL

# === CUDA graphs (reduce kernel launch overhead) ===
# Captures a sequence of GPU operations and replays them
# Reduces CPU launch overhead — critical for small-batch inference
# Enabled by default in PyTorch 2.x with torch.compile

GPU Multi-Tenancy: MIG, Time-Slicing & MPS

Modern datacenter GPUs support sharing a physical GPU across multiple workloads. MIG (Multi-Instance GPU) partitions A100/H100 into up to 7 isolated instances with dedicated memory and compute. Time-slicing shares GPU time (no memory isolation). MPS shares CUDA context across processes. At Enterprise, MIG is used for cost-efficient inference serving — partitioning a single A100 into 3x20GB instances for 3 models.
bash
# === MIG (Multi-Instance GPU) — A100/H100 only ===
# List MIG capabilities:
nvidia-smi mig -i 0                   # Check MIG support for GPU 0

# Enable MIG on GPU 0:
sudo nvidia-smi -mig 1 -i 0
sudo nvidia-smi mig -cgi 19,19,19,19  # Create 4 compute instances, 20GB each
# 19 = 'MIG 3g.20gb' profile (3 GPU slices, 20GB) on A100
# Profiles: 1g.10gb, 2g.20gb, 3g.40gb, 4g.40gb, 7g.80gb

# List MIG instances:
nvidia-smi mig -lgi

# Assign MIG device to container:
docker run --gpus '"device=MIG-<UUID>"' nvidia/cuda:12.0-runtime

# === Time-slicing (Kubernetes) ===
# GPU sharing without memory isolation
# Each pod gets GPU time but can use all GPU memory
# Best for: burstable inference workloads
# Configure via NVIDIA device plugin with 'time-slicing' config

# === MPS (Multi-Process Service) ===
# Enables CUDA context sharing across processes
# Better utilization for many small inference requests
export CUDA_MPS_PIPE_DIRECTORY=/tmp/mps_pipe
export CUDA_MPS_LOG_DIRECTORY=/tmp/mps_log
nvidia-cuda-mps-control -d           # Start MPS daemon
# All CUDA processes now share a single CUDA context
# echo "quit" | nvidia-cuda-mps-control  # Stop MPS

# === GPU memory bandwidth contention ===
# Multiple processes sharing GPU memory bandwidth can conflict
# MIG isolates memory bandwidth; time-slicing does not
# At Enterprise: inference workloads use MIG; training gets full GPU

# === GPU oversubscription (CUDA Unified Memory) ===
# GPU can access more memory than physical VRAM via page faults
# Slow! Only use for prototyping
# Production: allocate within GPU memory limits

NVIDIA Container Toolkit & Kubernetes GPU

GPU access in containers requires the NVIDIA Container Toolkit (nvidia-docker2 / nvidia-container-toolkit). It mounts GPU drivers and CUDA libraries into containers. At Enterprise, GPU pods are scheduled via the NVIDIA Kubernetes Device Plugin, which advertises GPU count per node and handles MIG partitioning.
bash
# === NVIDIA Container Toolkit ===
# Install:
sudo apt install nvidia-container-toolkit
sudo nvidia-ctk runtime configure --runtime=docker
sudo systemctl restart docker

# Test:
docker run --gpus all nvidia/cuda:12.0-runtime nvidia-smi

# === How it works ===
# The toolkit mounts:
#   /usr/lib/x86_64-linux-gnu/libcuda.so  — CUDA driver library
#   /usr/lib/x86_64-linux-gnu/libnvidia-*  — NVIDIA libraries
#   /proc/driver/nvidia/                   — Driver information
#   /dev/nvidia*                           — GPU device files
# Without toolkit: container can't access /dev/nvidia0

# === Kubernetes GPU scheduling ===
# NVIDIA device plugin registers GPU resources
# kubectl describe node | grep nvidia.com/gpu
# Pod requests GPU:
#   resources:
#     limits:
#       nvidia.com/gpu: 1
#
# Device plugin also handles MIG profiles:
#   nvidia.com/mig-1g.10gb: 2

# === CUDA version compatibility ===
# nvidia-smi shows CUDA Version (driver API version)
# Container can use any CUDA version <= driver version
# Driver: 545.23.08 = supports CUDA 12.3 and older
# Container can run: CUDA 12.3, 12.2, 12.1, ..., 11.x, 10.x, etc.
# This is why Enterprise uses: driver on host, CUDA toolkit in container

# === GPU topology-aware scheduling ===
# NVLink-connected GPUs communicate faster than PCIe
# nvidia-smi topo -m shows connectivity
# Kubernetes: use 'nvidia.com/gpu.nvlink' label for topology-aware scheduling

# === GPU monitoring in containers ===
# Inside container:
dcgmi group -c all                     # Create DCGM group
dcgmi stats -g 1 -s true               # Enable stats collection
dcgmi dmon -e 100,101,102,200,201      # Key metrics: GPU util, mem util, temp, power,
PCIe

# Prometheus + DCGM Exporter:
# https://github.com/NVIDIA/dcgm-exporter
# Exposes: DCGM_FI_DEV_GPU_UTIL, DCGM_FI_DEV_MEM_COPY_UTIL, etc.

Interview Q&A

Q: 'Your training job is running at 30% GPU utilization. What do you check?'

A: 'Check nvidia-smi to confirm utilization. Then identify the bottleneck: is it data loading (CPU/IO bound), compute (kernel inefficient), or communication (NCCL sync). Use nvidia-smi dmon for per-GPU metrics, NSight Systems to profile the full pipeline. Common causes: (1) Data loader bottleneck — increase num_workers, use NVMe storage, enable pinned memory. (2) Small batch size — GPU finishes compute quickly, waits for next batch. (3) Gradient accumulation steps are inefficient. (4) Model has many small kernels with launch overhead — use torch.compile or CUDA graphs. (5) AllReduce communication overhead in multi-GPU — check NCCL settings and network bandwidth. At Enterprise, we have a standard 'GPU health check' script that profiles the full stack before every training run.'

Q: 'How do you choose MIG vs time-slicing vs full GPU for production inference?'

A: 'MIG provides hard isolation — guaranteed memory and compute, no interference. Use MIG when you need QoS guarantees (e.g., a latency-sensitive model sharing GPU with a throughput-oriented model). Time-slicing provides compute sharing but no memory isolation — a memory leak in one container can crash others. Use time-slicing for burstable workloads where occasional interference is acceptable. Full GPU is for large models that need all memory (LLMs, large Vision models) or for training. At Enterprise, we use MIG for production inference serving and time-slicing for development/staging environments where cost efficiency matters more than isolation.'

Q: 'An ML engineer says 'CUDA out of memory' on an A100 with 80GB, but the model should fit in 40GB. What do you check?'

A: 'Several possibilities: (1) Memory fragmentation — allocate largest tensors first, use memory pools. (2) Intermediate memory — activation memory can exceed parameter memory for large batch sizes. Reduce batch size or use gradient checkpointing. (3) Memory leak — tensors not freed between epochs (Python references holding GPU memory). Use torch.cuda.empty_cache() and check for reference cycles. (4) The engineer is not using MIG and another process is using the remaining memory — check nvidia-smi for other processes on that GPU. (5) NCCL buffers — NCCL allocates scratch memory for AllReduce. Set NCCL_BUFFSIZE or use environment variables. At Enterprise, we run nvidia-smi and then do a memory allocation test to find the exact available memory before launching training.'

References & Further Reading

  • nvidia-smi Documentation — Complete reference for GPU monitoring and management
  • NVIDIA Container Toolkit — GPU access in Docker and Kubernetes
  • NVIDIA Deep Learning Documentation — CUDA, cuDNN, TensorRT, and NGC catalog

Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage

Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime

Chapter 22

Linux Build & Toolchain

advancedAdvanced · 17 min read · 13 min practice

Every engineer compiles code — but understanding the toolchain (compiler, linker, loader, libraries, build systems) separates those who debug build issues in minutes from those who fight them for hours. At Enterprise, build toolchain knowledge is essential for: debugging linker errors, optimizing binary performance, reducing container image size, and working with hermetic build systems like Bazel.

faang-insightLinuxbuildtoolchaincompilerlinkerBazeloptimization

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux cli basics, linux shell basics, linux git

Learning Objectives

  • Understand the C Compilation Pipeline
  • Understand shared Libraries: SONAME, RPATH & LD_LIBRARY_PATH
  • Understand build Systems: Make, CMake, Bazel & Hermetic Builds
  • Understand lTO, PGO & Binary Optimization

Real-Life Analogy: Build Toolchain as a Factory

Compiler (gcc/clang) translates source to machine code. Linker connects object files into an executable. Make/CMake/Ninja are the production schedules. pkg-config finds dependency parts. ccache is the parts cache - reuse what you already built.

The C Compilation Pipeline

C/C++ compilation is a multi-stage pipeline: preprocessing (handle #include, #define, #ifdef), compilation (translate to assembly), assembly (translate to machine code/object file), linking (combine object files + libraries into executable). Understanding each stage helps you debug build errors and optimize build times.
bash
# === The four stages ===
# Stage 1: Preprocessing
gcc -E hello.c -o hello.i             # Preprocessed output (expanded macros, includes)

# Stage 2: Compilation
gcc -S hello.i -o hello.s             # Assembly output (human-readable)

# Stage 3: Assembly
gcc -c hello.s -o hello.o             # Object file (machine code, not executable)

# Stage 4: Linking
gcc hello.o -o hello                  # Executable (linked with libc, crt0, etc.)

# Or all at once:
gcc -Wall -O2 -o hello hello.c

# === Object file inspection ===
file hello.o                          # ELF 64-bit LSB relocatable, x86-64
nm hello.o                            # Symbol table (functions, globals)
nm -C hello.o                         # Demangled C++ names
objdump -d hello.o                    # Disassembly
objdump -t hello.o                    # Symbol table
objdump -x hello.o                    # All headers, sections, symbols

# === Shared library dependencies ===
ldd /usr/bin/python3                  # List shared library dependencies
# linux-vdso.so.1 — Virtual Dynamic Shared Object (kernel-provided)
# libpython3.10.so.1.0 — Python runtime
# libc.so.6 — GNU C Library (glibc)
# /lib64/ld-linux-x86-64.so.2 — Dynamic linker/loader

# === ELF sections ===
readelf -S hello.o                    # List sections (.text, .data, .bss, .rodata)
readelf -h hello.o                    # ELF header (architecture, entry point, flags)
readelf -l hello.o                    # Program headers (segments for loading)
# .text — executable code
# .data — initialized global/static variables
# .bss — uninitialized global variables (zero-initialized)
# .rodata — read-only data (string literals, const)
# .symtab — symbol table

# === Strip debugging symbols ===
strip hello                           # Remove symbols (smaller binary, harder to debug)
strip --strip-debug hello             # Keep global symbols, remove debug info
# Production: binaries are stripped, debug symbols are archived separately

Shared Libraries: SONAME, RPATH & LD_LIBRARY_PATH

Shared libraries (.so files) are loaded at runtime by the dynamic linker (ld-linux.so). The linker searches: RPATH (hardcoded in binary) → LD_LIBRARY_PATH → /etc/ld.so.cache → /lib, /usr/lib. Mismatched library versions cause the infamous 'symbol not found' errors. At Enterprise, RPATH is preferred over LD_LIBRARY_PATH for production — it's baked into the binary and can't be accidentally overridden.
bash
# === SONAME (Shared Object Name) ===
# Libraries have three names:
# 1. Real name: libcrypto.so.3.0.0 (full version)
# 2. SONAME: libcrypto.so.3 (compatible ABI version)
# 3. Linker name: libcrypto.so (no version, for -lcrypto flag)

# Check SONAME:
objdump -p /usr/lib/x86_64-linux-gnu/libcrypto.so.3 | grep SONAME
# SONAME is embedded in the library file
# ldconfig creates symlinks from SONAME to real name

# === RPATH vs RUNPATH ===
# RPATH: search path embedded in binary, searched BEFORE LD_LIBRARY_PATH
# RUNPATH: similar but searched AFTER LD_LIBRARY_PATH
# Prefer RUNPATH (allows overriding with LD_LIBRARY_PATH for debugging)

gcc -Wl,-rpath,'$ORIGIN/../lib' -o myapp myapp.o
# $ORIGIN expands to the directory containing the executable
# This is how portable app bundles work (e.g., AppImage)

# Check RPATH:
readelf -d myapp | grep -E "(RPATH|RUNPATH)"

# === LD_LIBRARY_PATH (use for development, not production) ===
export LD_LIBRARY_PATH=/opt/custom/lib:$LD_LIBRARY_PATH
./myapp                                 # Searches custom path first

# === ldconfig — update library cache ===
sudo ldconfig                          # Rebuild /etc/ld.so.cache
sudo ldconfig -p | grep nvda          # Search cached libraries by name

# === ld.so.conf — system-wide library paths ===
cat /etc/ld.so.conf                    # Include /etc/ld.so.conf.d/*.conf
ls /etc/ld.so.conf.d/
# Each .conf file adds a search path

# === Library versioning best practices ===
# libfoo.so.1 -> libfoo.so.1.0.0 (major.minor.patch)
# Major version change = ABI break
# Minor/patch = backward-compatible
# Never replace a library with incompatible ABI without changing SONAME

# === Static vs shared libraries ===
# Static (.a): linked into executable, larger binary, no runtime deps
# Shared (.so): loaded at runtime, smaller binary, memory sharing
# Production: mostly shared (container images share library pages)
# For small utilities: static (Go is fully static by default)

Build Systems: Make, CMake, Bazel & Hermetic Builds

At Enterprise, build systems are hermetic — isolated from the developer's machine, producing identical outputs from identical inputs. Bazel (Google), Buck (Meta), and Pants (Twitter) enforce dependency tracking, remote caching, and incremental builds. Understanding build system concepts — dependency graph, caching, sandboxing, remote execution — is essential for infrastructure engineers.
bash
# === Make ===
# Simple but limited: no dependency tracking, timestamp-based
# Every Production engineers should be able to read a Makefile

# === CMake ===
# Generates build files for different systems (Make, Ninja, Xcode, VS)
# CMakeLists.txt example:
# cmake_minimum_required(VERSION 3.20)
# project(myapp CXX)
# add_executable(myapp main.cpp util.cpp)
# target_link_libraries(myapp PRIVATE fmt)

cmake -B build -G Ninja .           # Configure (generate Ninja build files)
cmake --build build                 # Build

# === Bazel (hermetic build system — Google's standard) ===
# BUILD file:
# cc_binary(
#     name = "myapp",
#     srcs = ["main.cc", "util.cc"],
#     deps = [":mylib", "@fmt//:fmt"],
# )

bazel build //:myapp                 # Build (hermetic, cached, distributed)
bazel test //:tests                  # Run tests
bazel query 'deps(//:myapp)'         # Show dependency graph

# Hermetic build principles:
# 1. All inputs declared (source, tools, environment)
# 2. No network access during build
# 3. No access to /usr, /opt, etc.
# 4. Outputs depend only on inputs (no timestamps, no hostname)
# 5. Remote caching: rebuild is instant if inputs haven't changed

# === Ninja (fast incremental builds) ===
ninja -C build                       # Faster than make (parallel by default)

# === ccache (compiler cache) ===
export CCACHE_DIR=~/.ccache
export PATH=/usr/lib/ccache:$PATH    # Transparent compiler caching
# Second build of same file: instant (cache hit)

LTO, PGO & Binary Optimization

Modern compilers offer profile-guided and link-time optimizations that can improve performance by 10-30%. LTO (Link-Time Optimization) enables cross-module inlining and optimization. PGO (Profile-Guided Optimization) uses runtime profiling data to guide optimization decisions. At Enterprise, PGO is standard for critical services — the performance gain in compute-heavy workloads justifies the build complexity.
bash
# === LTO (Link-Time Optimization) ===
# Traditional: compile each .c file separately, then link
# LTO: compile to intermediate representation (IR), optimize at link time

gcc -flto -O2 -o myapp file1.c file2.c   # LTO enabled
# Benefits: cross-module inlining, devirtualization, dead code elimination
# Cost: slower linking (~2x), more memory during link

# === PGO (Profile-Guided Optimization) ===
# Step 1: Compile with instrumentation
gcc -fprofile-generate -O2 -o myapp.prof myapp.c

# Step 2: Run with representative workload
./myapp.prof < representative_input > /dev/null
# Generates .gcda files with profiling data

# Step 3: Recompile with profile data
gcc -fprofile-use -O2 -o myapp myapp.c
# Now: branch prediction, inlining, and loop optimization use real profiling data

# AutoFDO (Linux 5.x+): use perf data instead of instrumented binary
# perf record -b ./myapp  # Branch-recorded profiling
# create_gcov --profile=perf.data --gcov=myapp.gcov ./myapp
# gcc -fprofile-use=myapp.gcov -O2 -o myapp myapp.c

# === Size optimization ===
gcc -Os -o small_binary myapp.c          # Optimize for size (embedded, containers)
# Compare: -O2 (speed) vs -Os (size) — typical difference is 30-50%

# === Debug symbols ===
gcc -g -o myapp.debug myapp.c            # Full debug symbols
gcc -g1 -o myapp.debug myapp.c           # Minimal debug symbols (line numbers only)
objcopy --only-keep-debug myapp.debug myapp.dbg   # Extract debug info
objcopy --strip-debug myapp.debug myapp   # Strip for production
# Enterprise pattern: separate debug symbols (myapp.dbg) uploaded to artifact store

# === Binary size analysis ===
size myapp                               # text, data, bss, total
bloaty myapp                             # Symbol-level size breakdown (install: apt
install bloaty)
nm myapp | sort -k2,2 | uniq -c | sort -rn | head  # Largest symbol categories

Interview Q&A

Q: 'Your program compiles fine on your machine but fails with 'cannot open shared object file' on the server. Why?'

A: 'Missing shared library or wrong search path. Check with ldd myapp to find the missing library. Common fixes: (1) Install the library on the server (apt install). (2) Set LD_LIBRARY_PATH. (3) Link with RPATH/RUNPATH embedded in the binary. (4) The library version on the server is too old — check SONAME compatibility. At Enterprise, this is solved by containers — the binary and all its shared libraries are in the container image, guaranteed to match.'

Q: 'What's the difference between dynamic and static linking, and when would you use each?'

A: 'Dynamic linking loads libraries at runtime — smaller binaries, memory sharing between processes, and library updates without recompiling. Downside: dependency hell (wrong library version = crash). Static linking embeds everything into the binary — no runtime dependencies, fully portable, but larger binaries and no library updates without rebuilding. At Enterprise: most services use dynamic linking within containers (container = static deployment unit, libraries inside are fixed). Go uses fully static linking by default. For C/C++, static linking is preferred for small command-line tools and for Alpine-based containers (musl libc vs glibc compatibility issues).'

Q: 'How would you set up a build system for a monorepo with 1000 microservices?'

A: 'Use a hermetic build system like Bazel. Key requirements: (1) Explicit dependency declarations — every build target declares its deps. (2) Remote caching — builds reuse cached outputs from CI, not just local. (3) Remote execution — distribute build across hundreds of machines. (4) Incremental builds — changing one service rebuilds only that service and its dependents. (5) Sandboxed builds — no access to /usr, no network during build. (6) Multi-language support — Java, C++, Python, Go, Rust all in one repo. (7) CI integration — build and test only what changed (affected target detection). At Enterprise, this scale of build system is a dedicated team's responsibility — the build infrastructure is as critical as the production infrastructure.'

References & Further Reading

  • Linkers & Loaders — John Levine, the definitive reference on linking
  • Bazel Build System — Google's build system documentation
  • GCC Optimization Options — Official GCC optimization documentation

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes
Mistake: Checking /proc/meminfo inside containers for memory
Solution: Use cgroup v2 files: /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current, memory.events

Troubleshooting

Symptom: gcc returns unexpected output
Cause: Incorrect arguments, wrong working directory, or stale cached data
Solution: Verify arguments with `man gcc`; check current path with `pwd`; clear cache if applicable
Symptom: Configuration changes not taking effect
Cause: Service not reloaded or syntax error in config file
Solution: Validate config syntax with built-in test flag; reload service with `systemctl reload` or `restart`
Symptom: build toolchain operation slow or timing out
Cause: Resource exhaustion (disk I/O, memory pressure, or lock contention)
Solution: Check `iostat`, `free -m`, and `top`; look for stuck processes with `ps aux --sort=-%cpu`
Symptom: Permission issues accessing build toolchain resources
Cause: Incorrect file ownership or overly restrictive permissions
Solution: Inspect with `ls -la`; fix with `chown`/ `chmod`; check ACLs with `getfacl`

Quick Quiz

Q1: What is The C Compilation Pipeline?

A: A standardized directory structure that defines where files and directories should be placed on Linux systems

Q2: What does `gcc` do?

A: Lists files and directories in the current or specified path

Q3: Which command would you use to view disk usage of the build toolchain directory?

A: du -sh /path/to/directory

Q4: A user reports "Permission denied" when trying to access a build toolchain directory. What is the first command you should run?

A: ls -ld /path/to/directory

Q5: In a build toolchain scenario, your monitoring alerts show gcc failing intermittently. What is the correct debugging workflow?

A: Check logs → verify configuration → test in staging → apply fix → monitor
