Chapter 23

cgroups & Namespaces

intermediateContainers & SRE · 19 min read · 14 min practice

Control groups (cgroups) and namespaces are the two Linux kernel features that make containers possible. cgroups limit resource usage (CPU, memory, I/O, PID count); namespaces isolate process views (filesystem, network, PID, user, mount). At Enterprise, every process runs inside cgroups — from individual containers to batch jobs to user shells in shared environments.

faang-insightLinuxcgroupsnamespacescontainersDockerKubernetes

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux processes, linux kernel

Learning Objectives

  • Understand namespaces: The Illusion of Isolation
  • Understand cgroups v2: Resource Accounting & Limiting
  • Understand docker & Containers: cgroups + Namespaces in Practice
  • Understand debugging Resource Issues with cgroups

Real-Life Analogy: Cgroups as Resource Quotas

Cgroups are budgets. cpu.max = "you get this much CPU time." memory.max = "you cannot exceed this RAM." pids.max = "you can only hire this many workers." Containers are processes with budgets enforced by the kernel.

Namespaces: The Illusion of Isolation

Namespaces give each process its own view of the system. A process in a mount namespace sees only its own filesystem. A process in a network namespace sees only its own network interfaces. A process in a PID namespace sees only its own process tree. There are 8 namespace types. Docker creates a new set of namespaces for each container.
bash
# === Namespace types ===
# Mount (mnt)     — Isolate filesystem mount points
# PID (pid)       — Isolate process IDs
# Network (net)   — Isolate network interfaces, routes, iptables
# IPC (ipc)       — Isolate System V IPC and POSIX message queues
# UTS (uts)       — Isolate hostname and domain name
# User (user)     — Isolate UID and GID mappings
# Cgroup (cgroup) — Isolate cgroup root
# Time (time)     — Isolate system time (Linux 5.6+)

# === Check namespaces of a process ===
ls -l /proc/1/ns/                    # Namespace inodes for PID 1
# lrwxrwxrwx 1 root root 0 ... mnt -> mnt:[4026531840]
# lrwxrwxrwx 1 root root 0 ... net -> net:[4026531840]
# Same inode numbers = same namespace

# === Compare namespaces ===
ls -l /proc/<PID1>/ns/net /proc/<PID2>/ns/net  # Same? Same network namespace

# === Create a new namespace (unshare) ===
# New mount namespace (isolated filesystem):
sudo unshare --mount --fork /bin/bash
# Inside: mount is isolated — changes don't affect host

# New PID namespace:
sudo unshare --pid --fork --mount-proc /bin/bash
# Inside: only sees processes in this namespace

# New network namespace:
sudo ip netns add myns              # Create network namespace
sudo ip netns exec myns ip addr     # Run command inside namespace
sudo ip netns delete myns           # Delete namespace

# === Network namespace use: per-process networking ===
# Create veth pair to connect namespaces:
sudo ip link add veth0 type veth peer name veth1
sudo ip link set veth1 netns myns
sudo ip addr add 10.0.0.1/24 dev veth0
sudo ip netns exec myns ip addr add 10.0.0.2/24 dev veth1
sudo ip link set veth0 up
sudo ip netns exec myns ip link set veth1 up
# Now traffic flows between host and namespace

# === List all network namespaces ===
sudo ip netns list
# Each Docker container gets its own network namespace

# === Enter a container's namespace ===
# Get container PID:
docker inspect <container> | grep Pid
# Enter namespaces:
sudo nsenter -t <PID> -n /bin/bash   # Enter network namespace
sudo nsenter -t <PID> -p /bin/bash   # Enter PID namespace
sudo nsenter -t <PID> -a /bin/bash   # Enter ALL namespaces

cgroups v2: Resource Accounting & Limiting

cgroups v2 (unified hierarchy) is the modern cgroup implementation. Every process belongs to a cgroup, and the kernel enforces resource limits at the cgroup level. At Enterprise, cgroups v2 is the standard on modern Linux distros (Ubuntu 22.04+, Amazon Linux 2023, RHEL 9+). cgroups v1 is legacy but still present for backward compatibility.
bash
# === cgroups v2 hierarchy ===
# Mounted at /sys/fs/cgroup/
ls /sys/fs/cgroup/
# cpu.max, cpu.stat          — CPU limits
# memory.max, memory.current — Memory limits
# io.max, io.stat            — I/O limits
# pids.max                   — PID limit

# === Create and configure a cgroup ===
# On systemd-based systems, use systemd:
sudo systemd-run --unit=testapp --scope -p CPUQuota=50% -p MemoryMax=256M sleep 300

# Or direct cgroup manipulation:
sudo mkdir /sys/fs/cgroup/myapp

# CPU limit: 50% of one core (50,000/100,000 μs)
echo "50000 100000" | sudo tee /sys/fs/cgroup/myapp/cpu.max

# Memory limit: 256 MB
echo "268435456" | sudo tee /sys/fs/cgroup/myapp/memory.max

# Memory + swap limit: 512 MB
echo "536870912" | sudo tee /sys/fs/cgroup/myapp/memory.swap.max

# PID limit: 100
echo "100" | sudo tee /sys/fs/cgroup/myapp/pids.max

# I/O limit: 10MB/s write
echo "8:0 wbps=10485760" | sudo tee /sys/fs/cgroup/myapp/io.max
# 8:0 = major:minor of the block device

# === Add process to cgroup ===
echo $PID | sudo tee /sys/fs/cgroup/myapp/cgroup.procs

# === Check cgroup stats ===
cat /sys/fs/cgroup/myapp/cpu.stat
# usage_usec  — CPU time used
# nr_periods  — number of enforcement periods
# nr_throttled — periods where tasks were throttled
# throttled_usec — total throttled time

cat /sys/fs/cgroup/myapp/memory.current
# Current memory usage in bytes

cat /sys/fs/cgroup/myapp/memory.events
# low, high, max, oom, oom_kill — event counters
# If 'max > 0', the cgroup hit its memory limit
# If 'oom_kill > 0', the OOM killer was triggered

# === Pressure Stall Information (PSI) ===
cat /sys/fs/cgroup/cpu.pressure       # CPU pressure
cat /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.pressure    # Memory pressure
cat /sys/fs/cgroup/io.pressure        # I/O pressure
# Format: some avg10=0.00 avg60=0.00 avg300=0.00 total=0
# 'some' — % of time at least one task was stalled
# 'full' — % of time all tasks were stalled
# PSI is the key metric for resource contention at Enterprise

Docker & Containers: cgroups + Namespaces in Practice

Docker combines cgroups (for resource limits) and namespaces (for isolation). When you run 'docker run -m 256M --cpus=0.5 nginx', Docker creates a new cgroup in /sys/fs/cgroup/system.slice/docker-.scope/ and configures the limits. Understanding this mapping is essential for debugging container resource issues.
bash
# === Docker cgroup hierarchy ===
# Docker containers live under:
/sys/fs/cgroup/system.slice/docker-<container-id>.scope/

# Inside a container:
cat /proc/1/cgroup                     # Shows container's cgroup path
# 0::/system.slice/docker-abc123.scope

# From host, inspect container cgroup:
CONTAINER_ID=$(docker ps -q --no-trunc | head -1)
cat /sys/fs/cgroup/system.slice/docker-$CONTAINER_ID.scope/memory.current

# === Docker resource limits vs cgroup ===
docker run --memory=256M --cpus=0.5 --pids-limit=100 nginx
# Maps to:
# memory.max = 268435456
# cpu.max = 50000 100000
# pids.max = 100

# === Common container resource issues ===
# 1. Container sees host memory (cat /proc/meminfo shows host, not cgroup)
#    Fix: Use cgroup-aware tools or read /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current
#
# 2. Java/Golang apps ignore cgroup limits
#    Fix: Use JDK 10+ (cgroup-aware), Go 1.19+ (GOMEMLIMIT)
#
# 3. OOM inside container doesn't show in host dmesg
#    Fix: Check memory.events inside container cgroup

# === cgroups v2 in Kubernetes ===
# Kubernetes 1.24+ uses cgroups v2 by default
# kubelet creates cgroups under /sys/fs/cgroup/kubepods/
ls /sys/fs/cgroup/kubepods/             # All pod cgroups
ls /sys/fs/cgroup/kubepods/burstable/   # Best-effort pods
ls /sys/fs/cgroup/kubepods/guaranteed/  # Guaranteed QoS pods

# === systemd resource control ===
# systemd services run as scopes under:
/sys/fs/cgroup/system.slice/<service-name>.service/

# Example: nginx service cgroup
cat /sys/fs/cgroup/system.slice/nginx.service/cpu.max
cat /sys/fs/cgroup/system.slice/nginx.service/memory.max

Debugging Resource Issues with cgroups

When a container or service is hitting resource limits, cgroup statistics tell you exactly what's happening. At Enterprise, cgroup-level metrics are the primary source of truth for resource usage — more reliable than /proc/meminfo (which shows host-level data).
bash
# === Is the process being throttled? ===
cat /sys/fs/cgroup/system.slice/myapp.service/cpu.stat
# nr_throttled > 0 = CPU throttled (hitting CPU limit)
# throttled_usec = total time spent throttled

# === Is the process hitting memory limit? ===
cat /sys/fs/cgroup/system.slice/myapp.service/memory.events
# low: 0         — hitting memory.low threshold
# high: 0        — hitting memory.high (reclaiming aggressively)
# max: 5         — hit memory.max (OOM kill threshold!)
# oom: 5         — OOM events
# oom_kill: 5    — processes killed by OOM

# === Current memory breakdown ===
cat /sys/fs/cgroup/system.slice/myapp.service/memory.stat
# anon — anonymous (heap, stack)
# file — file-backed (page cache)
# kernel — kernel memory (slab, page tables)

# === Is the process hitting PID limit? ===
cat /sys/fs/cgroup/system.slice/myapp.service/pids.current
cat /sys/fs/cgroup/system.slice/myapp.service/pids.max
# If pids.current == pids.max, the process is hitting PID limits
# Common for: forking servers, thread leaks

# === Is I/O being throttled? ===
cat /sys/fs/cgroup/system.slice/myapp.service/io.stat
# rbytes, wbytes — bytes read/written
# rios, wios — I/O operations

# === PSI (Pressure Stall Info) ===
cat /sys/fs/cgroup/system.slice/myapp.service/memory.pressure
# some avg10=5.00 — 5% of time at least one task was stalled on memory
# If avg10 > 10%, you're under memory pressure

# === One-liner: Check all limits for a container ===
CONTAINER_CGROUP=$(docker inspect --format '{{.Id}}' $CONTAINER | cut -c1-12)
CGROUP_PATH="/sys/fs/cgroup/system.slice/docker-$CONTAINER_CGROUP.scope"
echo "=== CPU ==="
cat $CGROUP_PATH/cpu.max
echo "=== Memory ==="
cat $CGROUP_PATH/memory.current
cat $CGROUP_PATH/memory.events
echo "=== PIDs ==="
cat $CGROUP_PATH/pids.current
cat $CGROUP_PATH/pids.max

Interview Q&A

Q: 'Explain how Docker containers work under the hood.'

A: 'Docker containers use Linux namespaces for isolation and cgroups for resource limits. When Docker starts a container: (1) It creates new namespaces (mount, PID, network, IPC, UTS, user) via the clone() syscall. (2) It sets up the container's filesystem using a layered image (overlayfs) and chroots into it (pivot_root). (3) It creates cgroups under /sys/fs/cgroup/system.slice/ and sets CPU, memory, I/O, and PID limits. (4) It sets up the network (veth pair → bridge or overlay). (5) It applies seccomp and LSMA (AppArmor/SELinux) profiles. (6) It execs the container's CMD inside these namespaces. The container doesn't 'contain' anything — it's just a regular Linux process that can't see anything outside its namespaces and can't use more resources than its cgroup allows.'

Q: 'A container is running out of memory, but free -h inside the container shows plenty of free memory. Why?'

A: 'Because free and /proc/meminfo inside a container show host-level memory, not the cgroup limit — unless the container runtime correctly mounts the cgroup filesystem. On older Docker/Cgroup v1 setups, /proc/meminfo is not virtualized. The container is hitting the cgroup memory limit even though /proc/meminfo says there's free memory. Always check /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current and /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.events inside the container for the real story. Modern runtimes (containerd, cgroups v2) handle this better, but it's still a common pitfall.'

Q: 'How do you troubleshoot a Node.js container that's hitting CPU throttling?'

A: 'Check cat /sys/fs/cgroup/cpu.max to see the CPU limit. Check cat /sys/fs/cgroup/cpu.stat for nr_throttled > 0. If throttled, either increase the CPU limit or optimize the application. Use perf top inside the container to identify hot functions. Check if the event loop is blocked — Node.js runs on a single thread, so CPU throttling directly impacts throughput. If the container is a sidecar or batch job, consider that its burstable CPU might be competing with other containers on the same pod/node. At Enterprise, we use cgroup-aware CPU metrics for autoscaling decisions — not host CPU metrics.'

References & Further Reading

  • cgroups v2 Documentation — Kernel.org official documentation
  • namespaces(7) man page — Complete namespace reference
  • Container Architectures (InfoQ) — Jérôme Petazzoni's deep dive on container internals

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes
Mistake: Checking /proc/meminfo inside containers for memory
Solution: Use cgroup v2 files: /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current, memory.events

Troubleshooting

Symptom: ls returns unexpected output
Cause: Incorrect arguments, wrong working directory, or stale cached data
Solution: Verify arguments with `man ls`; check current path with `pwd`; clear cache if applicable
Symptom: Configuration changes not taking effect
Cause: Service not reloaded or syntax error in config file
Solution: Validate config syntax with built-in test flag; reload service with `systemctl reload` or `restart`
Symptom: cgroups operation slow or timing out
Cause: Resource exhaustion (disk I/O, memory pressure, or lock contention)
Solution: Check `iostat`, `free -m`, and `top`; look for stuck processes with `ps aux --sort=-%cpu`
Symptom: Permission issues accessing cgroups resources
Cause: Incorrect file ownership or overly restrictive permissions
Solution: Inspect with `ls -la`; fix with `chown`/ `chmod`; check ACLs with `getfacl`

Q2: What does `ls` do?

A: Lists files and directories in the current or specified path

Q3: Which command would you use to view disk usage of the cgroups directory?

A: Check logs → verify configuration → test in staging → apply fix → monitor

Chapter 24

Docker & Container Lifecycle

intermediateContainers & SRE · 17 min read · 13 min practice

Docker is the most widely used container runtime, built on Linux cgroups and namespaces. At Enterprise, Docker (or its OCI-compatible alternatives like containerd) is the standard unit of deployment. Understanding the container lifecycle — from building to running to debugging — is essential for any infrastructure engineer.

faang-insightLinuxDockercontainersOCIcontainer lifecycle

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux cgroups, linux networking, linux filesystem

Learning Objectives

  • Understand container Images: Layers & Build Caching
  • Understand container Runtime & Lifecycle
  • Understand networking & Storage in Docker
  • Understand debugging Containers

Real-Life Analogy: Docker as a Shipping Container

The image is a sealed container - same contents everywhere. Layers are stacked pallets - reuse common bases. The Dockerfile is the packing list. The daemon is the port authority. Namespaces isolate "this container sees only its own cargo."

Container Images: Layers & Build Caching

A Docker image is a stack of read-only layers, each representing a change to the filesystem. Layers are cached and shared between images — this is why pulling updates is fast (only new layers are downloaded). At Enterprise, image layer optimization is critical for deployment speed: minimizing layers, caching dependencies, and using distroless base images reduce both build time and attack surface.
bash
# === Dockerfile best practices ===

# 1. Use specific base image versions (not 'latest')
FROM ubuntu:22.04@sha256:abc123...

# 2. Combine RUN commands to minimize layers
RUN apt-get update && apt-get install -y \\
    python3 python3-pip \\
    && rm -rf /var/lib/apt/lists/*

# 3. Use .dockerignore (like .gitignore for builds)
# .dockerignore:
# node_modules
# .git
# *.log

# 4. Multi-stage builds (separate build deps from runtime)
FROM node:22 AS builder
WORKDIR /app
COPY package*.json ./
RUN npm ci --only=production

FROM node:22-alpine AS runtime
COPY --from=builder /app /app
USER node
CMD ["node", "server.js"]

# 5. Use distroless or alpine for smaller images
# Distroless: gcr.io/distroless/nodejs22-debian12
# Alpine: node:22-alpine (much smaller, but uses musl libc)

# 6. Don't run as root inside the container
RUN addgroup -S myapp && adduser -S myapp -G myapp
USER myapp

# === Build optimization ===
# Cache layers that change infrequently (package.json → deps first, then source)
COPY package*.json ./
RUN npm ci            # This layer only rebuilds when package.json changes
COPY . .              # This layer rebuilds every time (frequent changes)

# === Layer inspection ===
docker history nginx              # Show layer sizes and commands
docker inspect nginx              # Detailed image metadata (layers, env, ports)

# === Image size commands ===
docker images --format "table {{.Repository}}\t{{.Tag}}\t{{.Size}}"
docker system df                  # Total disk usage of Docker

Container Runtime & Lifecycle

When Docker runs a container, it goes through: pull image → create container → create namespaces → set up cgroups → mount rootfs → apply seccomp → exec CMD. Understanding this sequence helps you debug startup failures and performance issues. At Enterprise, the container runtime is usually containerd (the industry-standard OCI runtime) with runc as the low-level runtime.
bash
# === Container lifecycle commands ===
# Build:
docker build -t myapp:latest .       # Build image from Dockerfile
docker build --no-cache -t myapp .   # Force rebuild (ignore cache)

# Run:
docker run -d --name myapp -p 8080:80 myapp:latest
docker run -it --rm ubuntu:22.04 bash  # Interactive, auto-remove

# Stop/start:
docker stop myapp                    # SIGTERM + wait + SIGKILL
docker start myapp                   # Restart stopped container
docker restart myapp                 # Stop + start
docker kill myapp                    # SIGKILL immediately

# Inspect:
docker ps                            # Running containers
docker ps -a                         # All containers (including stopped)
docker logs -f myapp                 # Follow logs
docker logs --tail 100 myapp         # Last 100 lines
docker inspect myapp                 # Full container metadata (JSON)

# Execute in running container:
docker exec -it myapp bash           # Start shell in container
docker exec myapp ps aux             # Run command in container

# === Resource limits ===
docker run -d --name db \
  --memory=512M \
  --memory-reservation=256M \
  --cpus=0.5 \
  --pids-limit=200 \
  --ulimit nofile=65536:65536 \
  postgres:16

# === Health checks ===
# In Dockerfile:
HEALTHCHECK --interval=30s --timeout=3s --retries=3 \
  CMD curl -f http://localhost:8080/health || exit 1

# Manual check:
docker inspect --format='{{.State.Health.Status}}' myapp

# === Copy files ===
docker cp myapp:/var/log/app.log ./local.log
docker cp ./config.yaml myapp:/etc/app/config.yaml

# === Cleanup ===
docker rm myapp                      # Remove container
docker rmi myapp:latest              # Remove image
docker system prune -a               # Remove ALL unused containers, images, networks

Networking & Storage in Docker

Docker provides several network drivers (bridge for single-host, overlay for multi-host, host for performance, macvlan for direct MAC assignment) and storage options (volumes for persistent data, bind mounts for host directories, tmpfs for temporary memory-backed storage). At Enterprise, overlay networks (Cilium, Flannel, Calico) handle container networking, and cloud block storage (EBS volumes) is mounted via bind mounts.
bash
# === Network drivers ===
# Bridge (default): containers connect via docker0 bridge
docker network create mynet          # Create custom bridge network
docker run --network=mynet nginx

# Host: container uses host's network stack (no isolation, best performance)
docker run --network=host nginx

# Overlay: multi-host networking (Docker Swarm or Kubernetes)
docker network create -d overlay myoverlay

# === Port mapping ===
docker run -p 8080:80 nginx          # host:8080 → container:80

# === DNS within Docker ===
# Containers on same user-defined bridge can resolve each other by name
# docker run --name web --network=mynet nginx
# docker run --network=mynet alpine ping web  # Works!

# === Volumes ===
docker volume create mydata           # Create volume
docker run -v mydata:/var/lib/data postgres  # Mount volume

# Bind mount:
docker run -v /host/path:/container/path nginx

# tmpfs (RAM storage):
docker run --tmpfs /tmp:size=100M nginx

# === Backup volume ===
docker run --rm -v mydata:/source -v $(pwd):/backup alpine tar czf /backup/mydata.tar.gz
-C /source .

# === Storage drivers ===
# overlay2 (default, modern) — Copy-on-Write, fast
# aufs (legacy) — Older Ubuntu default
# devicemapper — RHEL legacy (slow, avoid)
docker info | grep Storage

# === Container resource inspection ===
docker stats                        # Live resource usage
# CPU%, MEM%, MEM USAGE/LIMIT, NET I/O, BLOCK I/O, PIDS

docker stats --no-stream --format "table {{.Name}}\t{{.CPUPerc}}\t{{.MemUsage}}"

Debugging Containers

Containers introduce debugging challenges: you're running in an isolated environment with limited tools. At Enterprise, debugging containers requires understanding both the application inside and the host outside. Key tools: docker exec for inside, nsenter for entering namespaces from host, and cgroup inspections for resource issues.
bash
# === Inside-container debugging ===
docker exec -it myapp bash            # Shell inside container

# Install debugging tools (temporary container):
docker run --rm -it --net=container:myapp --pid=container:myapp \
  nicolaka/netshoot:latest            # Network troubleshooting toolbox

# === Host-level debugging ===
# Find container PID:
CONTAINER_PID=$(docker inspect --format '{{.State.Pid}}' myapp)
echo $CONTAINER_PID

# Enter container namespaces from host:
sudo nsenter -t $CONTAINER_PID -n ip addr  # Network namespace
sudo nsenter -t $CONTAINER_PID -p ps aux   # PID namespace
sudo nsenter -t $CONTAINER_PID -a bash     # All namespaces

# Check container cgroup:
CGROUP_PATH=$(cat /proc/$CONTAINER_PID/cgroup | grep -oP
'/system.slice/docker-.*\.scope')
cat /sys/fs/cgroup$CGROUP_PATH/memory.events

# === Container logs ===
docker logs myapp                     # All logs
docker logs --tail 50 --follow --timestamps myapp

# === Check container filesystem ===
docker diff myapp                     # Show changed files (A=added, C=changed,
D=deleted)

# === Export container filesystem ===
docker export myapp > container.tar   # Export as tar archive
docker import container.tar           # Import as image

# === Debug entrypoint/cmd ===
# Override entrypoint to debug:
docker run -it --entrypoint sh myapp:latest

# === Common issues ===
# 1. Container exits immediately: check CMD/ENTRYPOINT and logs
# 2. Port already in use: change host port mapping
# 3. Out of disk space: docker system prune -a
# 4. Permission denied inside: check USER directive and volume permissions
# 5. DNS resolution fails: check --dns or /etc/docker/daemon.json

Interview Q&A

Q: 'Your containerized application is running slow in production. How do you debug?'

A: 'First, rule out resource limits. Check docker stats or the cgroup metrics: cat /sys/fs/cgroup/cpu.stat (throttling), memory.events (OOM), io.stat (I/O throttling). If the container is hitting limits, increase them or optimize the app. Next, check the host: top for CPU, iostat for disk, free -h for memory. If the host is fine, debug inside the container: docker exec -it myapp bash, then use standard Linux tools (top, strace, lsof). If the container lacks tools, use nsenter from the host. At Enterprise, we use ephemeral debug containers — a sidecar with network and PID namespace sharing that contains all debugging tools, without modifying the production container.'

Q: 'How would you design a container image for production?'

A: 'Production container image requirements: (1) Use a specific base image version, pinned by digest, not tag. (2) Multi-stage build: builder stage with build tools, runtime stage with only the binary and minimal dependencies. (3) Non-root user — never run as root. (4) HEALTHCHECK instruction. (5) No secrets in the image — use Docker build secrets or runtime secrets injection. (6) Minimal size: use distroless or Alpine base, combined RUN layers, clean package manager cache. (7) Reproducible: exact same image for the same git commit. (8) Signed: use Docker Content Trust or cosign for image signing. At Enterprise, image compliance is enforced by admission controllers in the container registry — images without a HEALTHCHECK, running as root, or with known vulnerabilities are rejected.'

Q: 'What's the difference between CMD and ENTRYPOINT in a Dockerfile?'

A: 'ENTRYPOINT defines the executable that always runs. CMD provides default arguments to ENTRYPOINT. ENTRYPOINT ["nginx"] + CMD ["-g", "daemon off;"] = runs nginx -g 'daemon off;'. If you override CMD at runtime (docker run nginx -t), it becomes nginx -t (CMD replaced). If you override ENTRYPOINT (docker run --entrypoint bash nginx), it runs bash instead of nginx. Best practice: use ENTRYPOINT for the fixed binary and CMD for the default arguments — this makes the container behavior predictable while allowing argument customization.'

References & Further Reading

  • Dockerfile Best Practices — Official Docker documentation
  • Docker Compose — Multi-container orchestration (development & testing)
  • containerd documentation — Industry-standard container runtime used in Kubernetes

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Running destructive commands without verification
Solution: Always use `rm -i` or `ls` first to confirm what will be deleted
Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes

Chapter 25

Kubernetes for Linux Engineers

advancedContainers & SRE · 17 min read · 13 min practice

Kubernetes is a Linux cluster operating system. Every Kubernetes node runs Linux, and every container is a Linux process. Understanding Kubernetes from the Linux perspective — kubelet, CRI, CNI, CSI, cgroups, namespaces, eBPF — transforms how you debug cluster issues and design resilient systems. At Enterprise, this Linux-first understanding is what separates infrastructure engineers who 'manage YAML' from those who 'operate clusters'.

faang-insightLinuxKuberneteskubeletCRICNICSIcontainerseBPF

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux docker, linux networking, linux systemd

Learning Objectives

  • Understand kubelet: The Node Agent
  • Understand cRI: Container Runtime Interface
  • Understand cNI: Container Networking Interface
  • Understand cSI: Container Storage Interface

Real-Life Analogy: Kubernetes as a Port Authority

Pods are containers sharing a dock. Services are stable addresses for shifting pods. Deployments manage rolling updates. The scheduler assigns pods to nodes (ships). Controllers (replicasets) maintain desired state. etcd is the manifest - the source of truth.

Kubelet: The Node Agent

The kubelet is the primary node agent — it registers the node with the control plane, watches for pod assignments, and ensures containers are running. It interacts with the container runtime via CRI (Container Runtime Interface). At Enterprise, the kubelet is configured with custom flags for node-level resource reservation, pod eviction thresholds, and garbage collection — all critical for cluster stability.
bash
# === Kubelet on the node ===
ps aux | grep kubelet                  # Check kubelet process and flags

# Key kubelet flags:
# --node-ip=10.0.0.5                    # Node's internal IP
# --container-runtime-endpoint=unix:///run/containerd/containerd.sock
# --kubeconfig=/var/lib/kubelet/kubeconfig
# --register-node=true
# --node-status-update-frequency=10s
# --eviction-hard=memory.available<100Mi,nodefs.available<10%
# --system-reserved=cpu=200m,memory=512Mi  # Resources reserved for system
# --kube-reserved=cpu=200m,memory=512Mi    # Resources reserved for kubelet itself

# === Kubelet logs ===
journalctl -u kubelet -n 100 --no-pager  # Recent kubelet logs
journalctl -u kubelet -f                 # Follow kubelet logs

# === Kubelet state directory ===
ls /var/lib/kubelet/
# pod-manifests/        — Static pod YAML files
# device-plugins/       — GPU/device plugin sockets
# pods/                 — Pod sandbox directories (one per pod)
# cpu_manager_state     — CPU manager state (static CPU pinning)

# === Pod sandbox on disk ===
ls /var/lib/kubelet/pods/<POD_UID>/
# containers/           — container logs and state
# volumes/              — mounted volumes
# etc-hosts             — pod-level /etc/hosts

# === Check node capacity ===
kubectl describe node <node> | grep -A 10 "Capacity"
# cpu, memory, pods, ephemeral-storage, hugepages
# Also: nvidia.com/gpu, nvidia.com/mig-1g.10gb (device plugin resources)

# === Kubelet garbage collection ===
# Image GC: remove unused images when disk > threshold
# Container GC: remove terminated containers
# Eviction: kill pods when node resources are low
# Kubelet flags:
# --image-gc-high-threshold=85         # Start GC when disk > 85%
# --image-gc-low-threshold=80          # Stop GC when disk < 80%
# --maximum-dead-containers-per-container=5

CRI: Container Runtime Interface

The CRI abstracts the container runtime behind a gRPC API. containerd is the default runtime (used by Docker, EKS, GKE, AKS). cri-o is a Kubernetes-native runtime optimized for CRI. runc is the low-level OCI runtime. At Enterprise, containerd is standard — but some workloads use alternative runtimes for security (gVisor, Kata Containers) or performance (crun in Rust).
bash
# === Check container runtime ===
kubectl get nodes -o wide | awk '{print $1, $NF}'
# Shows container-runtime version for each node

# === containerd (most common) ===
# containerd socket:
ls /run/containerd/containerd.sock

# containerd commands:
ctr images list                       # List images in containerd
ctr containers list                   # List containers
ctr tasks list                        # List running tasks
ctr image pull docker.io/library/nginx:latest

# === crictl (CRI debug tool — ESSENTIAL) ===
crictl pods                           # List pods (CRI-level)
crictl ps                             # List containers
crictl images                         # List images
crictl inspect <CONTAINER_ID>         # Detailed container info
crictl logs <CONTAINER_ID>            # Container logs (bypasses kubectl)
crictl exec -it <CONTAINER_ID> sh    # Exec into container
crictl stats                          # Resource usage (CPU, memory)

# === crictl vs docker ===
# crictl is the CRI-native replacement for docker CLI
# Use crictl when docker CLI is not available (containerd-only nodes)
# Same concepts: pods=, containers=, images=

# === Low-level OCI runtime ===
# runc: the reference OCI runtime
# Creates cgroups + namespaces + mounts for each container
# Check which runtime:
runc --version

# === Runtime alternatives ===
# crun (Rust) — faster than runc, used by Red Hat/OpenShift
# gVisor (Google) — user-space kernel, stronger isolation than runc
# Kata Containers — lightweight VM per container, hardware isolation
# Check node:
kubectl get nodes -o json | jq '.items[].status.nodeInfo.containerRuntimeVersion'

# === CRI-O ===
# Red Hat's Kubernetes-native runtime
# Socket: /run/crio/crio.sock
# Use crictl with: crictl --runtime-endpoint unix:///run/crio/crio.sock

CNI: Container Networking Interface

CNI is the plugin model for Kubernetes networking. Each pod gets its own network namespace. The CNI plugin creates a veth pair, attaches one end to the pod namespace and the other to a bridge/overlay, and assigns an IP address. At Enterprise, choices include: Cilium (eBPF-based, fastest growing), Calico (policy-rich, widely used), and AWS VPC CNI (native VPC IPs, no overlay).
bash
# === Pod networking (Linux view) ===
kubectl exec <pod> -- ip addr        # Inside pod: network namespace
kubectl exec <pod> -- ip route       # Inside pod: routing table
kubectl exec <pod> -- cat /etc/resolv.conf  # DNS config (inside pod)

# === Node-side: pod network namespace ===
# Find pod's network namespace:
POD_UID=$(kubectl get pod <pod> -o jsonpath='{.metadata.uid}')
# Enter pod's netns from node:
ln -sf /proc/1/ns/net /var/run/netns/default  # (on some systems)
# Better: use nsenter

# === CNI plugins directory ===
ls /opt/cni/bin/                      # Installed CNI plugins
# bridge, host-local, loopback, portmap, bandwidth, tuning

# === CNI config ===
cat /etc/cni/net.d/10-aws.conflist    # CNI configuration (varies by plugin)

# === Check which CNI is in use ===
kubectl get pods -n kube-system | grep -E "(cilium|calico|flannel|weave|aws-node)"

# === Cilium (eBPF-based CNI) ===
# Cilium replaces kube-proxy entirely with eBPF
# No iptables rules needed — eBPF programs handle service load balancing
# Check Cilium status:
kubectl -n kube-system exec daemonset/cilium -- cilium status
kubectl -n kube-system exec daemonset/cilium -- cilium endpoint list

# === Cilium Hubble (network observability) ===
# Shows pod-to-pod traffic with L3/L4/L7 visibility
# Requires no application changes — eBPF captures all traffic

# === Network policy ===
# CNI plugins enforce Kubernetes NetworkPolicy
# (default: all pods can communicate — you must add policies)
kubectl get networkpolicies --all-namespaces

CSI: Container Storage Interface

CSI provides a standard API for storage providers (EBS, EFS, GCE PD, Azure Disk). When a pod requests a PVC, the CSI driver provisions the underlying storage (e.g., an EBS volume), attaches it to the node, mounts it into the pod's filesystem. At Enterprise, understanding CSI is essential for debugging volume mount failures, performance issues, and capacity planning.
bash
# === Pod storage (Linux view) ===
kubectl exec <pod> -- df -h            # Mounted volumes inside pod
kubectl exec <pod> -- mount            # All mounts inside pod
kubectl exec <pod> -- ls -la /dev/     # Block devices inside pod

# === Node-side: volume mounts ===
# Host path for mounted volumes:
ls /var/lib/kubelet/pods/<POD_UID>/volumes/kubernetes.io~csi/
# Or for in-tree volumes:
ls /var/lib/kubelet/pods/<POD_UID>/volumes/kubernetes.io~aws-ebs/

# === Check CSI driver ===
kubectl get csidrivers                  # Installed CSI drivers
kubectl get nodes -o json | jq '.items[0].status.volumesAttached'
# Shows which volumes are attached to the node

# === EBS CSI driver debugging ===
kubectl logs -n kube-system -l app=ebs-csi-node  # Node-side driver logs

# Find EBS volume ID for a PVC:
PV_NAME=$(kubectl get pvc <pvc> -o jsonpath='{.spec.volumeName}')
VOLUME_ID=$(kubectl get pv $PV_NAME -o jsonpath='{.spec.csi.volumeHandle}')
echo $VOLUME_ID  # This is the EBS volume ID

# === Node-level: check attached volumes ===
lsblk                                 # Check if EBS volume is attached
sudo nvme list                        # NVMe device list (Nitro instances)
# Modern AWS Nitro instances expose EBS as NVMe devices

# === Filesystem inside volume ===
# If a volume can't mount, check:
# 1. Is the volume attached? (lsblk)
# 2. Is there a filesystem? (file -s /dev/nvme1n1)
# 3. Can it be mounted manually? (mount /dev/nvme1n1 /mnt)

# === Storage classes ===
kubectl get storageclass
# Each SC defines: provisioner, reclaim policy, filesystem type, IOPS, etc.
# Example: gp3 EBS with 5000 IOPS

Interview Q&A

Q: 'A pod is stuck in 'ContainerCreating' state. How do you debug from the Linux node perspective?'

A: 'SSH into the node (or use kubectl node shell). Check kubelet logs: journalctl -u kubelet --since '5 min ago'. Common causes: (1) Image pull failure — check network connectivity to the registry. (2) Volume mount failure — check if the CSI driver created the volume, if it's attached (lsblk), if the filesystem exists. (3) Resource pressure — the node may be out of CPU/memory/disk. Run kubectl describe node for conditions. (4) CNI error — pod network setup failed. Check CNI daemon logs. (5) Admission webhook timeout — sometimes a webhook hangs and prevents pod creation. At Enterprise, we have automation that detects 'ContainerCreating' > 2 minutes and pages the on-call engineer with the most likely cause based on patterns.'

Q: 'How does kube-proxy work, and why is Cilium replacing it?'

A: 'Kube-proxy implements Service load balancing via userspace proxy, iptables, or IPVS. The iptables mode creates iptables rules for every Service endpoint — on a 10,000-service cluster, this means 100K+ iptables rules. Every packet traverses all these rules (O(n) cost), causing latency spikes during rule updates. Cilium replaces this with eBPF — a BPF program for each Service that runs at the XDP or TC layer, with O(1) lookup. Updates are atomic (bpf map update, no rule churn). Result: lower latency, better scalability, and no iptables overhead. At Enterprise, any cluster with > 1000 services has either moved to Cilium or IPVS.'

Q: 'A node is reporting 'DiskPressure'. What's happening at the Linux level?'

A: 'DiskPressure means the node's available disk space or inodes are below the kubelet's eviction threshold. Check df -h and df -i on the node. Common culprits: (1) Container logs not rotated — /var/log/pods/ can grow large. (2) Docker/containerd images — docker system prune -a or crictl rmi --prune. (3) Ephemeral volume usage — pods writing to emptyDir volumes. (4) System logs — journalctl --vacuum-size=500M. Kubelet will start evicting pods when DiskPressure is true, starting with the lowest priority pods. At Enterprise, we set aggressive kubelet GC thresholds and monitor node disk usage to prevent this.'

References & Further Reading

  • Kubernetes Architecture — Official docs on nodes, kubelet, control plane
  • CRI Specification — Container Runtime Interface deep dive
  • Cilium: eBPF for Kubernetes — How Cilium replaces kube-proxy with eBPF

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes
Mistake: Checking /proc/meminfo inside containers for memory
Solution: Use cgroup v2 files: /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current, memory.events

Quick Reference

| Command | Description | |---|---| | `ps` | Kubelet: The Node Agent | | `journalctl` | Kubelet: The Node Agent | | `ls` | Kubelet: The Node Agent | | `kubectl` | CRI: Container Runtime Interface | | `ls` | CRI: Container Runtime Interface | | `ctr` | CRI: Container Runtime Interface | | `kubectl` | CNI: Container Networking Interface | | `POD_UID` | CNI: Container Networking Interface | | `ln` | CNI: Container Networking Interface | | `kubectl` | CSI: Container Storage Interface | | `ls` | CSI: Container Storage Interface | | `PV_NAME` | CSI: Container Storage Interface |

Troubleshooting

Symptom: ps returns unexpected output
Cause: Incorrect arguments, wrong working directory, or stale cached data
Solution: Verify arguments with `man ps`; check current path with `pwd`; clear cache if applicable
Symptom: Configuration changes not taking effect
Cause: Service not reloaded or syntax error in config file
Solution: Validate config syntax with built-in test flag; reload service with `systemctl reload` or `restart`
Symptom: kubernetes operation slow or timing out
Cause: Resource exhaustion (disk I/O, memory pressure, or lock contention)
Solution: Check `iostat`, `free -m`, and `top`; look for stuck processes with `ps aux --sort=-%cpu`
Symptom: Permission issues accessing kubernetes resources
Cause: Incorrect file ownership or overly restrictive permissions
Solution: Inspect with `ls -la`; fix with `chown`/ `chmod`; check ACLs with `getfacl`

Quick Quiz

Q1: What is Kubelet: The Node Agent?

A: A standardized directory structure that defines where files and directories should be placed on Linux systems

Q2: What does `ps` do?

A: Lists files and directories in the current or specified path

Q3: Which command would you use to view disk usage of the kubernetes directory?

A: du -sh /path/to/directory

Q4: A user reports "Permission denied" when trying to access a kubernetes directory. What is the first command you should run?

A: ls -ld /path/to/directory

Q5: In a kubernetes scenario, your monitoring alerts show ps failing intermittently. What is the correct debugging workflow?

A: Check logs → verify configuration → test in staging → apply fix → monitor

Production Tips

  • Production Tip: Every Kubernetes engineer should know crictl

Best Practices

  • Use version control for all configuration (/etc, scripts, Dockerfiles)
  • Implement infrastructure as code; avoid manual server changes
  • Test changes in staging before production; have rollback plans

Enterprise Perspective

In enterprise environments, kubernetes for linux engineers is managed through automation (Ansible, Terraform), monitored centrally (Prometheus, Datadog), and governed by compliance policies (CIS benchmarks, SOC2). Changes go through change advisory boards with rollback procedures documented.

Chapter 26

Production Troubleshooting

advancedContainers & SRE · 17 min read · 13 min practice

Production troubleshooting is the art of diagnosing and resolving issues in live systems under pressure. At Enterprise, the expectation is that you can SSH into any server (or use equivalent remote access) and systematically identify the root cause of an issue — whether it's a performance degradation, a crash, a network partition, or a resource exhaustion. This guide covers the systematic approach and essential techniques.

faang-insightLinuxtroubleshootingincident responsedebuggingSRE

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux processes, linux networking, linux storage, linux systemd

Learning Objectives

  • Understand incident Response: The First 5 Minutes
  • Understand diagnosing High CPU & Memory Issues
  • Understand diagnosing Disk & I/O Issues
  • Understand diagnosing Network Issues

Real-Life Analogy: Troubleshooting as Differential Diagnosis

Symptoms (high CPU, latency) are not the disease. Check vitals first (top, vmstat, iostat). Narrow scope: is it the app, the kernel, the network, the disk? Use the scientific method: hypothesize, test, eliminate. The runbook is your medical textbook.

Incident Response: The First 5 Minutes

When a page comes in, the first 5 minutes set the tone for the entire incident. Stay calm, follow the runbook, and gather data before making changes. At Enterprise, the incident response protocol is: acknowledge → assess → assemble → act. The cardinal rule: don't make things worse.
bash
# === The 60-second triage (Brendan Gregg's checklist) ===

# 1. uptime — load averages
echo "=== UPTIME ==="
uptime

# 2. dmesg — kernel errors
echo "=== KERNEL ==="
dmesg | tail -20

# 3. vmstat — system-level CPU/memory/swap
echo "=== VMSTAT ==="
vmstat 1 3

# 4. mpstat — per-CPU balance
echo "=== PER-CPU ==="
mpstat -P ALL 1 1

# 5. pidstat — per-process CPU
echo "=== TOP CPU PROCESSES ==="
pidstat 1 3 | tail -10

# 6. iostat — disk I/O
echo "=== DISK I/O ==="
iostat -xz 1 2

# 7. free -m — memory
echo "=== MEMORY ==="
free -h

# 8. sar -n DEV — network
echo "=== NETWORK ==="
sar -n DEV 1 2 | grep -E "(Interface|eth|ens|bond)"

# 9. sar -n TCP,ETCP — TCP stats
echo "=== TCP ==="
sar -n TCP,ETCP 1 1

# 10. top — overview
echo "=== TOP ==="
top -b -n 1 | head -20

# === Before making any change ===
# 1. Confirm the issue: is this an alert false positive?
# 2. Check if a recent deploy/change caused it
# 3. Is this a known issue? Check the runbook
# 4. Communicate: 'I'm investigating, ETA unknown'
# 5. If risky: 'What's the rollback plan?'

Diagnosing High CPU & Memory Issues

CPU and memory issues are the most common production problems. The key question: is this a traffic increase (legitimate high usage) or a code issue (infinite loop, memory leak)? At Enterprise, we use perf, heap dumps, and continuous profiling to answer this question in minutes, not hours.
bash
# === High CPU ===
# 1. Identify the process:
ps aux --sort=-%cpu | head -10

# 2. Check threads:
top -H -p <PID>

# 3. Sample the hot function:
perf top -p <PID>

# 4. Capture stack samples:
perf record -F 99 -p <PID> -g --sleep 30
perf report -g --stdio | head -40

# 5. Check for common patterns:
# - Infinite loop: check application logs for repeated log messages
# - GC storm (Java/Go): check GC metrics
# - Event loop blocked (Node/Python): check event loop lag
# - Contention: check thread dumps (jstack, pstack, gdb)

# === Java thread dump (blocked threads) ===
jstack <PID>                          # Full thread dump
jstack <PID> | grep -A 20 "BLOCKED"   # Find blocked threads

# === Memory leak ===
# 1. Confirm leak (RSS grows over time):
ps -p <PID> -o rss,vsz,pmem

# 2. Check /proc/<PID>/status for memory breakdown:
cat /proc/<PID>/status | grep -E "(VmRSS|VmSize|VmPeak|VmSwap)"

# 3. Check page fault rate (major faults = swapping):
cat /proc/<PID>/status | grep -i fault

# 4. Java heap analysis:
# Capture heap dump:
jmap -dump:live,format=b,file=heap.hprof <PID>
# Analyze with Eclipse MAT or jhat

# 5. Native memory leak (Valgrind):
valgrind --tool=memcheck --leak-check=full ./myapp

# 6. Python memory profiling:
pip install memory_profiler
python -m memory_profiler myapp.py

# === Node.js memory ===
# Take heap snapshot (need --inspect):
kill -USR2 <PID>                       # Toggle inspector
# Then connect Chrome DevTools or use node --inspect

# === Go memory ===
curl http://localhost:6060/debug/pprof/heap > heap.out
go tool pprof heap.out

Diagnosing Disk & I/O Issues

Disk issues manifest as: 'disk full', 'slow I/O', 'filesystem errors', or 'process stuck in D state'. The most common cause of disk-full incidents is log files that weren't rotated. At Enterprise, disk monitoring is automated but engineers still need to diagnose root cause when the alert fires.
bash
# === Disk full ===
df -h                                 # Check space
df -i                                 # Check inodes (filesystem can be full even with
free space!)

# Find the culprit:
du -sh /* | sort -rh | head -10       # Top-level directories
du -sh /var/log/* | sort -rh | head -10  # Log directories
find /var/log -type f -size +100M     # Large files > 100MB

# Common space hogs:
# - Log files: /var/log/, /var/log/journal/
# - Docker: /var/lib/docker/, docker system prune -a
# - Package cache: apt-get clean, yum clean all
# - Core dumps: /var/crash/, /var/lib/systemd/coredump/
# - Old kernels: /boot/, apt-get autoremove --purge

# === Filesystem corruption ===
# If disk errors appear:
dmesg | grep -i "I/O error\|buffer I/O"

# Check filesystem (unmounted):
sudo fsck.ext4 -f /dev/sdb1

# XFS repair:
sudo xfs_repair /dev/sdb1

# === Slow I/O ===
iostat -x 1 5                         # Check %util, await, aqu-sz
iotop -oP                             # Which process is doing I/O?

# Check for I/O throttling:
cat /sys/fs/cgroup/system.slice/*.service/io.stat 2>/dev/null

# === Process stuck in D (uninterruptible sleep) ===
ps aux | grep " D"
# D state = waiting for I/O in kernel (can't be killed)
cat /proc/<PID>/stack                 # Show kernel stack (what's it waiting for?)
cat /proc/<PID>/wchan                 # Kernel function it's blocked in

# === NFS issues ===
mount | grep nfs                     # Check NFS mounts
# If NFS server is down, processes hang in D state
# Fix: remount with 'soft' option (but risk of data corruption)
# Better: fix the NFS server or use 'hard,intr' with reasonable timeo

Diagnosing Network Issues

Network issues can be: 'connection refused' (service not listening), 'connection timed out' (firewall or routing), 'connection reset' (service crashed or overloaded), or 'intermittent failures' (packet loss, DNS issues). At Enterprise, network troubleshooting is a layered skill — you start with basic connectivity tests and escalate to packet captures.
bash
# === Layer 1: Is the port open? ===
ss -tlnp | grep :80                  # Is something listening on port 80?
nc -zv localhost 80                  # Local connectivity test

# Layer 2: Is the network reachable?
ping -c 3 10.0.0.1                  # Basic ICMP test
mtr 10.0.0.1                         # Path analysis (traceroute + ping)

# Layer 3: Is the firewall blocking?
sudo iptables -L -n | grep :80       # Check iptables rules
# Cloud: check security groups / network ACLs

# Layer 4: Socket state
ss -tanp | grep :80                   # Connection state
# TIME_WAIT high: normal for proxies, but can exhaust ports
# CLOSE_WAIT high: application bug (not closing sockets)

# Layer 5: Application-level
curl -v http://localhost:80           # HTTP headers + timing
curl -w "@curl-format.txt" -o /dev/null -s http://localhost:80
# Custom timing format:
cat > curl-format.txt << 'EOF'
    time_namelookup:  %{time_namelookup}s
       time_connect:  %{time_connect}s
    time_appconnect:  %{time_appconnect}s
   time_pretransfer:  %{time_pretransfer}s
      time_redirect:  %{time_redirect}s
 time_starttransfer:  %{time_starttransfer}s
                    ----------
         time_total:  %{time_total}s
EOF

# === DNS issues ===
dig google.com +short                # Quick DNS check
dig @8.8.8.8 myapp.internal          # Bypass local DNS resolver
getent hosts myapp.internal          # System resolver (respects /etc/hosts)

# === Packet capture for deep debugging ===
sudo tcpdump -ni any port 80 -c 100  # Capture 100 packets on port 80
sudo tcpdump -ni any 'tcp[tcpflags] & (tcp-syn) != 0'  # SYN packets only

# === TCP retransmit check ===
netstat -s | grep -i "retrans\|segments"   # TCP retransmit count
# Compare to total segments: retransmits > 1% indicates packet loss

# === Connection tracking ===
cat /proc/net/nf_conntrack | wc -l     # Connection tracking table size
cat /proc/sys/net/netfilter/nf_conntrack_max  # Max connections tracked
# If connection table is full, new connections are dropped!

Interview Q&A

Q: 'Walk me through debugging 'It doesn't work' from a production system.'

A: 'First, I need specifics — 'doesn't work' is not actionable. What exactly is failing? What's the error? Which users are affected? When did it start? Was there a recent deploy? With that information: (1) Check the service's health endpoint. (2) Check the four golden signals: latency, traffic, errors, saturation. (3) Check the application logs for error messages. (4) Check the load balancer metrics — is traffic being evenly distributed? (5) Check upstream dependencies — is the database responding? Is the cache hot? (6) Check the host metrics — CPU, memory, disk, network. (7) If everything looks normal, I'd look at recent changes — a deploy, a config change, or a dependency upgrade. At Enterprise, the first question in any incident is always: 'What changed?' because 90% of incidents are caused by changes.'

Q: 'What's the difference between a crash and a hang?'

A: 'A crash is when the process terminates unexpectedly (segfault, OOM kill, uncaught exception). You can restart it and move on — but you need to find the root cause to prevent recurrence. Look at core dumps, application logs, and crash logs. A hang is when the process is still running but not making progress (deadlock, infinite loop, waiting on a resource that never comes). The process is alive but not serving. Debug with thread dumps (jstack, pstack, gdb), strace to see what syscall it's stuck on, and /proc//stack to see the kernel function it's blocked in. Hangs are harder to debug than crashes because the process is still alive — you need to inspect its state without killing it.'

Q: 'A service was working yesterday and is broken today. What's your troubleshooting approach?'

A: 'This is the classic 'it was working' scenario. Approach: (1) Change detection — what changed since it was working? Deploy? Config change? DNS change? Dependency update? Certificate expiry? (2) Isolation — can I reproduce the issue in a non-production environment? Can I rollback the most recent change? (3) Compare — compare the working config (git history) to the current config. Compare the working version to the broken version. (4) Temporal correlation — did the issue start at the same time as another event (cron job, backup, traffic spike, upstream degradation)? At Enterprise, we practice 'immutable deployments' — we never modify running servers, so 'it was working yesterday' means the issue is either a new deployment, a change in traffic patterns, a dependency failure, or a time-based condition (cert expiry, rate limit, database full). The rollback is almost always the fastest fix — then investigate why the new version broke things.'

References & Further Reading

  • The USE Method — Brendan Gregg's systematic performance analysis methodology
  • Linux Performance: 60-Second Troubleshooting — The essential 60-second triage checklist
  • Google SRE Book: Effective Troubleshooting — Systematic approach to incident response

Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes

Chapter 27

System Rescue & Recovery

intermediateContainers & SRE · 18 min read · 14 min practice

Every senior engineer faces an unbootable server, a corrupt filesystem, or a forgotten root password at some point. System rescue skills — the ability to recover a system using recovery media, chroot, and manual intervention — separate engineers who can recover from disaster from those who have to reinstall. At Enterprise, automated recovery handles most cases, but when the automation fails, manual rescue skills are essential.

faang-insightLinuxrecoveryGRUBchrootrescuefilesystem repair

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux troubleshooting, linux storage, linux filesystem

Learning Objectives

  • Understand boot Process: From Power-On to Login Prompt
  • Understand gRUB: Bootloader Rescue & Repair
  • Understand chroot Rescue & Single-User Mode
  • Understand filesystem Recovery & Data Rescue

Real-Life Analogy: System Rescue as Emergency Surgery

Boot from live media (the ambulance). Mount the root filesystem (access the patient). chroot into it (operate in context). Fix fstab, reinstall grub, reset passwords, recover from backup. The goal: stabilize and discharge.

Boot Process: From Power-On to Login Prompt

Understanding the boot sequence lets you identify where a boot failure occurs. BIOS/UEFI → Bootloader (GRUB) → Kernel → initramfs → root filesystem → systemd (PID 1) → login. Each stage has specific failure modes. At Enterprise, the boot sequence is optimized for speed (fast boot targets, parallel service startup) but the fundamentals are the same.
bash
# === Boot stage 1: Firmware (BIOS/UEFI) ===
# BIOS: legacy, MBR partition, 16-bit mode
# UEFI: modern, GPT partition, 64-bit, Secure Boot
# Check current mode:
[ -d /sys/firmware/efi ] && echo "UEFI" || echo "BIOS"
ls /sys/firmware/efi/efivars/          # UEFI variables (if UEFI)

# === Boot stage 2: Bootloader (GRUB) ===
# GRUB config: /boot/grub/grub.cfg (DO NOT EDIT — generated by update-grub)
# User config: /etc/default/grub (edit this, then run update-grub)

cat /etc/default/grub
# GRUB_DEFAULT=0                       # Default menu entry
# GRUB_TIMEOUT=5                       # Seconds before auto-boot
# GRUB_CMDLINE_LINUX=""               # Kernel boot parameters
# GRUB_CMDLINE_LINUX_DEFAULT="quiet splash"  # Additional params

# Common boot parameters:
# single            — Boot to single-user mode (recovery)
# emergency         — Boot to emergency shell
# init=/bin/bash    — Override init (skip systemd)
# nomodeset         — Disable kernel mode-setting (display issues)
# selinux=0         — Disable SELinux (troubleshooting only)
# systemd.unit=rescue.target  — Boot to rescue target

# Update GRUB after changes:
sudo update-grub                       # Debian/Ubuntu
sudo grub2-mkconfig -o /boot/grub2/grub.cfg  # RHEL/Amazon Linux

# === Boot stage 3: Kernel + initramfs ===
# initramfs (initial RAM filesystem) contains:
# - Essential kernel modules (storage, filesystem, network)
# - /init script that mounts root filesystem
# - Busybox or recovery tools

# List initramfs contents:
lsinitramfs /boot/initrd.img-$(uname -r)  # Ubuntu
lsinitrd /boot/initramfs-$(uname -r).img    # RHEL

# Rebuild initramfs:
sudo update-initramfs -u                 # Ubuntu
sudo dracut -f                           # RHEL/Amazon Linux

# === Boot stage 4: Root filesystem + systemd ===
# /init mounts rootfs, then pivots to real root
# systemd reads default.target → starts services
# Check default target:
systemctl get-default

GRUB: Bootloader Rescue & Repair

GRUB failure = system won't boot. Common causes: misconfigured grub.cfg, missing kernel, damaged MBR/GPT, or wrong root filesystem UUID. At Enterprise, boot failures are handled by automated recovery (PXE boot, IPMI console, AWS Systems Manager) — but manual GRUB rescue is the fallback.
bash
# === GRUB rescue shell ===
# If GRUB can't find its config, you get:
# grub rescue>

# Commands in grub rescue:
grub rescue> ls                        # List available drives
# (hd0) (hd0,msdos1) (hd0,msdos2)
grub rescue> ls (hd0,msdos1)/         # List files on partition
grub rescue> set prefix=(hd0,msdos1)/boot/grub
grub rescue> set root=(hd0,msdos1)
grub rescue> insmod normal
grub rescue> normal                    # Load normal GRUB menu

# === GRUB interactive editing ===
# At GRUB menu, press 'e' to edit boot entry
# Common fixes:
# 1. Change root=UUID=... to root=/dev/sda1 (if UUID changed)
# 2. Add 'single' or 'emergency' to boot into recovery
# 3. Add 'init=/bin/bash' to skip init entirely
# 4. Fix broken root= parameter

# === Reinstall GRUB from live CD ===
# Boot from Ubuntu live USB, mount root filesystem:
sudo mkdir /mnt/root
sudo mount /dev/sda1 /mnt/root
sudo mount --bind /dev /mnt/root/dev
sudo mount --bind /proc /mnt/root/proc
sudo mount --bind /sys /mnt/root/sys

# Chroot and reinstall GRUB:
sudo chroot /mnt/root
grub-install /dev/sda
update-grub
exit
sudo umount -R /mnt/root
# Reboot

# === Fix broken kernel/boot files ===
# If /boot is empty (accidental rm -rf /boot):
# Boot from live CD, chroot, install new kernel:
apt-get install --reinstall linux-image-$(uname -r)
update-grub

# === GRUB password protection ===
grub-mkpasswd-pbkdf2                   # Generate password hash
# Add to /etc/grub.d/40_custom:
# set superusers="admin"
# password_pbkdf2 admin grub.pbkdf2.sha512.10000...
# Then update-grub

Chroot Rescue & Single-User Mode

When a system won't boot normally, you can boot from recovery media, mount the root filesystem, and chroot into it to repair packages, fix configuration files, reset passwords, or rebuild the bootloader. Single-user mode boots with minimum services and a root shell. At Enterprise, these techniques are documented in runbooks and tested during disaster recovery drills.
bash
# === Single-user mode ===
# At GRUB prompt, edit boot entry, add 'single' to kernel line
# Press Ctrl+X to boot
# You get a root shell with minimal services
# Remount root as read-write:
mount -o remount,rw /

# Fix the issue, then reboot

# === Emergency mode ===
# Even more minimal than single-user
# At GRUB: add 'emergency' or 'systemd.unit=emergency.target'
# Only PID 1 runs, root filesystem is read-only

# === Chroot rescue (from live USB) ===
# Step 1: Boot from Ubuntu/Debian live USB
# Step 2: Identify and mount root:
lsblk                                    # Find root partition
sudo mkdir /mnt/root
sudo mount /dev/sda2 /mnt/root           # Mount root filesystem

# Step 3: Mount essential filesystems:
sudo mount --bind /dev /mnt/root/dev
sudo mount --bind /proc /mnt/root/proc
sudo mount --bind /sys /mnt/root/sys
sudo mount --bind /run /mnt/root/run     # Some systems need this

# Step 4: Mount boot partition if separate:
sudo mount /dev/sda1 /mnt/root/boot

# Step 5: Chroot:
sudo chroot /mnt/root /bin/bash
source /etc/profile
export PS1="(chroot) \[\e[31m\]\u@\h:\w\$\[\e[0m\] "

# === Inside chroot: common repairs ===
# Reset root password:
passwd root

# Fix broken packages:
apt-get install -f

# Reinstall bootloader:
grub-install /dev/sda
update-grub

# Rebuild initramfs:
update-initramfs -u -k all

# Fix filesystem:
e2fsck -f /dev/sda2

# === Exit chroot ===
exit
sudo umount -R /mnt/root
sudo reboot

Filesystem Recovery & Data Rescue

Filesystem corruption can result from power loss, hardware failure, or improper shutdown. ext4 has a journal for fast recovery; XFS has metadata writeback logging. At Enterprise, filesystem corruption is rare (EBS volumes have 99.999% durability, proper shutdown procedures are enforced), but when it happens, the response is methodical: assess damage → backup → repair → restore.
bash
# === Check filesystem health ===
# Check without modifying (safe):
sudo fsck -n /dev/sdb1                   # ext4: check only, no repair
sudo xfs_repair -n /dev/sdb1             # XFS: check only

# === ext4 journal recovery ===
# ext4 has a journal that records metadata changes
# On unclean shutdown: journal is replayed on mount (automatic)
# If journal is corrupt:
sudo fsck.ext4 -f /dev/sdb1              # Force check
sudo fsck.ext4 -fy /dev/sdb1             # Force + yes (non-interactive)

# === XFS repair ===
# XFS must be unmounted for repair
sudo xfs_repair /dev/sdb1
sudo xfs_repair -v /dev/sdb1             # Verbose
sudo xfs_repair -L /dev/sdb1             # Clear log (DANGER: may lose recent metadata)

# === Bad blocks ===
# Check for hardware-level bad sectors:
sudo badblocks -sv /dev/sdb1
# If bad blocks found: replace the drive!
# For EBS: snapshot, create new volume, detach old

# === SMART data (hardware health) ===
sudo smartctl -a /dev/sda                # Full SMART report
sudo smartctl -H /dev/sda                # Overall health (PASSED/FAILED)
# Key SMART attributes:
# 5: Reallocated_Sector_Ct (> 0 = bad!)
# 187: Reported_Uncorrectable_Errors
# 197: Current_Pending_Sector
# 198: Offline_Uncorrectable

# === ddrescue (recover data from failing disk) ===
sudo apt install gddrescue
sudo ddrescue -d /dev/sdb /dev/sdc rescue.log
# Retry failed sectors:
sudo ddrescue -d -r3 /dev/sdb /dev/sdc rescue.log

# === Backup superblock (ext4) ===
sudo mkfs.ext4 -n /dev/sdb1              # Show backup superblock locations
sudo fsck.ext4 -b 32768 /dev/sdb1       # Use alternate superblock

# === XFS backup superblock ===
sudo xfs_db -x -c "sb 0" -c "p" /dev/sdb1  # Check primary superblock
sudo xfs_repair -b 1 /dev/sdb1          # Use backup superblock (1, 2, 4, 8, ...)

# === If data is critical ===
# 1. STOP: don't mount the filesystem, don't run fsck
# 2. Create a block-level backup:
sudo dd if=/dev/sdb1 of=/backup/sdb1.img bs=1M status=progress
# 3. Work on the backup, not the original
# 4. Use testdisk or photorec for file recovery
# 5. At Enterprise: snapshot EBS volume, then repair snapshot

Interview Q&A

Q: 'A server won't boot. You can't SSH in. What do you do?'

A: 'First, access the console out-of-band — IPMI/iLO/iDRAC for bare metal, AWS Console → EC2 Serial Console for cloud, or recovery mode for VMs. Check what stage it reaches: (1) Power-on: does it POST? Check power supply, IPMI status. (2) Bootloader: can you see GRUB? Edit GRUB entry and add 'single' for recovery. (3) Kernel: is it panicking? Add 'earlyprintk' to boot params. (4) Filesystem: is fsck failing? Boot from recovery media (live CD, rescue ISO) and check. (5) Systemd: is a service hanging? Add 'systemd.log_level=debug systemd.log_target=console' to boot params. At Enterprise, the first response is usually: attach the EBS volume to a healthy instance and repair from there — faster than debugging the failing boot process.'

Q: 'A team member accidentally deleted /etc/passwd on a production server. How do you recover?'

A: 'Don't reboot! The system is still running — as long as existing processes are alive, they keep their credentials. First, restore the file from backup: cp /var/backups/passwd /etc/passwd (if backups exist). If no backup, reconstruct from /etc/passwd- (a backup maintained by pwconv): cp /etc/passwd- /etc/passwd. If that doesn't exist, recreate users from getent passwd output captured on other servers, or reconstruct from /etc/shadow and /etc/group. At Enterprise, we have immutable infrastructure — /etc/passwd is managed by configuration management and restored automatically. If a file is deleted, the config management agent (Chef, Ansible) recreates it on the next run.'

Q: 'What would you include in a disaster recovery plan for a Linux-based service?'

A: 'A DR plan should cover: (1) Recovery objectives: RTO (Recovery Time Objective — how fast) and RPO (Recovery Point Objective — how much data loss). (2) Backup strategy: filesystem-level (duplicity/rsync), block-level (EBS snapshots), database-level (WAL archiving). (3) Recovery procedures: step-by-step runbooks for rebuilding a server, restoring from backup, promoting a replica. (4) Boot recovery: live USB images, PXE boot configuration, GRUB rescue procedures. (5) Testing: regular DR drills where the team actually goes through the recovery process. At Enterprise, DR is not a document — it's an automated process. We practice 'chaos engineering' where servers are intentionally terminated to verify auto-recovery. The manual DR procedures are for the 0.1% of cases where automation fails.'

References & Further Reading

  • GRUB Rescue Shell — Official GRUB rescue documentation
  • Ubuntu Live CD Recovery — Community guide for chroot rescue procedures
  • Arch Linux Filesystem Repair — Comprehensive fsck and recovery reference

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Running destructive commands without verification
Solution: Always use `rm -i` or `ls` first to confirm what will be deleted
Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes
