Quick Reference

| Command | Description | |---|---| | `ls` | Boot Process: From Power-On to Login Prompt | | `cat` | Boot Process: From Power-On to Login Prompt | | `sudo` | Boot Process: From Power-On to Login Prompt | | `grub` | GRUB: Bootloader Rescue & Repair | | `sudo` | GRUB: Bootloader Rescue & Repair | | `grub-install` | GRUB: Bootloader Rescue & Repair | | `mount` | Chroot Rescue & Single-User Mode | | `lsblk` | Chroot Rescue & Single-User Mode | | `sudo` | Chroot Rescue & Single-User Mode | | `sudo` | Filesystem Recovery & Data Rescue |

Troubleshooting

Symptom: ls returns unexpected output
Cause: Incorrect arguments, wrong working directory, or stale cached data
Solution: Verify arguments with `man ls`; check current path with `pwd`; clear cache if applicable
Symptom: Configuration changes not taking effect
Cause: Service not reloaded or syntax error in config file
Solution: Validate config syntax with built-in test flag; reload service with `systemctl reload` or `restart`
Symptom: system rescue operation slow or timing out
Cause: Resource exhaustion (disk I/O, memory pressure, or lock contention)
Solution: Check `iostat`, `free -m`, and `top`; look for stuck processes with `ps aux --sort=-%cpu`
Symptom: Permission issues accessing system rescue resources
Cause: Incorrect file ownership or overly restrictive permissions
Solution: Inspect with `ls -la`; fix with `chown`/ `chmod`; check ACLs with `getfacl`

Quick Quiz

Q1: What is Boot Process: From Power-On to Login Prompt?

A: A standardized directory structure that defines where files and directories should be placed on Linux systems

Q2: What does `ls` do?

A: Lists files and directories in the current or specified path

Q3: Which command would you use to view disk usage of the system rescue directory?

A: du -sh /path/to/directory

Q4: A user reports "Permission denied" when trying to access a system rescue directory. What is the first command you should run?

A: ls -ld /path/to/directory

Q5: In a system rescue scenario, your monitoring alerts show ls failing intermittently. What is the correct debugging workflow?

A: Check logs → verify configuration → test in staging → apply fix → monitor

FAANG Insight

  • FAANG Architect's Note: System rescue is one of those skills that seems obsolete in the cloud era — until you need it
  • FAANG, we have automated recovery for 99% of scenarios: instance health checks, auto-scaling, self-healing Kubernetes, immutable infrastructure

Production Tips

  • Production Tip: Before you need it, create a rescue USB or ISO for your distribution and test it

Best Practices

  • Use version control for all configuration (/etc, scripts, Dockerfiles)
  • Implement infrastructure as code; avoid manual server changes
  • Test changes in staging before production; have rollback plans

Enterprise Perspective

In enterprise environments, system rescue & recovery is managed through automation (Ansible, Terraform), monitored centrally (Prometheus, Datadog), and governed by compliance policies (CIS benchmarks, SOC2). Changes go through change advisory boards with rollback procedures documented.

Chapter 28

Security Hardening

intermediateContainers & SRE · 17 min read · 13 min practice

Linux security is defense in depth: from kernel hardening (SELinux, AppArmor, seccomp) to system configuration (sysctl, PAM, auditd) to operational practices (patch management, least privilege, incident response). At Enterprise, every server is hardened against a baseline, and deviations are automatically detected and remediated.

faang-insightLinuxsecuritySELinuxAppArmorseccompauditdhardening

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux users, linux permissions, linux networking, linux systemd

Learning Objectives

  • Understand kernel Hardening: sysctl Security Settings
  • Understand seccomp: Secure Computing Mode
  • Understand sELinux & AppArmor
  • Understand auditd: Linux Audit System

Real-Life Analogy: Security as Layers of Defense

Perimeter (firewall) is the castle wall. SELinux/AppArmor are guards inside each room. Seccomp filters which syscalls a process can make. Updates patch holes in the wall. Least privilege means every servant has only the keys they need. Auditd logs who opened which door.

Kernel Hardening: sysctl Security Settings

The Linux kernel has many security-related parameters accessible via sysctl. These settings mitigate common attack vectors: IP spoofing, SYN floods, source routing, and kernel pointer leaks. At Enterprise, these are enforced by configuration management (Ansible, Chef) and verified by compliance scanners.
bash
# === /etc/sysctl.d/99-security.conf ===

# === Network hardening ===
# Disable IP forwarding (unless the host is a router)
net.ipv4.ip_forward = 0
net.ipv6.conf.all.forwarding = 0

# Disable source packet routing
net.ipv4.conf.all.accept_source_route = 0
net.ipv6.conf.all.accept_source_route = 0

# Enable IP spoofing protection (reverse-path filtering)
net.ipv4.conf.all.rp_filter = 1

# Ignore ICMP redirects (MITM vector)
net.ipv4.conf.all.accept_redirects = 0
net.ipv6.conf.all.accept_redirects = 0
net.ipv4.conf.all.secure_redirects = 0
net.ipv4.conf.all.send_redirects = 0

# SYN flood protection
net.ipv4.tcp_syncookies = 1
net.ipv4.tcp_syn_retries = 5
net.ipv4.tcp_synack_retries = 2

# Don't log martians (bogus packets)
net.ipv4.conf.all.log_martians = 1

# === Kernel hardening ===
# Restrict kernel pointer access (prevents KASLR bypass)
kernel.kptr_restrict = 2

# Restrict dmesg to root only
kernel.dmesg_restrict = 1

# Restrict perf events to root only
kernel.perf_event_paranoid = 3

# Disable kexec (prevent kernel replacement without reboot)
kernel.kexec_load_disabled = 0

# ASLR (Address Space Layout Randomization)
kernel.randomize_va_space = 2

# Restrict ptrace (prevents process injection)
kernel.yama.ptrace_scope = 1

# === Apply ===
sudo sysctl -p /etc/sysctl.d/99-security.conf
sudo sysctl --system                  # Apply all sysctl files

# === Check current settings ===
sudo sysctl -a | grep -E
"(kernel.(kptr|dmesg|perf|yama)|net.ipv4.conf.all.(rp_filter|accept_source))"

Seccomp: Secure Computing Mode

Seccomp restricts which syscalls a process can make. seccomp-bpf (since Linux 3.5) allows programmable filters using BPF bytecode. At Enterprise, seccomp is mandatory for all containers — it's part of the container runtime default. A process that makes a syscall outside the allowed set is either killed (SECCOMP_RET_KILL) or denied with an error (SECCOMP_RET_ERRNO).
bash
# === Check seccomp status ===
cat /proc/<PID>/status | grep Seccomp
# 0 = disabled
# 1 = strict (only read, write, exit, sigreturn)
# 2 = filter (custom seccomp-bpf policy)

# === Docker seccomp profiles ===
# Default profile: /usr/share/containers/seccomp.json (or Docker's built-in)
# Run container with custom seccomp:
docker run --security-opt seccomp=custom.json nginx

# === Disable seccomp (not recommended!) ===
docker run --security-opt seccomp=unconfined nginx

# === Common seccomp policies ===
# For web apps: allow ~250-300 syscalls (most common)
# For databases: allow ~200-250 syscalls
# For minimal containers: allow ~50 syscalls
# Example denied syscalls: kexec_load, uselib, create_module, delete_module
# 
# Tools:
# - inspektor-gadget: inspect seccomp profiles of running containers
# - strace: generate a list of syscalls used by your app, then build profile

# === Generate seccomp profile ===
# Record syscalls:
strace -c -f -o syscalls.txt ./myapp
# Then convert to seccomp JSON profile using:
# https://github.com/docker/labs/blob/master/security/seccomp/seccomp-profiles/allow-all
.json

# === Check if seccomp blocked a syscall ===
# Look for: 'exit code 159' or SIGSYS (seccomp violation)
dmesg | grep -i "seccomp\|SIGSYS"
journalctl -k | grep -i seccomp

# === seccomp with systemd ===
[Service]
SystemCallFilter=@system-service   # Allow standard service syscalls
SystemCallArchitectures=native     # Only allow native architecture syscalls
SystemCallErrorNumber=EPERM        # Return 'Operation not permitted' instead of killing

SELinux & AppArmor

SELinux (Security-Enhanced Linux) and AppArmor are Linux Security Modules (LSM) that enforce mandatory access control (MAC). SELinux labels every file, process, and port with a security context and defines policies for allowed interactions. AppArmor uses path-based profiles instead of labels. At Enterprise, SELinux is enabled on RHEL/Amazon Linux; AppArmor is the default on Ubuntu/Debian. Both achieve the same goal: confine processes even if they run as root.
bash
# === SELinux (RHEL/Amazon Linux) ===
# Check status:
getenforce                           # Enforcing | Permissive | Disabled
sestatus                             # Detailed SELinux status

# Modes:
# Enforcing  — Security policy is enforced (production)
# Permissive — Policy violations are logged but not blocked (debugging)
# Disabled   — SELinux is off (not recommended)

# Switch modes (runtime):
sudo setenforce 0                    # Permissive (for troubleshooting)
sudo setenforce 1                    # Enforcing
# Permanent: /etc/selinux/config — SELINUX=enforcing

# === SELinux context ===
ls -Z /var/www/html/index.html       # Show SELinux context
# system_u:object_r:httpd_sys_content_t:s0
# user:role:type:level

ps auxZ | grep nginx                 # Show process SELinux context

# === Common SELinux commands ===
semanage fcontext -l | grep /var/log  # List file contexts
semanage port -l | grep http         # List port contexts

# Fix file context:
sudo restorecon -Rv /var/www/html     # Restore default contexts

# Change file context:
sudo semanage fcontext -a -t httpd_sys_content_t "/web(/.*)?"
sudo restorecon -Rv /web

# Enable/disable booleans:
getsebool -a | grep http             # List SELinux booleans
sudo setsebool -P httpd_can_network_connect on  # Allow Apache to make outbound
connections

# === SELinux audit log ===
sudo ausearch -m avc -ts recent      # Recent denials
sudo sealert -a /var/log/audit/audit.log  # Generate readable denials

# === AppArmor (Ubuntu/Debian) ===
sudo aa-status                       # List loaded profiles
sudo apparmor_status

# Profile modes:
# enforce — Policy enforced (production)
# complain — Policy violations logged but allowed (debugging)

# Switch profile mode:
sudo aa-complain /usr/sbin/nginx     # Set to complain mode
sudo aa-enforce /usr/sbin/nginx      # Set to enforce mode

# === AppArmor profile example ===
# /etc/apparmor.d/usr.sbin.nginx
# profile nginx /usr/sbin/nginx {
#   include <abstractions/base>
#   include <abstractions/nginx>
#   /etc/nginx/** r,
#   /var/log/nginx/* w,
#   /var/www/html/** r,
# }

# === Disable (not recommended for production) ===
# To disable temporarily:
sudo ln -sf /etc/apparmor.d/usr.sbin.nginx /etc/apparmor.d/disable/
sudo apparmor_parser -R /etc/apparmor.d/usr.sbin.nginx

Auditd: Linux Audit System

The Linux Audit System (auditd) records security-relevant events: file access, syscalls, user logins, and configuration changes. At Enterprise, auditd is mandatory on all servers — it provides the audit trail for security incidents and compliance. The audit logs are shipped to centralized SIEM systems (Splunk, Elasticsearch) for analysis and long-term retention.
bash
# === auditd setup ===
sudo apt install auditd audispd-plugins
sudo systemctl enable --now auditd

# === Rules file: /etc/audit/rules.d/audit.rules ===
# Monitor password file changes
-w /etc/passwd -p wa -k identity
-w /etc/shadow -p wa -k identity
-w /etc/sudoers -p wa -k sudoers
-w /etc/ssh/sshd_config -p wa -k sshd

# Monitor SUID/SGID file changes
-a always,exit -F arch=b64 -S execve -F euid=0 -k root_command

# Monitor user/group changes
-w /usr/sbin/useradd -p x -k useradd
-w /usr/sbin/usermod -p x -k usermod
-w /usr/sbin/groupadd -p x -k groupadd

# Monitor network configuration changes
-w /sbin/iptables -p x -k iptables
-w /sbin/ip -p x -k ip

# Monitor important directories
-w /etc/ -p wa -k etc_changes
-w /root/.bashrc -p wa -k root_config

# Monitor syscalls (performance impact!)
-a always,exit -F arch=b64 -S openat,open -F key=file_open

# === Query audit logs ===
# Recent events:
sudo ausearch -ts recent -k identity   # Events tagged 'identity'
sudo ausearch -m USER_LOGIN            # Login events
sudo ausearch -m EXECVE                # Commands executed (sudo)

# Live monitoring:
sudo tail -f /var/log/audit/audit.log | aureport --interpret

# Summary report:
sudo aureport --summary                 # Summary by event type
sudo aureport --user                   # By user
sudo aureport -ts this-week             # This week's events

# === Check auditd status ===
sudo auditctl -s                       # Status
sudo auditctl -l                       # List loaded rules

# === Log file ===
/var/log/audit/audit.log               # Raw audit events
# Format:
# type=SYSCALL msg=audit(1705300000.123:456): arch=c000003e syscall=2 success=yes exit=3
a0=...
# Interpret: ausearch --interpret or audit2why

Interview Q&A

Q: 'A compliance auditor asks: how do you ensure that only authorized users can access production servers?'

A: 'At Enterprise, we use a multi-layered approach: (1) No direct SSH — all access goes through a bastion host or session manager (AWS SSM, Teleport). (2) Authentication is via short-lived certificates (SSH CA) — no long-lived SSH keys on developer workstations. (3) Authorization is checked by the bastion — it integrates with the corporate identity system (Okta, LDAP) and enforces MFA. (4) All sessions are recorded — every keystroke is logged and retained for compliance. (5) Access is time-bound — sessions expire after 4-8 hours, and access reviews are automated. (6) Changes are made through CI/CD pipelines, not SSH — SSH access is reserved for emergencies and debugging, not routine changes.'

Q: 'What's the difference between DAC and MAC?'

A: 'DAC (Discretionary Access Control) is the standard Unix permission model — the owner of a file decides who can read/write/execute it. Root can bypass all DAC checks. MAC (Mandatory Access Control) is enforced by the kernel via LSM (SELinux/AppArmor) — it overrides DAC decisions. Even if a file is chmod 777, SELinux can deny access if the process doesn't have the right security context. MAC is defense in depth: if an attacker compromises a service (e.g., nginx with a remote code execution), MAC prevents them from accessing files labeled for other services, even if they're running as root.'

Q: 'A server was compromised. What do you do?'

A: 'Follow the incident response protocol: (1) Isolate the server — remove it from the network, block traffic at the firewall, but DON'T shut it down (memory forensics). (2) Capture forensic data: memory dump (LiME, fmem), disk image, audit logs, process list, network connections. (3) Determine the attack vector: check sshd logs, web server logs, package manager logs for unauthorized installs. (4) Check persistence mechanisms: cron jobs, systemd services, SSH authorized_keys, kernel modules. (5) Contain: rotate all credentials the server had access to. (6) Eradicate: rebuild the server from a known-good image, apply patches, review access controls. (7) Postmortem: what allowed the compromise? How do we prevent recurrence? At Enterprise, incident response is a well-rehearsed process with dedicated security engineers, runbooks, and automated containment workflows.'

References & Further Reading

  • Linux Security Modules — Kernel.org documentation on LSM framework
  • RHEL 9 Security Hardening — Red Hat's comprehensive security guide
  • CIS Benchmarks for Linux — Industry-standard security configuration guidelines

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes
Mistake: Checking /proc/meminfo inside containers for memory
Solution: Use cgroup v2 files: /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current, memory.events

Run `net.ipv4.conf.all.rp_filter` and examine the output. Note the structure, key fields, and patterns you observe. Try different flags to see how the output changes.

Quick Reference

| Command | Description | |---|---| | `net.ipv4.ip_forward` | Kernel Hardening: sysctl Security Settings | | `net.ipv6.conf.all.forwarding` | Kernel Hardening: sysctl Security Settings | | `net.ipv4.conf.all.accept_source_route` | Kernel Hardening: sysctl Security Settings | | `cat` | Seccomp: Secure Computing Mode | | `docker` | Seccomp: Secure Computing Mode | | `strace` | Seccomp: Secure Computing Mode | | `getenforce` | SELinux & AppArmor | | `sestatus` | SELinux & AppArmor | | `sudo` | SELinux & AppArmor | | `sudo` | Auditd: Linux Audit System |

Troubleshooting

Symptom: net.ipv4.ip_forward returns unexpected output
Cause: Incorrect arguments, wrong working directory, or stale cached data
Solution: Verify arguments with `man net.ipv4.ip_forward`; check current path with `pwd`; clear cache if applicable
Symptom: Configuration changes not taking effect
Cause: Service not reloaded or syntax error in config file
Solution: Validate config syntax with built-in test flag; reload service with `systemctl reload` or `restart`
Symptom: security operation slow or timing out
Cause: Resource exhaustion (disk I/O, memory pressure, or lock contention)
Solution: Check `iostat`, `free -m`, and `top`; look for stuck processes with `ps aux --sort=-%cpu`
Symptom: Permission issues accessing security resources
Cause: Incorrect file ownership or overly restrictive permissions
Solution: Inspect with `ls -la`; fix with `chown`/ `chmod`; check ACLs with `getfacl`

Quick Quiz

Q1: What is Kernel Hardening: sysctl Security Settings?

A: A standardized directory structure that defines where files and directories should be placed on Linux systems

Q2: What does `net.ipv4.ip_forward` do?

A: Lists files and directories in the current or specified path

Q3: Which command would you use to view disk usage of the security directory?

A: du -sh /path/to/directory

Q4: A user reports "Permission denied" when trying to access a security directory. What is the first command you should run?

A: ls -ld /path/to/directory

Q5: In a security scenario, your monitoring alerts show net.ipv4.ip_forward failing intermittently. What is the correct debugging workflow?

A: Check logs → verify configuration → test in staging → apply fix → monitor

FAANG Insight

  • FAANG Architect's Note: Security is everyone's job at FAANG, not just the security team's
  • FAANG, SELinux is 'enforcing by default' in AMIs, so applications are developed and tested with SELinux enabled from day one

Production Tips

  • Production Tip: Before enabling SELinux enforcement on an existing server, run in permissive mode for at least a week
  • production outage is an engineer enabling enforcement without first identifying all the necessary permissions — the application crashes because it can't access files that DAC would allow but SELinux denies

Best Practices

  • Use version control for all configuration (/etc, scripts, Dockerfiles)
  • Implement infrastructure as code; avoid manual server changes
  • Test changes in staging before production; have rollback plans

Enterprise Perspective

In enterprise environments, security hardening is managed through automation (Ansible, Terraform), monitored centrally (Prometheus, Datadog), and governed by compliance policies (CIS benchmarks, SOC2). Changes go through change advisory boards with rollback procedures documented.

Chapter 29

Linux at Enterprise scale

intermediateContainers & SRE · 19 min read · 14 min practice

Running Linux on 10,000+ servers is fundamentally different from running Linux on one server. At Enterprise, every Linux concern — kernel versions, package management, patching, monitoring, debugging — is automated, standardized, and scaled. This guide covers the architectural patterns, operational practices, and infrastructure systems that make Linux manageable at Enterprise scale.

faang-insightLinuxfleet managementGolden AMIcapacity planningSREscale

Prerequisites

It is recommended to read: linux kernel, linux networking, linux monitoring, linux docker

Learning Objectives

  • Understand the Golden AMI Pipeline
  • Understand kernel Management at Scale
  • Understand observability Infrastructure
  • Understand capacity Planning & Linux Resource Management

Real-Life Analogy: Scale as a Phase Change

What works at 10 servers fails at 10,000. Cattle vs pets. Automation replaces manual work. Observability replaces logs. Stateless services enable horizontal scaling. Consensus (Raft) replaces single leaders. The architecture must evolve with each order of magnitude.

The Golden AMI Pipeline

At Enterprise, servers are never installed manually. They're provisioned from a Golden AMI (Amazon Machine Image) — a pre-baked image with the approved OS version, kernel, packages, security agents, and configuration. Immutable infrastructure means: you never SSH into a running server to fix it — you build a new AMI and redeploy. The Golden AMI pipeline is one of the most critical pieces of infrastructure at any Enterprise company.
bash
# === Golden AMI pipeline stages ===
# 1. Base Image — Official Ubuntu/RHEL/Amazon Linux from vendor
# 2. Hardening — Apply CIS benchmarks, kernel params, security config
# 3. Agents — Install monitoring agent, logging agent, security agent
# 4. Packages — Pre-install common packages (Python, curl, jq, etc.)
# 5. Validation — Boot the AMI, run smoke tests, verify compliance
# 6. Signing — Sign the AMI for supply chain security
# 7. Promotion — Promote through environments: dev → staging → prod

# === Example: Packer template (simplified) ===
# packer build ubuntu-Enterprise.pkr.hcl
# source "amazon-ebs" "ubuntu" {
#   ami_name     = "Enterprise-ubuntu-22.04-{{timestamp}}"
#   instance_type = "c5.xlarge"
#   source_ami   = "ami-0c7217cdde317cfec"  # Canonical Ubuntu 22.04
#   ssh_username = "ubuntu"
# }
# build {
#   provisioner "shell" {
#     scripts = [
#       "scripts/harden.sh",
#       "scripts/install-agents.sh",
#       "scripts/cleanup.sh"
#     ]
#   }
# }

# === What's in a Golden AMI ===
# OS: Amazon Linux 2023, Ubuntu 22.04 LTS, RHEL 9
# Kernel: Distribution kernel with Enterprise-specific config
# Security: SELinux enforcing, auditd, OSSEC/Wazuh agent
# Monitoring: node_exporter, custom metric agent, Datadog/CloudWatch
# Logging: rsyslog forwarder, journald configured for central logging
# Networking: tuned sysctl params, ENA driver, Cilium prerequisites
# Packages: Python3, Go, curl, jq, htop, iotop, perf, tcpdump

# === AMI lifecycle ===
# - Weekly: Security patch AMI (CVE fixes)
# - Monthly: Feature AMI (new agents, config changes)
# - Quarterly: Major version AMI (Ubuntu 22.04 → 24.04 migration)
# Each AMI has a version tag: Enterprise-ubuntu-22.04-v2024-01-15

# === Immutable deployments ===
# Old model: SSH into server, apt update, apt upgrade
# Enterprise model: Build new AMI → Deploy autoscaling group → Terminate old instances
# Benefits: reproducible, testable, revertible (just deploy old AMI)
# Tools: Packer (build), Terraform/CloudFormation (deploy), Spinnaker/ArgoCD (pipeline)

Kernel Management at Scale

At Enterprise, kernel selection is strategic. Most Enterprises run custom or semi-custom kernels — either long-term stable with backported patches or an in-house fork. The kernel must support: eBPF (for Cilium, Falco), cgroups v2 (for container resource management), NVMe (for instance storage), and ENA (Elastic Network Adapter). Kernel upgrades are one of the riskiest operations and follow strict rollout procedures.
bash
# === Kernel version strategy ===
# Amazon Linux 2023: kernel 6.1 LTS (AWS-maintained)
# Ubuntu 22.04 LTS: kernel 5.15 HWE (Hardware Enablement)
# RHEL 9: kernel 5.14 (Red Hat-maintained)
# Google: custom kernel based on 5.x/6.x with Google patches
# Meta: custom kernel (kernel.org + patches for efficiency)

# === Kernel configuration for Enterprise workloads ===
# cat /boot/config-$(uname -r) | grep -E "(CGROUP|BPF|NAMESPACE|NVME)"
# CONFIG_CGROUPS=y             — cgroups v2
# CONFIG_BPF=y                 — eBPF support
# CONFIG_BPF_JIT=y             — BPF JIT compiler
# CONFIG_NAMESPACES=y          — Namespace support
# CONFIG_NVME_CORE=y           — NVMe SSD support
# CONFIG_XDP_SOCKETS=y         — XDP socket support
# CONFIG_IO_URING=y            — io_uring async I/O
# CONFIG_NUMA_BALANCING=y      — NUMA-aware scheduling

# === Patching strategy ===
# Critical CVEs: patched within 24-48 hours (emergency AMI build)
# Regular CVEs: patched weekly (scheduled AMI build)
# Kernel upgrades: quarterly (scheduled, tested, canaried)

# === Kernel rollout procedure ===
# 1. Build AMI with new kernel
# 2. Test in dev environment (automated)
# 3. Canary fleet: 1% of hosts, run for 24 hours
# 4. Watch metrics: kernel panics, OOM events, application latency
# 5. Expand to 10%, watch 24 hours
# 6. Expand to 50%, watch 12 hours
# 7. Full rollout
# Rollback: deploy previous AMI (instant)

# === Black screen of death: live patching ===
# Some Enterprises use live patching (Kpatch, Ksplice) for critical CVEs
# Patch the kernel without rebooting:
sudo kpatch load patch.ko
# kpatch list     # Show loaded patches
# Live patching can only fix function-level changes, not data structure changes

Observability Infrastructure

At Enterprise scale, you can't SSH into individual servers to check 'top'. Every metric is collected, aggregated, and alerted on automatically. The observability stack at Enterprise typically includes: a metrics system (Prometheus/Thanos), a logging system (Elasticsearch/Splunk), a tracing system (Jaeger), and a profiling system (continuous profiling with eBPF). Every new Linux feature or configuration change is validated by watching these metrics.
bash
# === What's monitored on every server ===
# CPU: utilization per core, context switches, load average, steal time
# Memory: total, used, available, page cache, swap, OOM events
# Disk: per-device IOPS, latency (await), queue depth, utilization, errors
# Network: bandwidth per interface, errors, drops, retransmits
# Kernel: slab usage, file handles, inode count, conntrack table
# Process: top 5 by CPU, top 5 by memory, zombie count

# === Sampling vs comprehensive ===
# At 10K+ servers, collecting EVERYTHING is expensive
# Strategy:
#   - Per-second: CPU, memory, disk, network (high-cardinality, low-retention)
#   - Per-minute: process list, connection stats (medium-cardinality)
#   - Per-hour: filesystem usage, inode count (low-cardinality, long-retention)
#   - On-event: OOM, kernel panic, process crash (immediate alert)

# === Standard dashboards ===
# Every server type has a standard dashboard:
# "EC2 c5.4xlarge General Purpose" dashboard:
#   - Row 1: CPU (per-core), Memory, Load, Context Switches
#   - Row 2: Disk (per-volume IOPS, latency), Network (throughput, errors)
#   - Row 3: Top processes (CPU, memory), Zombie count
#   - Row 4: Kernel (slab, file handles, conntrack)
#   - Row 5: Application-specific (varies by service)

# === Automated remediation ===
# Some issues are auto-remediated without human involvement:
# - Instance unhealthy → auto-scaling replaces it
# - Disk usage > 90% → log rotation + alert if still high
# - OOM kill → restart container, increase memory limit, alert
# - Kernel panic → instance replaced, crash dump uploaded for analysis

# === Pod/container-level monitoring ===
# For Kubernetes clusters: per-pod metrics (CPU, memory, network)
# Container-level: cgroup metrics, not host metrics
# Tools: cAdvisor, kube-state-metrics, metrics-server

Capacity Planning & Linux Resource Management

At Enterprise scale, capacity planning is a continuous process. You don't guess how many servers you need — you model it based on traffic forecasts, historical trends, and per-server benchmarks. Linux resource management feeds directly into capacity models: how much overhead does the OS consume? What's the performance profile of each instance type? How does kernel version affect throughput?
bash
# === Per-server resource budget ===
# For a c5.4xlarge (16 vCPU, 32 GB RAM):
# OS overhead: ~1 GB RAM, ~0.5 vCPU
# Monitoring agent: ~200 MB RAM, ~0.1 vCPU
# Security agent: ~300 MB RAM, ~0.1 vCPU
# Logging agent: ~200 MB RAM, ~0.05 vCPU
# Available for application: ~30 GB RAM, ~15 vCPU
# These numbers are measured, not guessed — from production profiling

# === Right-sizing methodology ===
# 1. Profile the application (perf, flamegraphs, memory profiling)
# 2. Run load tests at increasing instance sizes
# 3. Find the 'knee' in the cost/performance curve
# 4. Add 20% headroom for traffic spikes
# 5. Validate in production with auto-scaling

# === Instance family selection ===
# Compute-optimized (C series): CPU-bound apps, high perf/$, less memory
# Memory-optimized (R series): in-memory caches, databases
# General-purpose (M series): balanced, default choice
# GPU (P/G series): ML training, inference
# Storage-optimized (I series): high local disk I/O

# === Linux-specific capacity factors ===
# - Kernel version: newer kernels have better scheduler, eBPF, io_uring
# - Filesystem: XFS vs ext4 affects max throughput
# - NUMA: memory locality affects latency (use numactl)
# - Huge pages: reduce TLB misses, improve memory-intensive workloads
# - Network: ENA vs Intel vs eBPF affects max connections

# === Forecasting ===
# Model: traffic → requests per second → CPU time per request → total CPU → servers
# Daily: trend + seasonality → capacity needed
# Weekly: batch jobs, backups, data pipeline → peak usage
# Yearly: growth rate + new features → procurement plan

# === Headroom management ===
# Target: 60-70% utilization at peak (leaves room for traffic spikes)
# Auto-scaling trigger: 70% CPU for 5 minutes (scale up)
# Auto-scaling cooldown: 10 minutes (let metrics stabilize)
# Over-provisioning budget: 10-20% for safety

Interview Q&A

Q: 'How would you design a Linux server fleet management system for 50,000 servers?'

A: 'Key components: (1) Golden AMI pipeline — Packer to build, automated testing, versioned AMIs. (2) Configuration management — Ansible/Chef for any post-boot config, with idempotency and drift detection. (3) Fleet orchestration — AWS SSM or equivalent for running commands across the fleet without SSH. (4) Patch management — automated weekly patching via AMI rebuild, not in-place upgrades. (5) Monitoring — Prometheus + Thanos for metrics, centralized logging, and alerting with proper SLOs. (6) Instance lifecycle — auto-scaling groups manage instances, not humans. (7) Access control — bastion hosts, short-lived certificates, no direct SSH, all sessions logged. (8) Compliance — automated CIS scanning, auditd forwarding, compliance dashboards. The key principle: treat servers as cattle, not pets. If a server is broken, don't fix it — replace it.'

Q: 'Your team is migrating from Ubuntu 20.04 to Ubuntu 24.04 across 10,000 servers. What's your plan?'

A: 'Phase 1 — Assessment: catalog differences (kernel version, glibc, Python 3.8→3.12, OpenSSL), identify compatibility issues in our applications, test each service against the new OS. Phase 2 — AMI pipeline: build Golden AMI with Ubuntu 24.04, update all agents and configs. Phase 3 — Canary: deploy to 1% of non-production hosts, run integration tests for 1 week. Phase 4 — Staged rollout: 10% → 25% → 50% → 100% in non-prod. Phase 5 — Production canary: 1% of prod for 2 weeks with monitoring dashboards. Phase 6 — Full prod rollout: 10% per day, with automated rollback at any sign of regression. Phase 7 — Cleanup: decommission remaining 20.04 AMIs, update runbooks and documentation. This takes 3-4 months from start to finish.'

Q: 'How do you handle a kernel vulnerability (e.g., Dirty Pipe, Log4Shell-level but kernel) across the fleet?'

A: 'First, assess blast radius: which instances are affected (kernel version range, exposed attack surface). Second, contain: if possible, mitigate with sysctl/seccomp/iptables while the patch is being prepared. Third, fast-track patch: begin emergency AMI build with patched kernel, bypass normal staging (but still test on a small canary). Fourth, deploy: emergency automated rollout — canary for 1 hour, then full fleet rollout over 8-12 hours (vs typical 1-week rollout). Fifth, post-mortem: how did this CVE get through our vulnerability scanning? Why wasn't it caught earlier? At Enterprise, there is a 'Security Incident Response' team that coordinates kernel CVEs — the response team includes security engineers, Linux kernel engineers, and the fleet operations team.'

References & Further Reading

  • Packer Documentation — Tool for building Golden AMIs and machine images
  • Amazon Linux 2023 — AWS cloud-optimized Linux distribution
  • Google SRE Book: Eliminating Toil — The philosophy behind automating fleet operations

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime
Mistake: Not setting resource limits on containers
Solution: Always set `--cpus` and `--memory`; configure cgroup limits in Kubernetes
Mistake: Checking /proc/meminfo inside containers for memory
Solution: Use cgroup v2 files: /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current, memory.events

  • FAANG, I went months without SSH'ing into a server — because everything was automated

Production Tips

  • Production Tip: The most important metric in fleet management is time to recover — how long does it take to go from 'pager alert' to 'system healthy'? At Enterprise, the target is under 5 minutes for automated recovery and under 30 minutes for manual recovery

Chapter 30

Linux Interview Q&A

intermediateContainers & SRE · 16 min read · 12 min practice

Linux knowledge is tested throughout the Interview process — from initial phone screens to system design to on-site 'bar raiser' rounds. This guide covers the types of Linux questions you'll encounter across different roles (SRE, Infrastructure, Backend, Systems), with answers that demonstrate senior-level depth.

faang-insightLinuxinterviewpreparationsystem designdebugging

Learning Objectives

  • Understand role-Specific Linux Expectations
  • Understand system Design with Linux Constraints
  • Understand common Pitfalls & Anti-Patterns

Real-Life Analogy: Interviews as Technical Conversations

The interviewer is not testing memorization - they are evaluating how you think. "I don't know, but here is how I would find out" beats a wrong guess. Structure your answer: clarify, design, trade-offs, risks, monitoring. The best candidates teach the interviewer something.

Role-Specific Linux Expectations

Not all roles require the same depth of Linux knowledge. SRE/Infrastructure roles expect deep system-level knowledge. Backend/ML roles expect practical proficiency. Frontend/Product roles need basic familiarity. At Enterprise, the interview rubric clearly expects different levels — demonstrating appropriate depth for the role is key.
bash
# === Role expectations ===

# SRE / Infrastructure Engineer:
# - Kernel internals: syscalls, cgroups, namespaces, scheduler, memory mgmt
# - Performance: perf, flamegraphs, USE method, profiling
# - Networking: full stack (TCP, DNS, HTTP, iptables/nftables, BGP basics)
# - Storage: filesystems, LVM, NFS, RAID, distributed storage
# - Security: SELinux, seccomp, AppArmor, auditd, hardening
# - Containers: cgroups v2, OCI runtime, Docker internals, Kubernetes
# - Debugging: strace, gdb, crash dumps, core analysis
# Expected: Can debug any production issue and optimize system performance

# Backend / Software Engineer:
# - Practical: file operations, processes, signals, networking basics
# - Tools: SSH, git, grep, awk, sed, jq, curl
# - Scripting: bash scripting, error handling, job control
# - Containers: Dockerfiles, container basics
# Expected: Can develop and deploy services independently

# ML / Data Engineer:
# - Practical: package management, environment setup, Docker
# - Performance: GPU monitoring (nvidia-smi), memory profiling
# - Storage: filesystem basics, data pipeline I/O patterns
# Expected: Can set up and run ML workloads efficiently

# === How interviewers assess Linux depth ===
# "Tell me about a time you debugged a production issue"
# Junior answer: 'I restarted the server'
# Mid answer: 'I checked the logs and found an OOM error'
# Senior answer: 'I checked cgroup memory.events, found OOM kills,
#   analyzed the heap dump, identified a memory leak in the connection pool,
#   and added monitoring on cgroup memory pressure to prevent recurrence'

# === Sample question progression ===
# Basic: 'How do you check disk space?' -> df -h
# Intermediate: 'How do you find large files?' -> du -sh /* | sort -rh
# Advanced: 'A server has 50% disk free but can't write files. Why?' -> df -i (inodes)
# Expert: 'How does OOM killer select which process to kill?' -> oom_score algorithm

Common Interview Questions by Topic

These questions appear across Linux interviews. The key is not just knowing the right command, but explaining why it works and how you'd use it in a real scenario. For each question, interviewers look for: understanding of the underlying concept, practical experience, and systematic thinking.
bash
# === PROCESSES ===
# Q: What are the Linux process states?
# A: R (running/runnable), S (sleeping), D (uninterruptible sleep),
#    Z (zombie), T (stopped), t (tracing stop), X (dead)
#    D state is important: process waiting on I/O, can't be killed,
#    usually indicates NFS/disk issue

# Q: What is a zombie process? How do you clean them?
# A: Process that has exited but parent hasn't called wait().
#    Can't be killed (already dead). Kill the parent to let init reap them.
#    Check with: ps aux | grep 'Z'

# === MEMORY ===
# Q: Explain the output of 'free -h'. What's the difference between
#    'free', 'available', and 'buff/cache'?
# A: 'free' = completely unused RAM. 'buff/cache' = disk cache (reclaimable).
#    'available' = estimated memory for new apps (= free + reclaimable cache).
#    Linux uses free RAM for cache — this is normal, not 'wasted'.

# Q: What triggers OOM killer?
# A: When the kernel can't allocate memory and swap is full or disabled.
#    The kernel selects a victim based on oom_score (RSS + swap + page tables).
#    You can protect processes with oom_score_adj = -1000.

# === STORAGE ===
# Q: A disk is at 100% I/O utilization. How do you fix it?
# A: First, identify the source: iostat -x for device stats, iotop -oP for process.
#    Then: is it legitimate load (scale up) or a problem (missing index,
#    log flood, backup running during peak)?

# Q: What's the difference between ext4 and XFS?
# A: ext4: good general-purpose, reliable, widely compatible.
#    XFS: better for large files, parallel I/O, online defrag.
#    XFS is default on RHEL/Amazon Linux; ext4 on Ubuntu.

# === NETWORKING ===
# Q: How do you check if a port is listening?
# A: ss -tlnp | grep :PORT (modern, fast, detailed)
#    netstat -tlnp (legacy, may need net-tools)

# Q: A user says 'the network is slow'. How do you diagnose?
# A: Start with mtr to trace path + latency.
#    Check interface errors: ip -s link.
#    Check TCP retransmits: netstat -s | grep retrans.
#    Check bandwidth: sar -n DEV 1.
#    'Slow network' is usually an application issue, not the network.

# === CONTAINERS ===
# Q: How does Docker isolate processes?
# A: Namespaces (mount, PID, net, IPC, UTS, user) + cgroups (CPU, mem, I/O, PIDs) +
seccomp + LSM

# Q: A container is OOM-killed but 'free -h' inside shows free memory.
#    Why?
# A: /proc/meminfo shows host memory, not cgroup limit.
#    Check /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.current and memory.events.

# === DEBUGGING ===
# Q: A process is using 100% CPU. How do you find the hot path?
# A: perf top -p <PID> for live sampling,
#    perf record -g for call graphs,
#    generate a flamegraph.

# Q: What do you check in the first 60 seconds of an incident?
# A: uptime, dmesg | tail, vmstat 1, mpstat -P ALL 1, pidstat 1,
#    iostat -xz 1, free -m, sar -n DEV 1, sar -n TCP,ETCP 1, top

System Design with Linux Constraints

System design interviews often include Linux-specific constraints. At Enterprise, you're expected to design systems that work within Linux's capabilities and limitations. Understanding file descriptor limits, epoll vs select, memory-mapped files, and Linux-specific optimization is what separates a 'works on my laptop' design from a 'runs at Enterprise scale' design.
bash
# === File descriptor limits ===
# Default ulimit -n = 1024 (far too low for a web server)
# Production: ulimit -n 65536 or more
# 'Too many open files' = ulimit exhaustion
# Also check: system-wide limit (cat /proc/sys/fs/file-max)

# === Epoll vs Select/Poll ===
# epoll is O(1) for event notification — scales to millions of connections
# select/poll are O(n) — good for < 1000 connections
# This is why nginx/Node.js use epoll (via libuv/libevent)
# Check: cat /proc/sys/fs/epoll/max_user_watches

# === Memory-mapped files (mmap) ===
# mmap allows direct file access via memory operations
# Faster than read/write for random access
# Used by: databases (PostgreSQL shared buffers),
#          Kafka (page cache + mmap for log segments)

# === Linux-specific performance patterns ===
# - SO_REUSEPORT: multiple processes bind to same port (nginx, haproxy)
# - SO_BUSY_POLL: busy-poll for lower latency (Redis)
# - TCP_DEFER_ACCEPT: delay accept() until data arrives (web servers)
# - O_DIRECT: bypass page cache for databases (PostgreSQL, MySQL)
# - splice/sendfile: zero-copy data transfer

# === Design question with Linux constraints ===
# "Design a logging system that handles 100K writes/sec on a single server"
# Linux considerations:
# - File write buffering: use buffered I/O (write() is fast, fsync is slow)
# - Group commits: batch fsync every N ms (like PostgreSQL WAL)
# - Use append-only files (no random writes)
# - Consider filesystem: XFS for large sequential writes
# - Use a separate disk for logs (don't compete with data)
# - splice() for zero-copy from producer to socket
# - ulimit -n for file descriptors (log rotation creates new files)

Common Pitfalls & Anti-Patterns

Knowing what NOT to do is as important as knowing what to do. At Enterprise, certain patterns are banned in production — they've caused enough outages that they're now in the 'never do this' category.
bash
# === NEVER DO THESE IN PRODUCTION ===

# 1. Don't disable swap entirely
# swap provides a safety buffer against OOM
# Set vm.swappiness=10 (swap only when necessary) instead of 0
# But also: on modern systems with cgroups, swap may be disabled safely

# 2. Don't drop caches in production
# echo 3 > /proc/sys/vm/drop_caches destroys page cache
# This causes massive I/O as everything must be re-read from disk
# Only do this in controlled benchmarks

# 3. Don't use kill -9 as the first resort
# Always SIGTERM first (graceful shutdown)
# Use SIGKILL only when SIGTERM is ignored

# 4. Don't disable SELinux/AppArmor
# 'I disabled it to make it work' = losing defense in depth
# Instead: put it in permissive mode, collect denials, fix them, enable

# 5. Don't chmod 777
# If permissions don't work, fix them properly
# 777 means anyone can modify any file — including attackers

# 6. Don't run containers/applications as root
# If a root process is compromised, the attacker has full system access
# Always create a service account or use USER in Dockerfile

# 7. Don't rely on /proc/meminfo for container memory
# Use cgroup memory metrics (memory.current, memory.events)
# /proc/meminfo shows host memory, not container limits

# 8. Don't skip log rotation
# 'I'll clean it up later' = pager at 3 AM when disk is full
# Configure logrotate BEFORE deploying

# 9. Don't disable seccomp 'for performance'
# The performance cost of seccomp is negligible
# The security cost of disabling it is significant

# 10. Don't make changes without a rollback plan
# Before any production change: 'How do I undo this?'
# If you can't answer, don't make the change

References & Further Reading

  • Systems Performance: Enterprise and the Cloud — Brendan Gregg, the definitive Linux performance reference
  • UNIX and Linux System Administration Handbook — Comprehensive admin reference
  • Linux Systems Interview Questions — Community-curated question bank

Common Mistakes

Mistake: Using chmod 777 to fix permission issues
Solution: Use `chmod 750` or ACLs for specific users; find the minimal required permissions
Mistake: Using kill -9 as first resort
Solution: Send SIGTERM first (kill PID); wait for graceful shutdown; only use SIGKILL if process ignores SIGTERM
Mistake: Ignoring log rotation until disk fills up
Solution: Configure logrotate before deploying; monitor /var/log usage
Mistake: Disabling SELinux/AppArmor instead of fixing policies
Solution: Use `audit2allow` to generate policies from denials; keep MAC enabled
Mistake: Running containers as root
Solution: Add `USER nonroot` in Dockerfile; use `--user` flag at runtime

Quick Reference

| Command | Description | |---|---| | `ls` | List directory contents | | `cat` | Display file contents | | `grep` | Search text patterns |

Troubleshooting

Symptom: command returns unexpected output
Cause: Incorrect arguments, wrong working directory, or stale cached data
Solution: Verify arguments with `man command`; check current path with `pwd`; clear cache if applicable
Symptom: Configuration changes not taking effect
Cause: Service not reloaded or syntax error in config file
Solution: Validate config syntax with built-in test flag; reload service with `systemctl reload` or `restart`
Symptom: interview qs operation slow or timing out
Cause: Resource exhaustion (disk I/O, memory pressure, or lock contention)
Solution: Check `iostat`, `free -m`, and `top`; look for stuck processes with `ps aux --sort=-%cpu`
Symptom: Permission issues accessing interview qs resources
Cause: Incorrect file ownership or overly restrictive permissions
Solution: Inspect with `ls -la`; fix with `chown`/ `chmod`; check ACLs with `getfacl`

Quick Quiz

Q1: What is Role-Specific Linux Expectations?

A: A standardized directory structure that defines where files and directories should be placed on Linux systems

Q2: How do you list the contents of a interview qs related directory?

A: Lists files and directories in the current or specified path

Q3: Which command would you use to view disk usage of the interview qs directory?

A: du -sh /path/to/directory

Q4: A user reports "Permission denied" when trying to access a interview qs directory. What is the first command you should run?

A: ls -ld /path/to/directory

Q5: In a interview qs scenario, your monitoring alerts show ls failing intermittently. What is the correct debugging workflow?

A: Check logs → verify configuration → test in staging → apply fix → monitor

FAANG Insight

  • FAANG Architect's Note: Linux interview questions at FAANG test more than memorization — they test your mental model of how the system works

Production Tips

  • production issues? The best answer to 'How do you debug high CPU?' is not 'I use perf' — it's a story about a time you used perf to find and fix a real issue

Best Practices

  • Use version control for all configuration (/etc, scripts, Dockerfiles)
  • Implement infrastructure as code; avoid manual server changes
  • Test changes in staging before production; have rollback plans

Enterprise Perspective

In enterprise environments, Linux interview q&a is managed through automation (Ansible, Terraform), monitored centrally (Prometheus, Datadog), and governed by compliance policies (CIS benchmarks, SOC2). Changes go through change advisory boards with rollback procedures documented.

Appendix B: Filesystem Hierarchy Standard

Essential Directories

/bin — Essential command binaries (boot-critical)
/sbin — System administration binaries
/etc — Host-specific configuration
/var — Variable data (logs, databases, spool)
/tmp — Temporary files (cleared on boot)
/usr — Secondary hierarchy (read-only user data)
/opt — Add-on application packages
/proc — Virtual filesystem: process & kernel info
/sys — Virtual filesystem: kernel objects & devices
/dev — Device files
/run — Runtime variable data (tmpfs)
/home — User home directories
/root — Root user home

Configuration Files

/etc/passwd — User accounts
/etc/shadow — Password hashes
/etc/group — Group definitions
/etc/sudoers — Sudo privileges
/etc/fstab — Mount table
/etc/hosts — Hostname resolution
/etc/resolv.conf — DNS config
/etc/ssh/sshd_config — SSH server config

Appendix C: Permission & ACL Reference

File Permissions

r (4) — Read
w (2) — Write
x (1) — Execute
- (0) — No permission

Owner (u) | Group (g) | Others (o)

Common: 755 (rwxr-xr-x), 644 (rw-r--r--), 700 (rwx------), 600 (rw-------)

Special Bits

SUID (4) — Run as file owner (chmod u+s)
SGID (2) — Run as group / inherit group (chmod g+s)
Sticky (1) — Only owner can delete (chmod +t, /tmp)

ACL Commands

getfacl — Show ACL entries
setfacl — Set ACL entries (setfacl -m u:user:rwx file, setfacl -m g:group:rx file)

Default ACL: setfacl -d -m u:user:rwx dir/

Umask

umask 022 — Files: 644, Dirs: 755 (default)
umask 077 — Files: 600, Dirs: 700 (secure)
umask 002 — Files: 664, Dirs: 775 (collaborative)

Appendix D: Signal Table

Common Signals

SignalNumberActionDescription
SIGHUP1TermHangup (reload config)
SIGINT2TermInterrupt (Ctrl+C)
SIGQUIT3CoreQuit (Ctrl+\)
SIGKILL9TermKill (cannot be caught/blocked)
SIGTERM15TermTerminate (graceful shutdown)
SIGSTOP19StopStop process (cannot be caught)
SIGTSTP20StopTerminal stop (Ctrl+Z)
SIGCONT18ContContinue if stopped
SIGUSR110TermUser-defined 1
SIGUSR212TermUser-defined 2
SIGCHLD17IgnChild stopped or terminated
SIGPIPE13TermBroken pipe (write to closed pipe)

Appendix E: Systemd Quick Reference

Service Management

systemctl start service — Start
systemctl stop service — Stop
systemctl restart service — Restart
systemctl reload service — Reload config
systemctl status service — Show status
systemctl enable service — Start on boot
systemctl disable service — Disable on boot

Journal

journalctl — View all logs
journalctl -u service — Filter by unit
journalctl -f — Follow (tail)
journalctl -x — Add explanatory text
journalctl -n 50 — Last 50 lines
journalctl -p err — Filter by priority

Unit Types

.service — System services
.timer — Timed events (cron replacement)
.socket — Socket activation
.path — Path activation
.mount — Mount points
.target — Group of units (runlevel equivalent)
.slice — Resource management (cgroups)

Appendix F: Networking Commands

Interface & Address

ip addr — Show addresses
ip link — Show interfaces
ip route — Show routing table
ss -tlnp — Listening TCP ports with process
ss -uanp — UDP sockets with process
ip neigh — ARP cache

Connectivity

ping -c 5 host — ICMP test
mtr host — Path + latency
traceroute host — Route path
curl -v url — HTTP debug
nc -vz host port — TCP port check
dig domain — DNS lookup
nslookup domain — Legacy DNS

Packet inspection

tcpdump -i eth0 -nn — Capture packets
tcpdump port 80 — Filter by port
tcpdump -w capture.pcap — Save to file

Firewall

iptables -L -n -v — List rules
iptables -A INPUT -p tcp --dport 22 -j ACCEPT — Allow SSH
nft list ruleset — nftables rules
ufw status verbose — Uncomplicated firewall
